About the Author







Prologue



Jess sprinted unshod through the dark forest, the dog keeping pace at her side.

She didn’t know where she was headed, just away from that monster. From all that gore, all that death. That humorless smile.

Though I walk through the valley of the shadow of death, I will fear no evil, for thou art with me, she repeated in her head. She wanted to say it aloud, but her burning lungs refused.

After a few minutes, she stopped to catch her breath, bracing one hand against a tree as she gasped for air. The dog ran several yards ahead, collar jingling, then came to a hesitant stop and turned back toward her. Normally he would’ve barked—he never did know when to shut the hell up—but he’d been oddly silent this whole time.

A twig broke somewhere behind them.

Jess froze, holding her breath. Even the dog’s collar went still.

Was it real, or only in her imagination? After several seconds, she forced herself to look. She half expected to see those glowing eyes, but there was nothing. Just moonlit pines.

From somewhere in the distance, sirens wailed. Should she head to the highway and wave them down? Or would they just arrest her?

No, it was best to avoid the police. She knew her instructions. She’d been trained. There were more teams around. All she had to do was find one of them and she’d be safe.

She took a deep breath to calm herself and looked down at the dog. “Come on, Jack. Let’s get out of here.”







Part I





A Number as a Name







Chapter 1



The blood had been ripped out of their bodies. Asim considered himself a hard man to rattle, but he’d never seen anything like this before.

Walking through the crime scene, he couldn’t help but cover his nose with his forearm. He had declined a mask from one of the local police officers—the corpses were far too fresh to smell yet—but now he wished he’d taken it. Just the sight of this mess made him want something over his face.

As he surveyed the wall and ground, both stained red with dried blood that was now being reanimated by the drizzling rain, his mind ran through scenarios for how this could’ve happened. So far, he’d come up with little that made sense.

It was a highway rest stop deep in the New Jersey Pine Barrens. At around midnight, the manager had reported gunshots and an explosion to local police, who responded within minutes. The unusual gore and military context of what they found led them to escalate the issue, resulting in Asim coming out here with a unit early in the morning. At his rank he wasn’t generally meant to go into the field, but he had to see this one for himself.

The incident had occurred at the back of the rest stop, behind a convenience store. Three of the corpses—two Caucasian males in their forties and one Hispanic female in her twenties—were still intact, but had suffered obscene blood loss. They’d been operatives of some sort, judging by their expensive, slim nanocomposite armored vests, their combat boots, and the military-grade rifles strewn about. The blood appeared to have burst out at high velocity through the skin on their backs, piercing their body armor like a water jet cutter and splattering the area. There were no entry wounds.

A fourth corpse, a giant of a man in thick full-body armor, had been thrown with such force against the concrete wall of the store that the back of his skull had shattered, and the wall cracked straight through to the other side. Not even a high-speed car crash would’ve penetrated that deep. More curiously, the bloody crater from the impact was about nine feet off the ground, with a red streak extending down to where his broken body now lay.

Asim crouched and examined the giant man’s face, careful not to let his trench coat dip into the muck. Caucasian, about seven feet tall, likely in his late twenties or early thirties, with a bullet hole piercing the center of his forehead. It was a 9mm round from the look of it, but forensics would confirm and probably already had. The dead face stared vacantly ahead, its eyes an unnaturally vivid shade of pale green with vertical slits for pupils. He’d seen eye mods from aesthetic clinics before, but nothing this extensive.

He looked back at the other faces. The female corpse had the same weird vertical-pupil green eyes as the giant man, but the other two dead men’s eyes looked normal.

The fifth and final corpse had come to a different fate. With a broken neck and blunt-force trauma to the temple, the young man looked more like a typical murder victim, if there was such a thing. At least his blood was still where it belonged. He had a handsome face, with dark skin and high cheekbones. Average height, no armor, early twenties at most, and based on his thin build and casual attire, he likely wasn’t any sort of operative. He wore sneakers rather than combat boots, and he rested on the ground almost peacefully, his eyes the only ones closed. Deep marks on his wrists indicated that he’d been bound not long ago.

A hundred yards away from the corpses, a van lay upside down on the edge of the parking lot near the tree line, the pavement broken beneath it. The vehicle was crushed, with debris scattered dozens of feet in all directions. Its battery had burst into flames, and a forensics unit was still combing through the charred remains.

“Son of a bitch,” Asim whispered, running a hand through his rain-dampened hair.

After taking another moment to scan the area, he sighed and set out to the front of the store. Dozens of local and state police officers and firefighters swarmed the place, along with their various hovering service drones, while a mix of police and news helicopters thrummed above in the overcast sky. Ava Zhou, one of his most reliable special agents, stood interviewing the store’s night-duty manager.

Asim approached the police captain, a balding man in his fifties he’d already checked in with earlier. “Any sightings yet?”

“Not yet, Colonel,” the captain said. “But this manhunt’s our top priority. We have our entire precinct and the state troopers involved. Three drone fleets are out there scanning, and we’ve got some promising signs from the K-9 unit, so we’ll see where that leads. Anything you can tell us yet?”

Asim shook his head. “Afraid not. But we’ll keep in touch. I appreciate your team’s professionalism.” The investigation had already been passed to military jurisdiction, but the state and local police were the ones performing the manhunt for whoever had done this.

The captain swallowed, paused, then spoke again. “I thought they’d send the FBI when we called this in. Not the, uh, Army.”

Asim nodded. “We may pull in the Bureau, but for now the DoD prefers to clean up what may be one of its own messes.” Despite his civilian clothing, Asim was the deputy commander of Army Counterintelligence, or ACI for short, but many people weren’t familiar with what that even meant. His team’s job was to detect and neutralize military-related national security threats, including ones from insiders.

Catching Ava’s eye, Asim signaled her over and stepped away from the captain. Half a minute later, after wrapping up her conversation, she strode over with her tablet in hand. Wearing a well-tailored dark suit jacket over a pastel violet blouse, and with her hair pulled back into a prim bun, Ava stood out from most people here. Unsurprisingly, she had a smile on her face.

No matter the details of a case over the past few years, whether basic or gory—and this was by far the goriest—Ava seemed to take it in stride. A brilliant young civilian investigator in Asim’s counterintelligence group, she was methodical and efficient.

“You up for an early lunch, sir?” she asked. “I could go for spaghetti.”

She also cracked too many jokes, in his view. What a weird combination. He still hadn’t quite figured her out.

“Alright, give me the status,” Asim said. Not acknowledging her quips never seemed to stop them from coming, though. He led her to where they could see the corpses, but still comfortably away from the gore. Just damp asphalt around them, with the rain unlocking its oily smell.

“Yes, sir. Skipping the obvious,” she began. “The firearms are unregistered, but they’re all types issued by the Department of Defense. No usable fingerprints thanks to the van fire, though we’ve found two sets of footprints in the blood. One was a set of boot prints, matching the type that’s on all the operatives’ bodies but none of their precise sizes. The second was a set of bare footprints, potentially from a teenager or a woman based on their size. That makes two participants unaccounted for.”

“Likely the perp and a missing captive,” Asim said. “Sounds like the perp’s an operative, too. Gone rogue, maybe. Any clue how the hell that much blood sprayed out?”

She shook her head. “Not yet, sir. We’ll have to wait for the coroner to examine them. It seems to have ripped straight out of their veins. But whatever insanity caused this, it didn’t happen to the biggest one.” She pointed. “Forensics thinks the impact against the wall killed him. His armored back hit first, then his head snapped back. The 9mm gunshot wound to his forehead didn’t bleed much, suggesting it occurred postmortem. Must’ve been a popular guy.”

“Any other bullets found?” Asim asked.

“Two 9mm bullets and three casings were found on the ground nearby, and one more bullet and casing pair were recovered in the van wreckage, but the only gunshot wound found so far was in the big guy’s forehead. All the operatives had 9mm pistols holstered on their persons except for him—his holster’s empty. None of them were missing any rounds, sir. Neither were the rifles. Forensics found three 9mm pistols in the van, damaged to varying degrees, and one of them was missing a single round. So at least three of the four shots seem to be from the missing perp’s pistol, which is presumably still with them, and one likely came from that pistol in the van.”

“What’s our system say about the victims’ identities?”

“We ran their prints and DNA against both standard and restricted databases, and none of the green-eyed ones came up with a match in any system, sir. But the two males with normal eyes are both ex-military. Clean records. I’m sending you their files right . . . now.” She tapped her tablet.

Asim’s phone buzzed in his pocket. “What about him?” he asked, gesturing to the fifth corpse, the youngest one, as he pulled out his phone to glance at their files. “Do we have an ID for him?”

“Not yet, but judging by the restraint marks, he was a captive,” Ava said. “We found two pairs of ripped-apart plasticuffs on the ground that were likely used on him and the missing person without shoes. But here’s the thing, sir—his eyes are that strange type too.”

She went over to his corpse, deftly slipping a glove on along the way, and lifted his left eyelid. The iris was a striking green, and with a vertical pupil.

“Huh.” Asim scratched his head. He hadn’t expected that at all. It was embarrassing that he hadn’t even bothered to check the young man’s eyes, since unlike the rest, they were closed. “So, three of the five bodies have these weird eyes, rather than just two. I thought this civilian one would be our best lead, but he’s a fucking ghost too, somehow?”

“Seems that way, sir,” Ava said. “No additional bodies were found in the burnt van. But initial observations show that the van, well, fell from a great height onto its roof and skidded a few dozen feet, breaking apart in the process.”

“Fell? From where?” He looked up and gestured with his hand toward the open sky for emphasis. Chilly raindrops tapped his palm.

“It was launched somehow, sir. Based on the direction of the pavement damage and debris pattern, its trajectory likely originated from roughly where the bodies are. There’s a witness who saw the van flying in an arc-like motion.”

Asim raised his eyebrows.

Ava shrugged with exaggerated flair, then continued. “The action happened shortly before midnight. A charging station attendant saw the van arrive and charge its battery, but didn’t keep track of it after that. Our team is reviewing the store’s camera footage, which does capture the van leaving the station and heading to the area behind the store. The license plate is registered to a shell company that we’re looking into. Apparently, the store’s rear camera has been offline for over six months. Nobody bothered to have it fixed, so there’s nothing capturing the actual event. The—”

He sighed. “Of course.”

Ava eyed him, looked back down at her tablet, and spoke again. “The night manager of the convenience store heard gunshots and a slam that rattled the whole building, so he called the police. The building’s wall, concrete about a foot thick, has cracks straight to the other side from the big guy’s impact, and the wall’s interior steel reinforcement bars are bent. While the manager was on the phone with police, an explosion sounded in the distance, which was probably the van.” She gestured with her chin. “By the time he got the nerve to come out and look, there was nobody around. Just this bloody mess and the flaming van wreckage.”

“So, the witness didn’t see any of it either?”

“That wasn’t the only witness, sir,” Ava said, smiling and holding up her finger. “At the time of the incident, two eighteen-wheelers were parked over there. Both drivers awoke to the gunshots.” She pointed over to a long and empty parking lot, meant for trucks. Beyond that, the dense pine forest began.

“One piece of good news, at least.”

She nodded. “It was dark and far away for both of them, and one had a bad angle relative to the convenience store, but the other saw some of the event, sir. The police got his full report before we arrived, and they released him after questioning. He saw the van parked behind the convenience store, lights on, with its back-facing doors open. Bodies were already on the ground. He saw two figures moving around the vehicle in the dark. Then he ducked down in his cabin to hide. He claims someone near the van threw it, sir.”

“Like, he saw someone pick it up and . . .” Asim began. The sentence felt too stupid to finish.

She grinned. “The police pushed him about that, sir. Like I said, it was dark and far away. He clarified that someone stood next to it, and then it went flying.”

“And let me guess. He can’t identify the people he saw?”

Ava shook her head and shrugged. Of course not.

“Find out which security firm these two traceable ex-military ones worked for,” he said. “Then we’ll contact them and push for details and their client list.”

He turned and regarded the peaceful fifth corpse. Civilian-looking, younger, and yet still with those inhuman eyes. Most of the scene investigators were focused on the biggest corpse wearing the strange thick armor, but this fifth body was the one that gave Asim a chill. He lingered on the image for a moment, watching drops of rain tap the young man’s lifeless face.

“I want the coroner to examine their eyes as soon as possible. In fact, send one of ours in. Johnson, preferably. Highest priority. Get that process in motion now. We might be able to trace the clinic where they had their eyes modded and get IDs from there. With a potential live captive at risk, we need to move fast.”

Ava nodded. Her tablet was automatically transcribing the conversation, even though she’d likely remember it all anyway. “I can also have forensics check for tire tracks to see if they can isolate recent ones, and which way they may have gone. That wrecked van might not be the only one, sir.”

“Yes, do that,” he said. “But there will be tire tracks all over the place. I’ll authorize as much data analysis as you or Flanagan need for this. Check all the highway cameras, in both directions and all exits, within a hundred miles, going forty-eight hours back. Have the bots look for black vans of similar models to that one. I’ll sign for it.”

Ava’s eyes went wide, and she nodded again. That was a bigger dataset than they’d checked in a long time. She began setting up the analysis request on her tablet.

Truth be told, even though they made his agents’ jobs easier, Asim hated the ubiquity of the cameras these days. They were everywhere in towns and cities, and along the major transportation corridors. Police officers and feds could get access to a few camera recordings from pretty much anywhere if they needed them, but collecting more than that required a court order. After widespread protests four years ago in response to a major hack, the connections between public camera feeds and AI datacenters had been restricted to low bandwidth limits unless authorized on a case-by-case basis.

What the public didn’t know was that the feds had a backdoor. Because of course they did. A secret court dealing with national security matters had ruled to allow those with rare clearance levels, like Asim, to unilaterally bypass bandwidth caps and directly tap the surveillance systems into a military datacenter. He merely had to sign for it with his unique digital key.

Asim figured an override of nearly any scale was worth it to find out what the hell had happened here. More people could be in danger already, including the unaccounted-for captive who’d left the bare footprints.

The main thing that unnerved him—other than the whole ripped-out blood thing and the thrown van, as if they weren’t enough—was the seeming black-ops nature of the victims in a place like this. At his level he had access to most of the special off-record databases, and yet they had nothing on the green-eyed ones. Something was amiss.

“It’s ready for you to sign, sir.” Ava held up her tablet. Asim pulled out his phone, set up the visual code with his signing key, and let her scan it with her tablet camera. “Okay, it’s sent to headquarters. I’ll let you know when the findings come back, sir.”

“How many times have I said that you don’t have to call me ‘sir’?” He was a military officer, but Ava served his division as one of his civilian agents and thus had no such obligation. For some reason, hearing it so frequently in the field was more grating than back in the office.

“Of course. Still feels right, though.” She smiled. She’d previously told him that it wasn’t about his military rank and was instead about his professional seniority, but he kept assuming it’d drop off as she worked with him longer. It never did. Too casual in some ways, too formal in others, and thus somehow capable of annoying him at both ends.

“Fine,” he said. “Don’t forget to have the local coroners sign NDAs for their findings, and as I mentioned, we’ll probably have to send Johnson to assist anyway, so make sure to get on top of that right away. The coroner’s findings go to us first, and then we’ll give a redacted version to the local police.”

She nodded. “I already pinged Dr. Johnson. Anything else, sir?”

“Yes. Tell the police that we ran the green-eyed ones through our restricted system and identified them, but that we can’t share the names. Make it sound routine. It’s better they think we know the answers and view it as normal than for them to wonder and spread rumors.”

She stared down at her tablet for a moment with a frown, then nodded again. “Of course, sir.”



Hours later, the police manhunt hadn’t yet located the perp or the missing captive. Highway cameras, however, had caught the same black van several times in the hours prior to the night’s events. It drove east to west, slipped out of sight into some Pine Barrens back roads, then doubled back four hours later, heading east before pulling into the rest stop. The system also spotted two other identical vans with different license plate numbers.

In response, Asim had authorized a second highway camera sweep focused on the southeast region, where the van had originally appeared. Every hour mattered for the potential captive.

The coroner wouldn’t have a preliminary report on the corpses’ eyes until morning. So, after a long day, Asim and his field team boarded their helicopter and flew back to Maryland near Washington, DC—and from there, home.

At ACI headquarters, Asim’s analysts traced the license plate of the burnt van and the employer of the two deceased ex-military operatives with normal eyes. Hidden through layers of shell companies, it all led to a firm called Eidolon Solutions. Asim knew most of the major military security contractors, but not this one. And just to add to his growing headache, because why not, they refused to provide any information on their clients, citing top-secret clearances. ACI’s legal team would have to drag them through a military court to force it out of them, which would waste days.

By the time he walked through his front door, Asim was exhausted. Not physically, and perhaps not even mentally—maybe spiritually? Was that the right word for someone who wasn’t religious? Stripping off his boots, coat, sidearm, and plain black sweater, he felt his appreciation of humanity drop yet another notch lower, and that made him tired. He hadn’t seen gore like this since his service abroad.

After taking a shower, where he listened to vintage rock to blot out his thoughts, Asim pulled out a whiskey glass and lingered on it. It’d been three dry days in a row this time; maybe he owed himself some after a day like today? But then, with a sigh, he put it away and headed straight for his VR room instead. He was trying to be a better man. Usually failing, but trying.

Most of his apartment was unremarkable, except for his VR setup. That was expensive, top-shelf gear. With a mix of technologies, including wireless signals to the brain from the headset as well as a silk-thin haptic feedback suit that covered most of his skin, it let him experience all five senses of a virtual environment. For those on the true cutting edge, surgically implanted electrodes offered even deeper immersion, but Asim wasn’t willing to go that far yet.

He’d heard repeatedly that society’s obsession with VR had become unhealthy in recent years as the tech increasingly allowed people to feel the virtual environment rather than just see and hear it. The experience seemed genuine to the point of blurring the line with reality if you weren’t actively trying to spot the differences.

It was “society’s modern addiction” as Ava liked to say, and to her point, a growing number of people barely left the virtual world other than to deal with occasional necessities. She was one of the few people he knew who didn’t like to use VR and only rarely did so.

Yet Asim figured that if he replaced one addiction with another, then that wasn’t so bad. Or at least, a man should spread his addictions around a bit, right? For damage control? Even just thinking about it now, he pictured that whiskey glass and felt tempted again. But he pushed away the thought, suited himself up, strapped himself into his motion chair, and went in.

Seconds later, reclining under an umbrella, he opened his eyes to a beautiful beach. The breeze caressed his skin and hair, the sun warmed his face, the scent of saltwater filled his nose, and the sound of gentle waves sang to him. It wasn’t 100 percent accurate to real life, but it was close enough, and it made for a nice contrast to the overcast sky and chilly drizzle that’d lasted all day.

He reached up and rubbed his face, running his hands over his cheeks and short beard, then down his neck. Some of the headset’s brain signals were directly soothing his anxiety and promoting a mild sense of well-being, which was a nice touch. Those signals indirectly magnified the physical sensations and made them subjectively feel more pleasant, which helped conceal the minor imperfections of the experience.

After a while, Asim stood up and looked around. There were a few genuine people and families sprinkled in the distance enjoying themselves, enough to make the environment feel social and populated without being crowded. This was a virtual beach, one of countless public places in the edge realms for people to enjoy—Beautiful Beach, Turquoise Water, Low Population Density, Room 7.

He looked over at the water, which was his favorite part, and the reason he always picked this specific spot. It was a clear blue, almost unnaturally so, like the southern coasts of the Mediterranean, with scattered rays from the virtual sun lending it a crystalline aspect. He waded into it, feeling its cool touch over his skin, and swam out maybe thirty yards. Then he . . . did nothing. He let himself float on his back, eyes closed, and just be.

Then he got a fucking text notification, jarring him out of his relaxation. He had set notifications to “essentials only,” so it was likely Ava or one of his bosses. Almost certainly not his ex-wife or daughter, though they were on that list too in case they’d ever call.

He opened his eyes, sighed, and stared at the flawless blue sky for a moment.

“Show me,” he said aloud. The sky transformed into his phone background, along with a message from Ava. He hesitated, thinking maybe this could wait until tomorrow; maybe he could pretend to already be asleep by now. But he thought better of it, and reached out with his hand to swipe the message open.

There was a second attack, sir, it read. There’s video this time.







Chapter 2



Asim had only managed two hours of sleep last night, sandwiched between bouts of remote work in his apartment. That gave him license to be an ass today.

“Colonel Rahal, can you—” some new person started.

“No, not yet,” Asim said, walking to his office within ACI headquarters at Fort Meade, Maryland. If something became important enough, Ava would filter it up to him.

Filtering had been a challenge ever since he shifted from international fieldwork to domestic supervisory work. Too little, and everyone could tell you everything, leaving you mired in tactics instead of strategy. Too much, and you wouldn’t have your finger on the pulse of things properly, putting you out of touch.

Ava turned out to be the missing piece he’d needed. Just the right amount of filtering. Thanks to her, he ran a two-tier system for the people who reported to him. Ava kept her finger on the pulse. That was her job. The pulse listener. The details woman. She had the youthful energy for that type of shit and even helped filter some things from people more senior than her, since everyone knew that was what Asim wanted.

Although she was technically only a junior special agent, Asim had given her an extra assignment to organize and report intel on ACI’s active cases to him on a recurring basis. It gave her more access than she’d otherwise have. In exchange, he got more headspace for strategy, and fewer distractions.

After he passed through the maze of window-lined halls that looked out at yet more gray clouds, he sat at his desk and pulled up the video of the second incident on his phone. By now it must’ve been the tenth time he’d watched it. He should have been managing the division’s broader workload instead of fixating on a single case, but he couldn’t help himself with this one.

The footage was from Brooklyn, late the previous night, twenty-two hours after the rest stop incident. It was a rough part of town, where cameras were torn down or spray-painted faster than the city could replace them, so the one that caught the incident was far away. The team had to zoom in on the footage, which made the resolution poor.

On the curb stood a tall prostitute with long, voluminous hair and high heels. What looked like her pimp and two other men skulked about behind her in an alley entrance. A figure wearing a hooded sweatshirt wandered down the sidewalk, with boots that were a plausible match to those of the operatives from the rest stop. Asim’s team had estimated the figure’s height to be somewhere between five-foot-seven and five-foot-nine; the poor video quality limited the precision.

The prostitute gestured to the hooded figure, who ignored her and kept walking. Then the setup revealed itself—this was an angler scheme. Some crews would get a prostitute, either real or fake, to lure a mark into an alley to pay the “pimp” for her services, then mug them. That was what the men in the alley seemed prepared to do. But when the hooded figure failed to stop, the men instead came out and surrounded them, with two of the three brandishing weapons: a blade and a pistol.

The figure paused, eerily still, then calmly stepped into the alley, disappearing off screen with them. The prostitute stayed on the curb, keeping watch.

Less than a minute later, she glanced toward the alley, froze, and slowly raised her hands, trembling. Several more seconds passed. The hooded figure emerged, pointing a pistol at her and carrying a bag. After a brief dialogue, the prostitute nodded and walked down the sidewalk with the figure following behind, lowering the pistol to their side, muzzle toward the ground, glancing back to survey the area.

Asim leaned forward and squinted at the screen, as though it would help. The hooded figure’s face wasn’t visible, but the eyes shone more than they should have, like a cat’s in the night. The camera’s low-light mode didn’t capture color, so it only rendered two bright pixels amid the bad resolution of a shadowed face. Asim was willing to bet any amount of money they were green.

That alone was strange enough, but what police had found in the alley was stranger still. None of the men had been shot. All three had been strangled to death, with enough force to crush their throats and fracture several vertebrae—and with no other injuries. “Dead within seconds” was the phrase that stood out on the paramedics’ initial report.

The hooded figure was almost certainly the perp from the rest stop, and now with this prostitute taken at gunpoint, there were two potential captives taken less than a day apart. Asim’s nagging concern was for the fate of the first one, since the perp had been alone at the start of this encounter.

He sighed. The first captive was likely already dead, then. That was how these things usually went. The odds weren’t good for this second one either.

After shutting off the video, he sat for several minutes, running through scenarios in his mind.

“We have Dr. Johnson’s initial report on the victims’ eyes,” Ava said from his open office doorway, startling him. When had she shown up? Had he dozed off? “I just sent it to you, sir. Dr. Johnson’s still in Jersey, but I have him on speaker.”

Asim gestured for her to come in.

She nodded and approached his desk, tablet in hand. “He said he’ll need deeper tissue sampling and may have to send out data to other labs, but we’ve got initial findings.”

Asim rubbed his face. “Johnson, you there? Give me the conclusion.”

“I’m here, Colonel,” Johnson said through Ava’s tablet. “Look, there’s no known clinic that can do anything like this. It isn’t a simple color mod or basic enhancement. It’s an entire eye redesign based on traits from animals.”

“Which animals?” Asim glanced up at Ava, who smirked. She’d probably gotten as little sleep as he had, but nobody would tell from looking at her.

“They’re a blend of human, owl, and cat eyes,” Johnson said. “They have what’s called a tapetum lucidum, a mirrored layer to boost low-light vision. Nocturnal creatures typically have them; it’s why their eyes reflect at night and look like they glow in the dark.”

“What about the vertical pupils?” Asim asked. This felt like middle school biology all over again. “Like a cat’s?”

“Right. The vertical slit is all about controlling light, with a wider range of contraction and dilation. Their eyes absorb more light than ours and need a way to curtail it in the daytime to avoid light blindness. The pupil will become thin during the day and open back up at night. The female’s eyes also have some weird microstructures I don’t recognize. But overall? They’ve probably got about 20/5 nocturnal vision, meaning they can see things four times farther off than we can at any given level of clarity, and in near total darkness.”

“So, enhanced operatives, basically,” Asim said, looking at Ava.

She nodded. “Night killers.”

“The disturbing part is the lack of micro-scars or other signs of modification,” Johnson said. “When surgeons make people’s eyes reflective in clinics for aesthetics, they just put harmless reflective pigments into the iris, at the front of the eye. But these tapetum lucidum layers are deep in the eye, behind the retina. And the other enhancements, like the vertical pupils, are foundational to the eye structure itself. This suggests they were born like this.”

Asim, who’d been reflexively running a hand through his hair, paused and brought his arm down. “But that doesn’t make sense. That giant corpse with those eyes at the rest stop must’ve been in his late twenties or older. You’re saying this gene tech existed back when he was born?”

“Seems that way, at least for whoever did this,” Johnson said. “There are mods that can let someone see mildly better at night or with up to 20/10 vision in the day, but nothing this sophisticated. There were some other things as well. The largest corpse has extremely high bone density. Never saw anything like it. And the youngest corpse has a neural implant in the back of his head. An X-ray showed that it’s more extensive than any I’ve seen. Initial DNA analysis from all three of the green-eyed ones revealed a ton of unknown genetic patterns mixed in with what is otherwise human. It’ll take a while to figure out what most of it does.”

“Anything else?” Asim asked.

“Yeah, the three green-eyed ones all have chips surgically implanted deep in the backs of their necks. We’ve only removed one so far for analysis, but assuming that all three are the same, they’re GPS tracking chips.” Johnson’s voice dropped a bit lower. “A high-end model, though not typically used on people.”

Asim thought for a bit. It had to be deep research. The Department of Defense—his own employer, ironically—had the budget to invest billions of dark dollars a year for several years to develop this type of shit in secret. It usually went nowhere and would be written off, but occasionally it led to something big. He looked at Ava but spoke to Johnson, “Did anyone figure out how all that blood burst out the backs of the bodies, or how a van was thrown the length of a football field?”

Ava shook her head.

“I don’t have a clue,” Johnson said over the line. “Not yet, anyway.”

That was okay. Asim’s upcoming jog might address some of that. Sometimes he had to check the pulse personally.



This was the kind of meeting you wouldn’t bring a phone to, unless it was in a faraday bag. Even switched off, many models could still be tracked. It was also the kind of meeting that you wouldn’t hold in anyone’s home or office, in case they’d been bugged.

Indeed, it was one of those old-school, accidental park-bench type of meetups with no electronic devices on your person or theirs—if you trusted them, that was. And you’d arrive at least half an hour apart.

So, as it happened, Asim found himself jogging in Gravelly Point Park in Arlington, Virginia, about an hour’s drive south of Fort Meade. And oh look, his old war buddy Roy O’Callaghan turned out to be jogging there today, too. What a coincidence.

Back during the Red Sea War and the operations that led up to it, Asim had worked military intelligence on foreign adversaries, going deep into hostile territory as an Arabic-English bilingual spy. His ethnicity helped him blend in, and he’d partnered with both Army and Navy strike teams to provide intel on enemy installations and leaders. Roy had been a member of SEAL Team Six, one of the elite units that handled the most complex, difficult, off-the-record, and sometimes internationally illegal missions, especially in the Red Sea theater at the time. Asim’s team marked targets to destroy, while Roy’s team would bring the destruction. Along the way, Roy had also saved Asim’s life, and they’d stayed in touch ever since.

“Isn’t it time to give up on regaining your six-pack?” Asim asked. “Maybe just embrace your flabby arc.”

“Only tryin’ to stay healthy,” Roy said. “Don’t have as much time to exercise as you do. Some of us have real jobs.”

Asim smiled, and both men slowed to a walk. The sky was overcast, and the riverside trails were only lightly trafficked. Every few minutes, a commercial jet roared almost too low overhead from the nearby DC airport—a novelty that kids loved. Including his own daughter Yasmin when she was that age.

“How’ve you been?” Asim asked.

“Pretty good. I’m workin’ on a lotta things. You?”

“Ah, well,” Asim said. He’d avoided that question ever since the divorce. And he didn’t meditate and shit like Roy did these days. “Now that I’m dealing with a crazy case, not great.”

“Oh?” Roy asked.

“Literally the worst I’ve seen.”

“Worse than Miles?”

“Maybe. Shaping up to be. It’s bad enough that I convinced ACI’s commander to let me oversee the case personally. We’ve opened a joint task force with both the FBI and CID on this, so now I get to coordinate that circus.”

“Well, you’ve got me interested.”

“Good, because I’ve got a question for you,” Asim said.

“Oh?”

“Back at the Red Sea, you mentioned some super black-ops soldiers with weird green eyes that glowed at night. You weren’t joking, were you?”

Roy eyed him. “No,” he said after a moment. “But we had the strictest orders never to talk about ’em. Shouldn’t have even mentioned it back then. Eight years later, and I still can’t talk about it.”

“And yet, here we are,” Asim said. “Off the record. Did they have vertical pupils? You didn’t mention that part.”

“I’m serious. This goes to the top,” Roy said.

“I can give you something in return.”

“I’m outta this line of work. What could you give me?”

“Lives. Human lives. One of those green-eyed operatives is off their leash. Gone rogue or something. I need to know what you know to save real, human lives from them, here at home.”

“Hmm.”

They walked in silence for a while. Asim didn’t push. Both of them carried regrets from the war. Both questioned the purpose of the whole thing to start with. And both, in their own ways, felt they had debts to pay. To whom, it wasn’t clear, even after one too many nights at the bar trying to figure it out together. Asim had shifted to counterintelligence for precisely that reason, to prevent military violence rather than contribute to it. As for Roy, he’d gone into private security work for VIPs.

“It’s becoming serial,” Asim added. “I think it’ll get worse. But for now, there might be one or two live captives that I haven’t given up on.”

Roy stared out over the Potomac and scratched his head with his synth arm. He’d never bothered to get a skin coating; he left it looking robotic on purpose. “What do you need to know?”

“Who were those people? And did they have vertical pupils?”

“Super-soldiers,” Roy said. “And yes, they did. Better eyesight. Better strength. Better reflexes. Better healing. Bred to kill. As if you were to design a spec-ops soldier from the ground up.”

“Could any of them throw a van a hundred yards?” Asim asked, regretting how dumb it sounded as soon as the words came out.

Roy eyed him again. “Not throw, no. And not something that big. But some of ’em had other abilities.”

Asim glanced out over the water, took a deep breath, then looked back at Roy. The man clearly had more to say.

“Like . . . telekinesis,” Roy said. “Movin’ objects with your mind. A green-eyed woman had that ability instead of enhanced strength. Not vans a hundred yards, though.”

Asim looked down to the ground and then back up at his old friend. “Please tell me you’re fucking with me.”

“Wish I was. But I saw it myself.”

“How many of those operatives did you know?”

“Four men and one woman,” Roy said. “Don’t know the total, but those were assigned to our group at the Red Sea. All with the same eyes. The four men dwarfed us in strength and speed, and the only advantage we had was experience.” He sighed. “But I think in a twisted way, they were considered less risky to send on the most dangerous missions than us. No families back home. No news leaks if they died. They could go deep into towns and kill with more precision and subtlety than a missile or drone, to avoid headlines about collateral damage.”

“And the woman?”

“She was the youngest of the five I knew. Eighteen, maybe nineteen. Unlike the others, she wasn’t a real soldier. More like an assassin. The others were in their twenties. All of ’em would be eight years older now, if any are still alive.”

“Did she have a name?” Asim asked.

“They were numbered, though some of them used nicknames. More of ’em joined over the years. Men for strength, women for telekinesis. At least a couple dozen overall. They still work with the most elite spec-ops teams today.”

“What can you tell me about her? The woman you knew?”

“She went by Fallon,” he said. “She demanded to be called that, instead of her number. And if one of ’em is off their leash, it sounds like her.”







Chapter 3





Ten Years Earlier


We still have not identified where or how consciousness arises, despite our best efforts. We have identified and harnessed more lucrative outcomes, nonetheless.

–TS log 10726, excerpt

fallon took a deep breath to calm her nerves. Today was likely the day she would graduate.

Dr. Stallard strode onto the artificial grass in front of the girls, stern as ever behind his short gray beard. “Now it’s time for the Class C reclassification test,” he said. “We’ll see who moves up to the next tier, and whether any of you might graduate to fieldwork.” His gray eyes glanced toward Fallon as he said that last part.

Didn’t they? Yes, they did. He knows I’m ready, Fallon told herself.

Several members of Class B had entered fieldwork over the past few years, but Fallon would be the first from Class C. And at only sixteen years old, she’d be the youngest to reach that level, even compared to the earliest of Class B, which was full of brutes anyway.

Well, mostly. She glanced at Jax. He gave her a reassuring nod—and a wink.

No distractions, she thought. Eyes on Dr. Stallard.

She’d nearly reached tier three at the prior semiannual test but had narrowly failed the precision requirement, and she refused to let that happen again. Nobody expected anyone to graduate this young, but Fallon didn’t strive toward adequacy—she strove toward greatness. If you’re bred for just one thing, you might as well be the best at it.

Twelve other girls stood in line with Fallon at the end of the facility’s enclosed sports and testing field, but none were as skilled as her. Dozens of people watched, including several of the Class B men who’d been tested in the morning session, tons of lab dorks, that silly woman who pretended to be everyone’s mother, and some suit-wearers and generals from the outside world who only came during these semiannual tests. And the ever-present men with guns, of course.

Eyes on Dr. Stallard. Not them, she reminded herself. On Father.

“For those of you who will be seeing this for the first time, you’re in for something special,” Dr. Stallard told the audience. “Our young women of Class C have starkly different abilities than the young men of Class B you saw earlier. C3, you’re first. Please come up here.”

Maria, or “C3,” stepped forward from Fallon’s left side. The oldest living subject of Class C at seventeen, Maria had wavy brown hair that twirled down past her shoulders, olive skin, and the same special green eyes as the rest of the subjects. Her stiff posture made it clear she was nervous, at least to those who knew her well. And it was right for her to be nervous—Maria wasn’t ready for tier three.

“Just like last time,” Dr. Stallard said. “Please pick up the tungsten ball, hold it two yards off the ground, and then shift it toward your left by one yard.”

Maria looked at the dense, three-hundred-pound tungsten ball sitting on the zero-yard line and reached out with her right hand in a grasping motion. Although she couldn’t feel it yet, Fallon knew the air temperature would have dropped slightly around Maria.

As Maria moved her grasped hand upward, then slightly left, the tungsten ball levitated off the ground, paused briefly, then moved left and paused again. Kinetic energy in the air molecules, which people felt as heat, was transferred to the ball. Temperature into motion. One of the new outsiders in suits gasped, and a few of them murmured to each other. That one bald general, Price, remained expressionless. He watched every test. Despite being an outsider, he felt almost familiar by this point.

Dr. Stallard used this as the basic test for the first tier of telekinetics—just make a single object levitate under your rough control. The only known people in the world who could do it were all standing on this field.

“Now, pick up the three tennis balls,” Dr. Stallard said. “Move them up and down while you move the tungsten ball from left to right.”

Maria took a deep breath and, with a grasping motion from her left hand, made all three tennis balls rise into the air as well. The tungsten ball began moving left to right as the three tennis balls moved up and down in unison.

It was of course impossible for anyone in the class to separately control four objects in four different directions simultaneously; they only had two mental arms or “foci” of independent control built into their brain structures. But by grasping the tungsten ball as one mental group and all three tennis balls as a second mental group, they could be controlled as two independent groups. Each member of Class C also had two mental legs or “bracers” that they could plant on the ground or another solid foundation. They could transfer momentum from their foci through their bracers to that foundation rather than through their own bodies. And so, rather than Maria being pushed around when moving the tungsten ball, she transferred the momentum to the earth with her corresponding bracer. This simultaneous combination of balls was the test for tier two, which Maria had reached years ago. It likely wasn’t too challenging for her anymore, and yet only about half of the girls could do it.

The next part is where she’ll fail again, Fallon thought. Like last time, and the time before.

“Very good, C3,” Dr. Stallard said. “Now, keep moving the tennis balls up and down, and throw the tungsten ball as far as you can down the field.” Subjects needed to clear fifty yards to pass the tier-three power requirement. Fallon had been the sole member of her class to hit that distance, at sixty-one yards to be exact. Maria wouldn’t reach it; she could barely do forty.

Maria stared down the field. The air around her chilled further, cool enough now for Fallon to feel it where she stood. Maria shouted as she thrust out her right hand. The tungsten ball flew down the field and landed on the fifty-three-yard line. She breathed heavily, and a rare hint of a smile formed on her lips.

Fallon’s pulse quickened. Could Maria actually make tier three? No. No way. There’s still the hardest part.

“Excellent, C3,” Dr. Stallard said, a rare flicker of excitement in his voice. “That is a new distance for you.” He gestured to one of the lab dorks, who brought over . . . a bottle of champagne? And a champagne glass?

But there’s still the precision test!

Dr. Stallard grabbed the bottle from the lab dork, twisted off the foil, and popped the cork. Then he reached out, took the narrow glass, and poured in some of the golden liquid. “Now, C3, take this glass from my hand, bring it to yourself, and sip from it without spilling a drop.”

Fallon exhaled in relief. There still was the precision test, of course. The third tier required moving the same number of independent object groups as the second tier, but with more power and precision. Fallon had prioritized her recent practice on the precision aspect, which she’d failed last time, and she was confident it would separate her from Maria and the two other half-decent ones in her class, Jolce and Zarina. Unlike the test with the balls, Dr. Stallard mixed up the precision test each time so that no one could practice beforehand for a specific scenario. Last time, the scenario was to levitate a device that kept moving and shifting its center of mass through a tight maze without touching the walls.

This time, they only had to drink from a glass?

As Fallon watched along with everyone else, Maria gripped with the fingers of her right hand. Using the focus she had previously used to throw the tungsten ball, she gained control of the champagne glass. With a small but visible degree of wobbliness, she nudged the glass out of Dr. Stallard’s fingers and brought it toward herself, yard by yard, while still moving the three tennis balls up and down in unison with her left focus, her left hand rocking up and down at the same tempo. The testing field went even quieter.

Much to Fallon’s annoyance, Maria got the glass all the way to her mouth without a spill. But as she reached forward and began to tilt the glass toward her lips, she used slightly too much force, and a few drops of champagne dribbled down her chin as she sipped. Eyes wide, she looked up at Dr. Stallard.

“You’ve come a long way, C3,” he said, sounding rehearsed. “Your progress is of a caliber that all here should emulate. But you spilled some, so you remain on the high end of tier two. I’m confident you’ll pass next time. Please return to the line.”

Fallon stifled a smile. She herself had ranked at the top of tier two for a few years now, and despite her over-preparedness, she knew deep down that there was a small chance she’d fail to reach tier three today and stay stuck in development for another six months. Now, with Maria having failed, even if Fallon were to somehow mess up too, at least there’d be nobody from her class above her.

The cold region of air dissipated into the field, and everyone clapped. Fallon tapped her hands together several times to play along with the silly ritual, and she met Maria’s eyes as the girl rejoined them in line.

One of the Class B men, Santiago, hauled the tungsten ball back to the starting line. Although it weighed three hundred pounds, he carried it almost like a basketball.

“Step forward, C5,” Dr. Stallard said as the clapping waned. “You’re next.”

“Thank you, Dr. Stallard,” Fallon said with a proper soldier’s volume. She strode over to the starting spot. One day, he would use her chosen name rather than her lab number. Or she’d be powerful enough to make him.

Forget that, she reminded herself. Focus on the test.

Dr. Stallard cleared his throat. “Please pick up the . . .”

Fallon tucked a stray chunk of her chin-length black hair back behind her ear and started before he could finish his sentence. Mapping the details of the surrounding air into her awareness to a degree that lesser minds couldn’t fathom, she drew its energy toward the tungsten ball. Squeezing her right fist, she mentally gripped the ball with her right focus and raised it into the air, sliding it left to right. Simultaneously, she planted her right bracer into the ground to transfer the momentum of the heavy lift to the earth instead of to her own body. With her second focus, she took control of the tennis balls and moved them up and down, her left hand rocking up and down similarly. She didn’t need to plant her second bracer for that little weight.

“Yes, Dr. Stallard,” she said. Tier two passed, obviously. She’d been able to do this since she was ten years old.

“Very good,” Dr. Stallard said. “Now throw the tungsten ball as far as . . .”

Fallon had already been amping up during her father’s needless talk. The air chilled around her, prickling her skin, and she threw the tungsten ball forward with everything she had, reaching into new depths of herself. She’d have a headache all day after this, but it would be worth it for a test that only came twice a year. The ball blazed down the field, faster and farther than Maria could ever hope to throw it, and landed with a heavy thud near the end.

“It’s eighty-seven yards,” a lab dork said, excitement clear in his voice. Dr. Stallard turned to look.

He’s impressed, Fallon thought. He knew going into this that I’m the only one who’s ready. And I’ve surprised even him. Her head throbbed, but the thrill dulled some of the pain.

“Remarkable,” Dr. Stallard said in an annoyingly mild tone. “Jacob, we haven’t recorded anything of that distance even during practice, have we?”

The lab dork shook his head. “It’s a new record. The prior was sixty-one yards, also set by C5.”

Rather than looking happy, Dr. Stallard locked his smile-less expression onto Fallon. He spoke to the crowd, but with his eyes remaining fixed only on her, meant for her. “We now have the precision test for our second subject.”

He gestured to the lab dork, who came over again with the champagne and the glass. Dr. Stallard poured again, and as it neared the right level, Fallon took a deep breath, ready to move it.

Dr. Stallard, however, kept pouring. He let the bubbles subside, then poured in more, let them subside again, then filled the glass all the way up to the rim. “Bring it to your lips and sip, with no drops lost,” he said, voice like ice.

Fallon froze. It would be impossible not to spill the liquid, being within a millimeter of the rim. Even for her. She’d assumed he wanted her in the field, and that she was going to be the first Class C agent. Had she overstepped? What was he thinking? Why would he sabotage her like this?

Would . . . would she fail the precision test again?

No. Not again. She blocked out everyone else except Dr. Stallard and started to take the glass from his fingers. No, not even Dr. Stallard now. Eyes only on this hellish rim. Not a millimeter’s worth of a drop over. There’s a surface tension; you probably need to mess up by two millimeters to spill and fail the test. The field doesn’t exist. There is no Dr. Stallard. There is no Jax. There is no Maria. There is no—Focus. Ignore even that recursive thought.

Do. Not. Spill.

Most people couldn’t see clearly from this distance, but Fallon’s eyes were made for this. She zoomed in on the rim, seeing every glint and bubble, then gently lifted the glass from Dr. Stallard’s hand. The golden liquid’s surface rippled, but none of it dribbled over the millimeter edge.

As far as Fallon was concerned, she was that millimeter. It was the only millimeter to ever exist. She moved the glass toward herself with her right focus without thinking about those “why” questions as she normally would, instead keeping her mind on every microscopic tilt of the liquid relative to the glass rim. Yard after yard, the glass moved toward her, with not a drop spilled. She shivered from the air’s lingering chill on her skin and ignored it. Nothing but the narrow distance between the liquid and the glass mattered.

The champagne reached her lips, and then—Keep attention on the rim gap. She paused, making sure not to repeat Maria’s mistake. Fallon then carefully leaned forward and took a sip out of the glass, hands-free. It was more bitter than she’d expected; she hadn’t tasted champagne before. Partially drained, the glass was much easier to move. She shifted her attention to Dr. Stallard, staring at him and smirking, then moved the glass toward him and set it back in his hand despite him not having asked her to.

The audience murmured. Fallon took several seconds and then caught her breath, gasping visibly, much to her chagrin.

“I believe you’ve reached the third tier, C5,” Dr. Stallard said. “Welcome to graduation, and to fieldwork. Given that you’ve already passed your other requirements, you’ll be the first to join some of the young men of Class B in the field.”

“It would be my honor, Doctor,” Fallon said.

Everyone clapped, including General Price. Even Maria joined in, though with a slight delay and no smile. She, as Fallon’s elder, had been upstaged. Jax whistled from the audience.

“Thank you, C5,” Dr. Stallard continued, sounding robotic. Not even now would he act fatherly. “You have elevated today’s test with a new telekinetic record. Please return to your position.”

Fallon nodded to him respectfully, then walked back to the line of girls. She looked at Maria and smirked, because why not. Maria’s expression darkened.

The next couple of tier two Class C subjects, Jolce and Zarina, were boring. Yet again, they failed the distance test for tier three. A few yards deeper than their prior tests, sure, but not even to forty, and as such they weren’t invited to try the precision test. Quinn gave it her shot too and failed, of course.

Then there were several tests by the younger ones, thirteen and fourteen years old. Some tier one testers moved to tier two by demonstrating independent control of the tungsten ball and the three tennis balls for the first time. A couple remained in tier one. Fallon had trouble caring either way, except for the last girl. Sara. Twelve years old now, and she still couldn’t levitate a single object. A retard, basically.

“And C17,” Dr. Stallard said, looking toward her. “Please step forward.”

“I still can’t do it,” Sara said. “I’m sorry.”

He continued to stare. “Even shortcomings are worth documenting. Step forward.”

Fallon couldn’t help but crack a smile.

After a moment’s hesitation, Sara walked out to the starting position. A quiet girl with long, platinum-blond hair, she was the class failure. Dr. Stallard and his lab dorks sometimes tweaked the genetic modification a bit from subject to subject to test different iterations, and with Sara it’d been a misfire. She had the same special eyes, fast reflexes, and enhanced healing as all the rest, but she lacked telekinesis almost entirely. And she didn’t have the superior strength that the boys in Class B had either, of course. Worthless.

“Just try to lift one of the tennis balls, C17,” Dr. Stallard said.

Sara took a deep breath, narrowed her eyes, and made an exaggerated grasping motion with her right hand. The air didn’t seem to chill, and the tennis ball barely wobbled. Sara kept her hand in a clawlike grasp, gritting her teeth in effort, but to no avail. Eventually she gasped, breathing hard, and looked down at her feet as the tennis ball went still.

“Why do you think you still can’t lift it?” Dr. Stallard asked.

“I don’t know. I can grip it, but it feels like it weighs a thousand pounds.”

“For the record,” Dr. Stallard said to the room, “the ability is present, but the magnitude is wrong. C17 is like the rest of the class in all other respects, but the method we tried on her failed. We iterate the gene-editing process to optimize it, but in this one case, we seem to have erred.”

One case? Fallon thought. C1 had died of a brain tumor as a toddler before even choosing a name for herself. Morgan, C2, had gone crazy a couple of years ago and was put down. Heather, C4, killed herself last year. And Amber, C11, died from a brain hemorrhage in the middle of mentally lifting an object when she was eight.

Dr. Stallard rubbed his beard and scanned the line of girls. “I want to try something new today. C3, please step back up here.”

Maria raised her eyebrows. She turned to her right and glanced at Fallon, who shrugged in return. Maria walked out onto the field.

“Though I’ve not authorized it,” Dr. Stallard said, “I know some of you like to do your little duels. C3, I want you to do that with C17. Perhaps a bit of pressure will get it out of her.”

Sara’s eyes widened.

“I’m not one of those who duels,” Maria said.

“But you know how they work, yes?”

Maria nodded.

“Good.” He gestured to Sara. “Apply it to C17 here for motivation. Start with a push.”

Silence settled oddly over the field. Several of the lab dorks glanced at each other, and one of the outsiders in suits cleared his throat.

Maria scanned the audience, then turned back to Dr. Stallard. “But she doesn’t have any defenses. It’s unfair.”

Dr. Stallard chuckled and looked at the outsiders in suits and military uniforms. “I’m sure some people here take their children to martial arts lessons, correct? Or football? Sometimes what is needed to learn is pain. Applied safely.” He turned back toward Maria. “I’m not asking you to injure her. Just apply some pressure.”

Maria eyed Sara up and down. The girl trembled. A teenager against a child. Maria turned and paced a few yards away, then spun back around. “Ready?”

Sara glanced at Dr. Stallard, who stared back at her, stone-faced. She looked back at Maria, took another deep breath, and nodded. Immediately, Sara let out a yelp as she flew back several feet and landed hard on her back. Maria was holding out her right palm, which she had just thrust forward.

“Push your classmate back, C17,” Dr. Stallard said.

Sara scrambled back to her feet. “I can’t do it!”

Dr. Stallard looked over toward Maria. “Again.”

Maria met his eyes in return. “But I—”

“I said again, C3.”

Maria grimaced and took a deep breath. She pushed on Sara a second time, but with a bit less force. The girl was slammed back several feet. She staggered to catch herself, as though she’d been punched hard in the chest, but managed not to fall. Her eyes watered, and she looked over at eleven-year-old Damien, one of the creepy children of Class D that she spent most of her time with. As though he could save her.

“Concentrate, C17,” Dr. Stallard said, drawing out each syllable. “If you want this to end, just push your classmate once.”

Sara narrowed her eyes and pushed her palm toward Maria, but with no effect. Maria looked at Dr. Stallard.

“Again.”

Maria pushed Sara once more, gently this time, only forcing her to take a couple of steps back to avoid tripping.

Dr. Stallard clicked his tongue. “That wasn’t a real push. Again.”

Maria sighed and looked at Sara. “Sir . . . I can’t. It’s just—”

“Go back in line, C3, thank you. C5, please come up here.”

Fallon perked up. Oh, this’ll be fun. With no hesitation, she strode out to the field as Maria returned to the line. Fallon watched her as they crossed paths, but Maria wouldn’t make eye contact.

Duels were about two things: speed and power. If the opponent tried to move your body, you could negate, redirect, or “grab” their focus with yours, assuming you were powerful enough. Although they were invisible to normal people, telekineticists could see each other’s foci and bracers when they were active, like four ghostly limbs with flexible shapes. Telekinetic duels would end when one knocked the other off their feet, either because they reacted too slowly or because they were too weak to negate or redirect a force against them.

Sara looked at Fallon with glassy eyes, and Fallon smiled. Then she pushed.

Sara lifted off her feet and flew back even farther than when Maria had pushed her the first time. She landed on her side with a gasp, then clutched her ribs and started crying silently. Fallon looked around the field as some of the observers whispered to each other. Emmett, one of the senior lab dorks, squeezed the railing in front of him, furrowing his brow. The silly motherly woman, Michelle, frowned and murmured something to Emmett. General Price just watched with a stern face. Approving.

Sara stayed down, red-faced and pouting, until Dr. Stallard shouted at her to stand. Visibly startled—Dr. Stallard rarely raised his voice—Sara got back to her feet, sniffling. Her messy blond hair covered half her face, stuck to her cheeks by the tears. Pathetic.

Fallon struck a second time without Dr. Stallard having to tell her. Sara tried to use one of her foci to block it, but it was feeble, and Fallon’s focus punched through it like a piece of paper. The girl hit the ground again but somehow got back up right away.

Red-faced, teary-eyed, and with a furious expression, she slid one foot behind herself for more leverage to physically resist the next push. Adorable.

Fallon pulled this time, her hand making a grasping motion, and Sara flew forward as if shoved from her backside, letting out a surprised yelp as she did.

Fallon made sure to do these pushes and pulls with a slight diagonal upward tilt each time, to generate some lift. Might as well please the crowd. The girl landed hard a few feet in front of Fallon, barely breaking her fall with her hands in time. Then she clutched her wrist and started crying audibly. Fractured, probably.

“Okay, that’s enough,” Dr. Stallard said with a sense of disgust. Whispers rose from among the audience.

Fallon took a couple of steps toward Sara and looked down at her. She was on her knees, quivering and clutching her wrist. The funny thing was that Dr. Stallard wouldn’t be able to blame Fallon for this, not after having told Maria to go harder.

Sara looked up with a sniffle, and Fallon offered out her hand to help her up. Then she squeezed. Telekinesis consisted of more than just pushes and pulls. Like how someone could crush an aluminum can with their hand, Fallon could do it with her mind. But instead of a can, in this case it was Sara’s throat.

With her second focus, she locked down Sara’s arms and legs, preventing them from moving an inch. She could control all four limbs with her second focus, as long as she applied the same directional force to each of them, like she did with the tennis balls. Sara remained frozen on her knees, clutching her wrist, looking up with that pathetic wet face. She widened her eyes and gasped silently for air, but to anyone farther away it probably still looked like crying.

“It’s okay, take my hand,” Fallon said with a smile. She felt Sara frantically trying to move, but kept her locked down. Relative to throwing a three-hundred-pound tungsten ball eighty-seven yards, paralyzing someone was easy, despite her growing headache. She even did it without making any hand motions.

Although most of the younger girls required those gestures to focus properly, Fallon and Maria did not. The motions weren’t strictly necessary, just helpful in aiding concentration for precision or power due to the close connections between their arms and foci within their brain structures. Practicing without them was one of the things they did once they reached tier two, since it was often useful to hide what one was doing. Fallon just stood there, holding out her hand toward Sara, pretending to offer to help her up while mentally squeezing her throat and keeping her still.

Seconds passed, and Sara’s face grew redder. She couldn’t even move her head, with the mental clutch around her neck. Or make a sound. Seconds more. Exhilaration blossomed in Fallon’s chest; the girl’s life was in her hands. She stared into Sara’s eyes, watching every twitch and flicker of her pupils as her panic grew.

Unfortunately, it would become increasingly noticeable to onlookers if she kept Sara in this position, looking up for too long without movement. She tilted Sara’s throat a bit, forcing her to look down. On her knees, clutching her wrist, staring at the floor, and shaking. Fallon couldn’t see her eyes anymore, but the position looked more natural.

“Sara, what’s wrong?” Fallon asked out loud. She went down to one knee and stroked Sara’s hair. “It’s over now, you’re okay. Does your wrist hurt?”

She could feel every feeble attempt the girl made to move and prevented her from doing so. She caressed her hair twice more to appear comforting, letting more time flow by. “You’re a knife with a dull blade,” she whispered, low enough so that only Sara would hear. “Maybe I should put you out of your misery. Would you like that?”

“Go back in line, C5. I’ll take it from here.” Dr. Stallard’s voice. Fallon glanced up, startled. He stood right next to them. She’d been too focused on Sara to notice him come over. The air had chilled around them, and he surely felt it. His eyes looked grim. “Now.”

Sara’s struggles were weakening anyway. If Fallon held any longer, the girl would go unconscious. So, she released her. Sara gasped and coughed, then fell to her side into a little ball, clutching her throat.

“I was only trying to help. I don’t know what’s wrong with her,” Fallon said with the sweetest voice she could muster, for the room to hear. She shrugged and walked back to the line of her classmates. The audience couldn’t see what she’d done, but the girls could, including Maria. They all stared at Fallon with horrified expressions.

Good, she thought.







Chapter 4



Asim called an all-hands case team meeting, and over two dozen faces looked up at him as he stood at the head of their conference room table. ACI was working on other cases simultaneously, but this one was by far the strangest, and he needed progress now.

It’d been eight days since the New Jersey rest stop murder and seven since the Brooklyn alley slaughter. Despite ACI’s coordination with the FBI and others, the two potential captives were still unaccounted for, and the case had practically gone cold.

Part of the complication was that he couldn’t put Roy on the official record as his source about the green-eyed ones being black-ops soldiers and assassins from the Red Sea War and elsewhere. He’d quietly told Ava and Flanagan about it to guide their searches, but he couldn’t really tell anyone else.

“So, let’s see here,” Asim said to the room, feeling the pressure of so many eyes. Fortunately, he’d made sure not to be too hungover for this. “No ID from Johnson’s eye analysis, since there aren’t any clinics capable of this. But the complexity suggests DoD funding. They all have GPS trackers, and one of them has an unidentifiable neural implant. And we have other strange elements in this case, such as massive blood loss for three bodies through the skin, a nearly seven-foot armored man thrown into a wall hard enough to destabilize the building, and evidence of a van being launched a hundred yards.”

He raised his eyebrows and continued. “We’ve also faced unprecedented legal roadblocks trying to get a court order to force Eidolon Solutions, employer of the two identifiable bodies and owner of the busted van, to release client info. Whoever the client is, they’re more tied in than us. And nobody’s supposed to be more tied in than us. Even those ex-military operatives’ families won’t talk.”

Asim looked around, seeing if anyone had any comments or objections. No one did.

“Yet the part that is frankly unfathomable to me,” he said, “is how we can’t identify this fucking prostitute. The most mundane aspect of this whole case. I know the footage was grainy and in its shitty night mode, but what sample size could we be dealing with here? It’s not like she commutes from Ohio every day for work. She’s almost certainly around Brooklyn. People know her. How have none of our bots found any good patterns yet?”

They’d long since identified the prostitute’s three dead associates, but none of their relatives knew much about their illicit activities or with whom they worked. And criminals tended not to leave extensive records—they dealt in cash and off-the-record relational deals. One of them was believed to have owned bitcoin that had moved on the blockchain two days after his death, but because of privacy measures he’d taken, their chain analysis bots were only 87 percent certain it had been his in the first place.

A street-level canvass of businesses and potential contacts had produced a few clues about the prostitute. The first was that people only knew her by the nickname “Jitters.” The second was that she changed her hair color often but tended to default back to ruby red frequently. And the third was that she worked as a stripper and escort but had recently gotten into mugging with those men as well. Probably to skip the sex and move straight to the money.

“Brooklyn has some of the worst camera density in the Northeast, sir,” Ava said. “People keep disrupting them.”

“But still, how hard is it to find a red-headed prostitute that goes by fucking Jitters?” Asim asked. “Is it a problem of not enough people on the ground? Tell me how I can help.”

“Locals seem to like her,” Flanagan offered, raising his shoulders. He was Asim’s most senior analyst, a rotund civilian in his sixties, with a thick head of gray hair and a handlebar mustache. “There are a few grocery clerks that say they don’t know her, but we suspect they do. Even so, they won’t necessarily know her real name or address. We found the strip club she works at, but they didn’t collect all the employment info they were supposed to from her, and she hasn’t shown up since her abduction. She was popular there.”

“And the more polite term is sex worker, sir,” Ava added.

Asim sighed and pinched the bridge of his nose. “Fine. I want an updated list of every one of her acquaintances and their addresses. Including any repeat clients if possible. Someone knows her real name.”

“In addition to these other leads, I think we should focus more on the online activity, sir,” Ava said. “I’m still trying to make sense of it.”

“What’s the latest?” Asim asked.

“Well sir, I’d say that the defining characteristic is noise.” Ava had a knack for overseeing more autonomous AI agents at once than anyone else in their division; she could make sense of an enormous amount of data as they collected it for her. “Someone else’s bots are really focused on this. To an unusual degree.”

Asim had heard this multiple times from her and tried to make use of it somehow without any clear angle, but since this was an all-hands meeting, he made sure to broadcast the knowledge to the whole group. “Give us the current overview.”

“Yes, sir. The rest stop incident has accumulated a ton of social media activity in the edge realms, despite our attempts to contain data leaks. Within the first seventy-two hours, there were already thousands of posts adamantly arguing for different viewpoints. With so much content being bot-generated, it’s hard to be sure of any narrative’s origin. We collected a few main themes with significant social graph support, though.”

She continued, failing to hold back a smirk while raising one finger. “One popular view is that this is related to aliens. A subset of these posts claim that the nearby Stolguard Biological Research Facility was involved.” She raised a second finger to join the first. “Another popular view is that this is a false show of force by the US military for China, and that anything coming out soon will be faked via computer graphics and psyops, to pretend that we’re further along in bioweapon technology than we are.” Her third finger went up. “Another, much less popular view is that this was related to mutants, and that the Stolguard facility made super-soldiers, but that its director quietly built something more dangerous therein. A ‘dark god’ was one of the phrasings used. And there’s plenty of debate, of course.”

She looked around the room and lowered her hand. “There are hundreds of identities throughout the edge realms claiming to be the murderer, and a lot of them are making increasingly grandiose claims about these aliens or super-soldiers. Based on our analysis, the most credible claims from all this chatter seem to involve the Stolguard facility in some way. It’s only an hour’s drive from the rest stop crime scene. Our surveillance work traced the black van back to a highway exit right near it, along with other black vans as well, and the two deceased Eidolon Solutions ex-military operatives found at the rest stop have residences within commuting distance. The facility hasn’t returned any of our inquiries, and our warrant is stuck in limbo. They even turned some of our agents away at the front gate.”

“Aliens, psyops, and mutants are all fascinating,” Asim said, “but ultimately, we have three credible leads to focus on from all of this. The first is that prostitute, or sex worker. Because she’s our best clue to where the perp went next. The fact that we haven’t even identified someone who goes by ‘Jitters’ yet is embarrassing. The second is to find out more about this Stolguard facility. The third is to identify the client of Eidolon Solutions that those dead ex-military operatives had been assigned to. With the Stolguard facility, we have a likely candidate. Nobody’s supposed to be able to ignore our calls.”

“Wouldn’t it be easier to go to the top?” Flanagan asked. “Maybe Davis can connect us to whatever group created this problem faster than we can work it bottom-up from the streets.”

“You assume I haven’t tried that already?” Asim asked, raising his voice more than he intended. “You think I just saw a bunch of black-ops bodies at the rest stop, got blocked on the Eidolon client list and stonewalled by Stolguard, and never asked my commander for help with identification? There’s a wall here. Interdivision conflict. An impenetrable line drawn that I’ve never seen before. Which means we actually have to do our jobs for a change.”

Flanagan nodded and looked down at his coffee. In that moment, seeing the older man’s mustache droop, Asim felt more disgusted with himself than with his team. Ever since he’d returned from the war, his job had been simple: to use the government’s best surveillance resources to find and stop spies, saboteurs, and terrorists. Computers did most of the legwork.

One crazy exception was the Miles case, which had pushed him past his limits. But after his success and promotion from that national mess into his current role, work became smooth again. It was almost as if after he won once—overwhelmingly—and turned into a bureaucratic celebrity for it, everything became easy.

Too easy. Out-of-touch easy. It started not to matter what he did anymore.

Other than watching his team use cameras and data searches facilitated by AI, Asim knew in his heart that he barely did anything now. This was a cushy job after the war, after his prior success, and a prostitute called Jitters had somehow circumvented all of it.

People in the room looked up at him with mixed expressions. Right. Time to turn this around. He had read enough self-help books to know that, first and foremost, he shouldn’t be a self-absorbed emotional asshole.

“Look,” he said, softening his voice. “We’ve got the best resources for a case like this. But once or twice in our time together, we’ve hit a wall. Something that we can’t just out-surveil. So, we need to be real detectives again, and all of you were drawn from elite backgrounds. In some sense, you did so well at your jobs that you barely needed to do them anymore.” He took a breath and made sure to project. “Well, now I need your best work again. Others have intel on this that we don’t have access to, and we need to work for it the hard way. I have faith in all of you. Find me Jitters. Find me new angles. I’ll work on getting more intel on the Stolguard facility.”



Asim sat in one of his bosses’ offices at the Pentagon. “Since we’re still held up by the court on Eidolon Solutions’ client list, I’m again requesting direct access to whoever runs the Stolguard facility, sir. I’ve never seen this much resistance to basic info requests from inside our own system. The case is going cold.”

Across from him sat Brigadier General Tyler Davis, a tall, intense, dark-skinned man in his fifties with the deepest voice of anyone he knew. Asim viewed him as the prototypical general—he looked and sounded exactly like the type of guy who was cast to play every hard-ass general in the movies. The man served as the commander of ACI, with Asim serving as his number two and running most of the day-to-day operations.

Two days had passed since Asim’s all-hands meeting with his case team, and top-secret bureaucracy still stood in the way. It had never been an issue for him in the past, so he had to push again rather than sit on his hands.
