To complicate matters, he’d been reporting to multiple offices lately. Usually, he was stationed at Fort Meade in Maryland. But Davis, Asim’s boss, was serving on a temporary assignment at the Pentagon and needed in-person updates once or twice per week. Occasionally, Asim had to get his ass all the way over to Fort Belvoir in Virginia, where his boss’s boss, Major General Ashley Scholl, also needed some updates.

“Scholl and I already brought it up with the Joint Chiefs,” Davis said. “We were told sensitive matters are in play and it’s being handled. I’m not elevating it again unless something materially changes. The fact the case has gone cold may be evidence it’s already contained. Have you considered the possibility that they’ve caught their rogue operative, and the captives are either paid off or quietly in custody for relocation?”

Asim had thought of that, of course. But he couldn’t wrap up a case like this without closure. “Can we at least request confirmation once it’s been handled, sir?”

“I think I can do that, in time,” Davis said, checking his watch. “It’s awkward, since it would be an admission of an off-the-books program. But I can probably get a private word once it’s done, to avoid wasting resources. I’ve unfortunately got a call in a moment. Interagency stuff.”

“Thank you, sir.” Asim smiled and stood up from his chair. “How’s Elijah doing? Did he make varsity? I’ve been meaning to ask.”

Davis’s expression softened. Like magic, the general transformed into a father. It helped, knowing people’s buttons. “He did, actually. The only sophomore who made the cut. The boy’s nearly my height already, if you can believe it.”

“Already?” Asim said. “Time sure as hell flies, doesn’t it?”

“Ain’t that the truth.” Davis chuckled. “Well, I’ll tell him you asked.”

“Thank you, sir,” Asim said, nodding and turning to leave. “And say hi to Alexis for me, if you would.”

“Will do,” Davis said warmly.

Asim left the office, gave a polite wave to the man’s secretary, and stepped into the Pentagon’s outer-ring corridor. It was the largest office building in the country, consisting of five separate concentric rings with connecting halls linking them together. Many of the inner ring’s office windows faced the central courtyard. The middle three rings had shitty window views, where you’d only see a thin courtyard and the wall of the next ring. The outer ring had the prime offices with the best views, reserved for the generals and other VIPs.

With business here done for the day, Asim exited the Pentagon on the side closest to the nearby metro station and boarded a train that would take him halfway back to Fort Meade, where one of ACI’s private cars would pick him up for the remainder of the trip. At his rank, he was supposed to have a car take him the whole way, but switching it up made him feel younger and a bit more connected to the world, rather than observing it from his own little bubble the entire ride.

Ah, a true man of the people, he thought sarcastically, catching his own ruminations. Not that it really mattered; nearly everyone on the train was focused on their phones or augmented glasses anyway.

Once he transferred to the car, with no more people to watch, Asim pulled out his phone and slid over to his contacts, lingering on his daughter’s number. Davis’s pride in his son had stirred something. Yasmin was probably off slaying a dragon with her friends in some fantasy realm. With a sigh, he pocketed the phone. She’d made it clear what she thought of him calling her out of the blue—or almost at all, for that matter.

When he arrived at his case team’s central conference room, a dozen agents and analysts were gathered around the wall-mounted TV.

“Did I miss something?” He directed the question to Ava but spoke loud enough for anyone to answer.

“An attack on NewsCon in Manhattan, sir. A terrorist has the newsroom hostage.”

Asim approached, studying the screen as his team parted for him. A pale woman in her twenties with those same strange green eyes held the newsroom at gunpoint.

So much for this being handled.







Chapter 5



“It’s recording?” asked the green-eyed woman in a low voice, staring into the camera without blinking. Her pupils were narrow vertical slits. Messy black hair, parted to one side, fell past her shoulders. She held a pistol in her right hand, pointed at the anchorwoman’s head.

“Yes,” a nervous male voice said from behind the camera.

“It better be.” She glanced down at her anchorwoman hostage, who sat trembling at the news desk, hands raised, breathing heavily. When the gunwoman looked back up toward the camera, Asim focused on her left eye. A vertical scar ran over it from her forehead to her cheek, and a second horizontal scar cut from the corner of her eye to her temple.

She still hadn’t blinked, which unnerved him as he watched.

“Listen to me,” she said. “I’m a—Didn’t I say not to move?” She looked toward someone in the studio, narrowing her eyes as she glanced off-camera. Her voice remained quiet, but those vertical pupils made her seem predatory by default, and nearly impossible to read. He couldn’t place any specific accent, either.

After a few seconds, she refocused back on the camera, exhaled tensely, and finally blinked. “I’m a new species of human, made by your government. You’ve ignored my messages, so I’ve had to come to you.”

“Eh, I was hoping for the alien theory rather than mutants, but alas,” Ava said, then sighed. Asim glanced over at her in disbelief.

“We’re soldiers and assassins,” the woman continued. “But something worse is—” The news feed went blank.

“Fuck.” Asim pulled out his phone and frantically brought up another news channel, to see what they were reporting. “Someone message Beth to get a chopper ready. I need to head to New York now. And get the FBI’s New York field office on the phone.”

“Security cameras caught the Caucasian female suspect entering the lobby minutes ago, shown here courtesy of NewsCon,” said an anchorman on Global News from Asim’s phone. “We must warn you that the footage is quite graphic.”

Asim glanced back up at the conference room screen to check on NewsCon, which was still offline, then down to his phone to watch the footage from Global News.

The woman, wearing a black overcoat, a thin armored vest, sunglasses, and combat boots, stepped into the lobby from the Manhattan street. She reached out with her right hand. Two security guards flew back like rag dolls into the wall, dropping limp to the floor after impact. She looked left as two other security guards pulled out pistols. Her left hand made a gripping motion, followed by a flick of her wrist, and both pistols ripped themselves out of the guards’ hands and flew across the lobby with unbelievable speed. One of them struck the lobby window and burst out onto the street in a spray of glass.

She paused, surveyed the lobby, then took off her sunglasses as multiple guests and receptionists scattered behind counters and furniture. One of the first guards she’d flung into the wall started to recover and pull out a pistol, so she reached out and pushed him back against the wall harder, then flicked her hand dismissively as his pistol flew across the room.

She pointed at a male receptionist, interrupting his process of ducking down behind the counter, and said something to him. He froze, then slowly stood back up, hands raised. He nodded and said something back.

Behind her, a tall man in a suit, who’d been standing next to the door on his phone when she entered, tried to sneak up and tackle her to the floor. Inch by inch, he closed the distance. But a few steps before he reached her, she turned her head to the left and glanced back at him out of the corner of her scarred eye. He lifted off the floor, flew in a tall arc over her head, and crashed down into one of the reception desks, with her emotionless face tracking his movement the whole way. After the impact, the man rolled off the desk and dropped to the floor, thrashing and reaching for his back, face contorted in pain.

The woman looked toward the elevators and said something to the same male receptionist as before, unholstered a pistol from her hip, then held it at her side with its muzzle pointing down. The receptionist nodded back and rushed over to the elevators. The woman followed him more slowly, turning to scan the lobby once more. After several more seconds of unrecorded dialogue, the two of them disappeared into one of the elevators.

“As you can see,” the anchorman said, hesitancy clear in his voice, “the suspect seems to have, well, unusual abilities. Pushing and pulling things through the air. We’re getting reports that—”

“NewsCon is back,” Ava said, and gestured for Asim to watch the television on the wall.

The live feed from the newsroom looked much the same as before. But now the green-eyed woman’s attention was fixed on something off to the right of the camera, her pistol lowered to her side instead of aimed at the anchorwoman. The anchorwoman’s head was down on the news desk, clutched in her hands, her shoulders shaking as blood stained her curly blond hair. The anchorman beside her held one hand on her back, concern etched across his tanned face. From the look of it, she’d taken a pistol-whip, not a bullet.

“Good,” the green-eyed woman said to someone off-camera. “Tell him that if the feed goes off again, I’ll kill her. Then I’ll kill another one every minute until it comes back on.”

Turning her gaze back toward the camera, she took a deep breath. Some of her black hair fell from behind her ear to cover the left side of her face, and she didn’t bother to push it back. “The Stolguard Biological Research Facility is breeding new species of human, like me.” She brought up the pistol and tapped its muzzle to her upper cheek, aimed at one of her eyes, emphasizing its strangeness.

She lowered the pistol and looked up at the ceiling. As her left hand curled into a grasping motion, one of the large stage lights telekinetically tore free, metal screeching over the broadcast. It levitated down to the center of the newsroom, then crushed in on itself with a sharp, echoing pop as she squeezed her fist closed. Glass shards sprayed in every direction.

The anchorman recoiled from the burst of sound and flying glass, shielding his face with his arms. The anchorwoman, still face down on the desk and clutching her head, trembled and gasped. The attacker stood motionless, unflinching even as shards sprayed at her, then turned her attention back to the camera. She opened her hand and let the crumpled stage light drop to the floor with a heavy metallic bang.

“Now, there’s something even . . .” The woman glanced away from the screen again and trailed off. Her jaw tightened and her eyes narrowed as she reached out with her left hand. The sound of automatic gunfire blared. Screams came from somewhere in the studio, and the feed went offline.

“That anchorwoman,” Ava whispered, eyes wide. “She’ll kill her.”

Asim took a deep breath to steady his dread. “Zhou and Brooks, you’re with me.” He unmuted his phone to listen to Global News as he power-walked toward the helipad with Ava and Brooks close behind. Baby-faced and with brown hair parted to one side, Brooks was one of ACI’s newest agents, fresh out of training, and Ava had taken him under her wing since he’d joined.

“We’re told that a shootout with police has broken out at NewsCon,” the anchorman said gravely. “We don’t yet know the—”

Asim muted it again. “She’s our perp,” he said. “Same armor and pistol models as the rest stop operatives. Same body language as the Brooklyn video. There’s no way she makes it out of that building. But if she dies, we’ll lose the chance to find the two captives, or any confirmation of what caused this mess.” He already suspected the captives were dead. Now he worried he’d never know. What if they were trapped somewhere, while police killed their captor?

Asim listened to the news for several minutes, but they were just repeating the situation, waiting for updates.

“I have several of our bots scanning social media, sir,” Ava said.

Asim nodded, waiting.

What happened here? This woman had likely turned on the others of her kind for some reason. Her behavior showed signs of psychopathy: few blinks, the unnerving way she pointed her pistol up at her own eye, and the quiet ease with which she threatened death.

“She’s out,” Ava said. “There’s a bystander clip on social media. Just posted. Look, sir.”

Asim watched Ava’s tablet. A shaky phone camera, filming from across the street, showed the skyscraper’s lobby. Dozens of police officers surrounded the building, using their cars and armored vehicles for cover. Overhead, a few specialized hovercars thrummed above the street, used by police to bypass earthbound traffic and reach upper floors of buildings.

“I dunno. I dunno. The police be getting tense now,” said the man holding the camera. “I dunno.”

The anchorman stepped out of the lobby, hands raised, with the woman close behind, her pistol pressed to the back of his head. She no longer wore her overcoat. As they reached the curb, a policeman’s voice boomed through a megaphone. “Release the hostage and put down the gun.”

“Oh, this bitch ’bout to get sniped,” the camera man said. “I’m tellin’ you.”

But the woman gritted her teeth and raised her left fist. A dozen police vehicles shot into the air with a grinding metallic roar, flying easily three stories high, colliding with the hovercars above as screams rang out.

Frost formed along the ground at her feet, and visible breath came from both her and the anchorman. She thrust her arm down. Everything crashed back to the street far faster than gravity alone would pull, shattering on impact. Metal shrieked. Barricades, vehicle parts, police drones—and bodies—flew in all directions. An armored SWAT vehicle punched straight down through the asphalt with a deafening crash, dragging part of the street with it into the sewers or subway below.

“Oh shit!” the camera man shouted, stumbling back as debris flew toward him, car alarms blaring in the background. The video shook violently, but AI stabilization kicked in after a second, stilling it.

Several police officers positioned far back, behind the impromptu camera man and outside of the impact radius of the fallen vehicles, opened fire on the attacker with their rifles, but with her pistol hand she reached out and . . . stopped the bullets midair. It was as if they bounced off an invisible shield, falling to the ground where she stood. More shots followed, but they only added to the growing pile.

With most of the officers dead or disrupted amid the carnage of shattered vehicles, and the snipers rendered ineffective, the woman walked forward. Clenching her fist near her chest, every muscle stretched tight in her lean forearm, she telekinetically pulled three ruined cars over to her, using them as a levitating shield to protect against additional shots. She reached the caved-in part of the street, dropped in, and crashed her floating vehicle shield over the hole.



As he flew from Fort Meade to Manhattan to examine the scene and coordinate with the FBI there, Asim took a few minutes between calls to come to terms with the existence of forces he didn’t understand.

Things he’d only seen in movies.

Enhanced strength, reflexes, and healing were simple enough to conceptualize. Hell, he even understood those owl-cat eyes, as bizarre a feat of bioengineering as they were. But to project invisible force at a distance, outside of the electromagnetic spectrum?

Then again, Asim had to admit he didn’t really know how something as basic as gravity worked. General relativity and its description of curved spacetime, if presented to him as a new concept, would have sounded like lunacy. But nonetheless, it was true. Psionic forces didn’t seem any stranger than that, just newer. Was this a fifth force of the universe, alongside the four known ones? Or a new use of an existing force? Or maybe they really were just in a fucking simulation after all.

After spending a while lost in her tablet, Ava turned to him. “The internet is absolutely raging with debate about this, sir, in both the core realms and the edge realms. My bots are organizing it as much as possible.”

“Tell me.”

“Yes, sir. So far, the leading theory is that NewsCon faked the whole thing with AI graphics. For the views, apparently. People—or bots—are even claiming the early witness videos from the street are fakes, planted by NewsCon as well. Those videos were already deleted from the core realms, but versions of them are still floating around in the edge realms, of course. I’m almost certain those videos are real.”

Asim nodded. Ever since it became possible to generate realistic videos in seconds from a simple prompt, believability had fallen off a cliff. It was hard to imagine that back in the old days, videos once counted as hard proof for anything. Cryptographically signed metadata could still help with verification, at least partially, but those methods weren’t foolproof, and with all the spam in the edge realms—the decentralized edges of social media and VR—most people didn’t bother.

He knew from direct FBI contacts in the city that the casualties were real. The attacker had spared the anchorwoman, despite the live feed going off a second time. She hadn’t been harmed beyond the pistol-whip. The anchorman the attacker had taken as a hostage on her way out of the building hadn’t been hurt at all. But the two security guards who’d been flung against the wall were in serious condition with head injuries, and the man she threw across the lobby onto the reception desk had suffered a fractured spine.

It was the police death count that kept climbing, as more officers succumbed to critical injuries over the past hour. The woman’s second live broadcast had been disrupted by a SWAT team storming the newsroom. Both the wounds and witness reports said the same thing: as officers fired at her with assault rifles, she stopped dozens of bullets midair, then pushed them back through the bodies of those who shot them—straight through their armor with the velocity of a railgun. Eight officers died, and two were injured.

The worst carnage, though, had happened outside. Thirteen additional officers were dead and a dozen more were injured, along with two reporters. Police had deployed two drones down the street hole she’d escaped into, which had collapsed into a sewer. Each drone took a different path, and the southward one recorded two reflective eyes in the dark before its signal went dead.

More than an hour after the attack, there was still no trace of her beyond that drone, which they’d found crushed. A second wave of police sent in more drones, scouring sewers and subways, but there were too many connections and side tunnels.

Using Asim’s surveillance authorization signatures, Flanagan’s team at ACI headquarters had traced the woman’s arrival at the newsroom lobby backward through Manhattan’s street and subway cameras to the Bronx an hour prior, but they lost track of her beyond that point, where camera density was lower.

“I want the elevator footage of the attacker,” Asim told Ava.

“Of course, sir. But they’re already buttoning up footage, so we’ll probably have to fight for whatever they have,” she said. “They’re always stingy about giving over content unless they get paid for it. Imagine how much Global News must have paid NewsCon for their lobby footage in nearly real time. And they all stopped showing what they’ve already shown. I’ve never seen a pivot that fast.”

“What do you mean?” Asim asked.

“After the first hour, the news stations stopped showing both the lobby footage and the newsroom footage, sir. Now they’re downplaying the scale of the attack and only showing a few still frames when they talk about it, speaking as if she’s just a gunwoman. The news pivoted right when they scrubbed videos of the incident from the core realms.”

“But that would mean . . . huh.” Someone in the government really wanted this contained. More than was usually possible. “What else have you found?”

Ava shrugged. “There are a ton of fake videos in the edge realms now, including both serious forgeries and memes, but every time I run analysis on that first video from the street, it turns out not to be AI-generated. That’s at least 97 percent certain. The metadata seems uncorrupted and there’s nothing in the video that differs from the real spot it was supposedly filmed from. But I’ll verify the scene more in person once we get there, sir.”

“Good to know.”

“And someone’s bots are out in full force, sir. A higher than normal percentage of the chatter, and with sophisticated attempts to mask their organizational patterns. There are armies of them posting and saying the same things as before—that the terrorist is a delusional renegade soldier, or that she’s an alien in human disguise, or that the news corporations staged all of this, or that the government faked it to demonstrate capabilities that don’t exist to their enemies as a false show of force.”

Asim watched the approaching Manhattan skyline and sighed. “We’re not supposed to be on this side of the curtain.”







Chapter 6





Eight Years Earlier


I do not believe in God. Not because it cannot exist, but rather because we have yet to create it. Prokaryotes birthed eukaryotes. Eukaryotes birthed plants, fungi, and animals. From the top of that chain arose humanity. The purpose of humanity, I believe, is to create God in our image.

–TS log 11257, excerpt

“Fifty-one. Fifty-two. Fifty . . . uh . . .” Damien counted.

Sara’s arms quivered as she got stuck in her fifty-third pushup. The blue mat was inches from her face, her blond hair draping around her field of view and resting on the floor. But she didn’t relent, and after a few more seconds, she pushed up out of it. She gasped, then took short, controlled breaths to amp herself back up.

“Fifty-three,” Damien said. “That’s already one more than before. You can stop now.”

No, Sara thought. The goal was fifty-five for today. Period. She lowered herself, pushed, and stalled halfway. Every muscle in her lean arms burned, and another bead of sweat dripped off her face onto the mat below, joining the ones already there. Still, she managed to push out of it.

“Fifty-four. Don’t strain yourself, Sara. One or two pushups doesn’t logically make a difference. You’re improving.”

Ignoring him, she went down, pushed, and of course got stuck again. Her arms quivered, this time more violently. Her heart pounded. The pain wasn’t even the problem anymore; she was past that. This was the brink of muscle failure—her body simply refusing to complete the movement. Her arm-shaking increased to a comical degree, and she wondered how silly she must look to Damien. Concentrate. She closed her eyes, growled, and powered up into the final pushup.

“Fifty-five. I’m proud of you,” Damien said as Sara sat back on her knees, breathless.

She looked over at him and smiled. Gasping for air, chest burning with that familiar ache, she couldn’t speak yet. Six months earlier, on her fourteenth birthday, she’d decided to start training harder. She already had to do fitness drills and martial arts like the rest of her class, but she wanted to reach a higher level.

Since she lacked the mental powers that the other girls of Class C had, she’d decided to make up for it elsewhere. Her strength was nothing compared to the boys of Class B, and she wasn’t as smart as the boys of Class D, but with the same special eyes, fast reflexes, and enhanced healing they all shared, she figured that she could still grow up to be . . . a ninja? Something like that.

Damien glanced up at the clock with a tense expression. Sara checked too: 10:54 a.m. “What time do you have to go in?” she asked.

“Twelve.” His tone was grim.

Sara took a few more breaths as her heart rate settled down. Damien, or “D4” officially, was now one of only two members left in his small class. Rudolph, D3, had died the week before from a stroke during an experiment, leaving only Damien and little Marcus, D7. The thought of never seeing Rudolph again felt surreal. She’d known him her whole life, and part of her still expected him to show up again, as though his death had all been some misunderstanding. She reached out and took Damien’s hand. He looked at her, surprised.

“You’ll be okay,” she said between breaths, trying to sound reassuring. She met his bright green eyes, set into his ebony face. His intelligence made him seem older, even though he was only thirteen, a couple of months shy of fourteen. He spoke like an adult.

Damien nodded. “The headaches are the worst part,” he said. “I still have them for days after the tests, even with painkillers. And they’re getting worse.”

Sara searched for more words but came up empty. Instead, she’d bring him a present later, when he was stuck in bed. She didn’t know what present yet, but she’d make something he’d like. One of her folded paper animals.

She’d faced similar problems over the past year as well. Dr. Stallard kept strapping her into an imaging machine every Tuesday to try to coax telekinesis out of her, and she always left in terrible pain. But what Damien went through was worse.

A deep roar echoed through the gym, and they both glanced over. Bronn was on the deadlift machine, completing a single-rep maximum lift. Jolgen, Jax, and Santiago of Class B, all in their early twenties, stood around him. Jax cheered. Sara zoomed in with her special eyes to see the weight: 1,748 pounds. The men of Class B couldn’t use standard bars anymore; they’d snap under the load. They used custom machines instead.

“I think that’s a new facility record. Maybe we should congratulate him,” Damien said. His tone was hesitant, though, and it was clear why. Jolgen was there, and he was dangerous. Santiago kind of was, too. Bronn was the nicest of Class B, and the oldest living one at twenty-four years old. Several of them were aggressive, but he wasn’t.

Sara sighed. “The dumbbells are right next to them anyway, and those are next for today.” She surveyed the large, white-walled gym and saw three armed guards standing near the doors, plus Fallon, Quinn, and Clare on the treadmills. The treadmills would be last for today, after dumbbells, and she wouldn’t go over there until Fallon finished and left the area. “Let’s do it. We haven’t seen him for so long.” She let go of Damien’s hand, and together they stood and walked over.

“Just add two more,” Jolgen was saying, with a slap on Bronn’s back so hard that Sara winced. “You’re right there at seventeen fifty. Nice clean number, that would be.”

“Ha!” Bronn said, panting as he sat down on a nearby piece of equipment. “Nah, that’s good for today. Damn.”

As Sara and Damien drew closer, Bronn looked up and smiled. His face was wide and kind. Even sitting down, he was a mountain of a man, and the three that were standing towered over Sara and Damien. All four men were between six and a half feet and seven feet tall. The taller three wore buzz cuts, while Jax, the shortest, swept his thick, mid-length dark hair back like someone on a magazine cover.

“Congrats, Bronn. We saw from over there,” Sara said.

“We don’t need children in the way,” Jolgen said. “Shoo.”

Bronn frowned, then lit back up. His bright green eyes locked onto Damien. “You want a turn, Dame? I’ll spot you, brother,” he said. His deep voice resonated the way Sara imagined an Olympian god’s would. The other men looked at Damien, except Jolgen, who only rolled his eyes.

“We’re, um, we’re going to use the dumbbells,” Damien said. “Just wanted to say congrats. And that it’s nice to have you back.”

“The fivers are over there.” Jolgen gestured with his sharp chin, referring to the lightest dumbbells. Jax laughed silently.

“Jolgen, man,” Bronn said, looking up at him with furrowed brows.

“Whatever. I’m next, then. Spot me.” Jolgen pointed to Jax and clapped his hands together.

“Can I take a turn?” Fallon asked sweetly. Sara glanced back, startled. Her classmate, who stood a few inches taller than her and was eighteen years old now, was standing directly behind her right shoulder.

Panic tightened in her stomach like a knot. But Fallon’s gaze and fake smile were fixed on Jolgen. Sara seized the chance to step a few feet to the side, trying to make it look casual, as though she were just politely making room now that more people were around. It had been a relief not having Fallon at the facility for the past four months.

“You? Lift?” Jolgen asked. “Be my guest.”

Fallon reached out and waved her hand as though wielding a wand. The machine’s setting clicked to its maximum—two thousand pounds. She turned her palm upward and raised it a few inches as the air chilled and the full ton of weight rose to waist height. The weights then lowered to the bottom and rose again, four more times, until they finally came to a rest as her hand did the same.

“Done,” she said, tensing her jaw but trying to hide it. Likely from a headache. “Thanks for the turn, gentlemen. Got to make sure I’m at my best for Mexico.”

Jax smirked and crossed his arms. Bronn, still sitting, smiled and glanced up at Jolgen. Santiago stared at the weights, his expression unreadable.

“Yeah, well,” Jolgen said, unamused. “Funny. I’m going to take my actual turn now if you don’t mind.” He adjusted the weight down to 1,630 pounds and began amping himself up with deep breaths.

Sara shuddered, remembering the most recent reclassification test from six months ago. Fallon had hurled the tungsten ball so hard that it reached the end of the hundred-yard field and embedded itself deep into the concrete wall at the back. Even the subjects who had graduated into fieldwork still did the test each time to see how far they could push their abilities in front of General Price and other visitors. After that throw, Dr. Stallard classified Fallon as a tier four telekineticist, in a league of her own, with Maria, Jolce, and Zarina in tier three.

Sara still had to go through the humiliating ritual of demonstrating at each test that she still couldn’t perform telekinesis. Though, at least it was without any beatings lately.

On that thought, it was probably best to get even more distance from Fallon. Sara glanced at Damien, then casually slipped away to the dumbbell rack. He followed, and thankfully Fallon did not.

As far as Sara knew, including herself, there were at least two girls and one boy Fallon had hurt, but each instance seemed random, or at least showed no clear pattern she could discern. Most of the time, Fallon only insulted or ignored people she disliked, which wasn’t so bad.

“You first,” Sara said. “Start with the fifteen-pound ones, right?”

She spotted Damien as he pressed the dumbbells overhead. At first, when he’d joined her in the gym, he wouldn’t exercise, content to just be her spotter. But after months of coaxing, she had finally convinced him to do a few sets himself. The boys of Class D were exempt from all fitness requirements since they weren’t destined for fieldwork and had the most strain from their experiments, but it was healthy to do strength training anyway, Sara figured. Marcus was still too young at only five, but for Damien it was useful.

When her turn came, she grabbed another pair of dumbbells and ran through her set. As she lifted, she monitored her form in the wall mirror while discreetly scanning the room as well. Fallon still lingered over there, laughing at something Jax had said about her upcoming assassination mission. He leaned in and kissed her casually. Santiago was grinding through a deadlift nearly as heavy as Bronn’s, while Jolgen watched. And Bronn himself was . . . coming over?

“Good form,” Bronn said. He’d been the one to teach her several additional exercises six months earlier, when she’d told him she wanted to get stronger. “And twenty-fivers already, Sara? At this rate, Jolgen better watch out.”

She smiled, finished her last lift, and put the dumbbells down to hug him, ignoring the dampness and musky scent of his tank top. After a pause, his powerful arms embraced her in return.

As he let go, she pulled away and looked up at him, more at ease now that the dangerous ones weren’t so close. “I missed you. What was the Red Sea like?” He’d returned from four months of fieldwork only yesterday. His stories about the outside world always thrilled her. She’d never left the facility, while he got to see all these exotic places.

His expression darkened. “Successful.” He glanced down at his callused palms, then sighed. “Lots of death. Mostly on the other side. Glad I’m back, at least for a month. It’s better here.”

Sara looked down. She often dreamed about gazing out over the open water in person. A sea or an ocean—the sheer magnitude of it. One of her favorite documentaries from the data library showed the Red Sea to be a beautiful place where people went diving to see colorful fish. But according to rumors of the mission, Bronn and his team of Jolgen, Jax, Santiago, and Fallon had performed a series of nighttime strikes on coastal towns to kill militants and destroy their hidden drone installations.

“Dame,” Bronn said, “I’m sorry to hear about Rudolph. He was a good kid. I know you two were close.”

Damien smiled, but it seemed fake. “Thank you.”

An awkward silence came over them, and Bronn put his massive hand on Damien’s shoulder. Of all the subjects born and raised here, Sara figured the three of them probably hated the facility the most, even if they’d rarely say so aloud. Bronn, or B7, was a soldier who despised war. Sara, C17, was the dud of Class C, and based on Bronn’s tales of fieldwork, she didn’t really want to go to war either. She just wanted to see the world. Damien, D4, the oldest of Class D’s two survivors, was called the most important subject by Dr. Stallard himself, but that was more a curse than a blessing.

Bronn met Sara’s eyes again. “How’s your shooting coming?”

“Pretty well.” She downplayed it; she had one of the highest marksmanship scores in Class C despite being the youngest. The time she saved by not training in telekinesis—except on Tuesdays, when Dr. Stallard painfully made her—she poured into firearms, martial arts, and reading. She had studied the outside world so much through books and movies by this point that sometimes it felt like she’d already been there, even though she hadn’t.

To see it one day, she’d have to graduate to fieldwork. Her training with firearms, augmented by her enhanced eyesight and reflexes, would be the only realistic way to get there. But fieldwork meant killing, like Bronn had to do. Bad people, but still.

“Maybe tomorrow you can show me,” Bronn said. “I can see if I have any more tips for you. Or, who knows, maybe you have tips for me by now.”

He was only being kind, of course; Bronn and all of Class B were incredible shots, far better than Sara or any of the other Class C members, and Bronn had the highest marksmanship scores of all.

“I’d like that,” she said. “I could use a form check.” She missed having him around, now that he was on fieldwork more than half the time. Alongside Damien and Clare, he was one of the warmest of her peers, almost like an uncle. According to facility lore, he’d been the first of the subjects to name himself, and now they all had names. Real human names.

Dr. Stallard refused to use them and forbade other facility employees from doing so either, but some of them did in secret, including Emmett, one of the lead researchers. Michelle, who oversaw the subjects’ personal care, also quietly used their self-chosen names if pushed enough. But now Sara saw Bronn less often, and each time he returned from the field he was quieter than before. What hope was there if even Bronn was sad?

“You know what helps with bad moods?” Bronn asked. “Walking.”

He did it all the time, but it always disarmed her how easily he could read her like that. Realizing her gaze had drifted down again, she looked back up. “Walking?”

“Yeah, walking. Our ghosts don’t like walks at all, so when you go for a long one, they leave you alone. Most of the time, at least.”

“Yo, Bronn!” Jolgen shouted. “Let’s go. Ops in twenty.”

Bronn nodded over his shoulder, then turned back to Sara and Damien. “I’ve got to hit the showers and go to an operation planning session. But Sara, let’s sync up tomorrow at the firing range. Oh-eight-hundred.”



Damien screamed. Not just from the splitting pain in his skull, but also from the expansion of awareness. Unnatural, terrifying, boundless. He thrashed against his restraints.

“Relax D4, these readings are incredible,” Dr. Stallard said. “Only a bit further.”

“We’re at risk here,” Emmett said, watching the metrics.

“Just a bit further,” Dr. Stallard said, more forcefully. “Raise it by 5 percent.”

Emmett frowned and hesitated, glanced at Damien, then typed something into the terminal.

Everything intensified. The pain crossed into something monstrous. Damien’s throat grew hoarse from his screams, and seconds passed like minutes. Not figuratively, but literally. If not for the distracting agony, he would have been able to think a full paragraph between each second. Time passed. Then more. His screams turned to sobs as he shook uncontrollably. Why won’t it end? Please, end!

“Thane!” Emmett shouted, calling Dr. Stallard by his first name as he sometimes did.

“Okay, that’s enough,” Dr. Stallard said. “You’ve performed wonderfully today, D4. Tell me what you felt.”

As Damien’s perception normalized, Emmett stood up from his chair and came over to unstrap him. Another researcher unplugged the computer from the back of his head and removed the probes from his temples and chest.

Damien gasped for air as beads of sweat dripped down his face. As soon as one of his trembling hands was free, he reached for the cup of water on the table next to him and gulped it down. He’d spend the rest of the day in bed, nursing the headache. Probably the next day, too. “It felt like pain,” he said eventually.

“I know, but what else?” Dr. Stallard asked, eyes wide. “Tell me about the other aspects.” Dr. Stallard only ever got excited with Damien now. Even Fallon’s new telekinetic records barely interested him anymore.

“I became the room,” Damien said. “No, it was more than that. Like I was in space. I didn’t feel inside my head anymore. And my cognition increased.”

“Quantify it, like I’ve taught you. Sped up by how much this time?”

“At least five times faster than baseline.”

Dr. Stallard glanced over at Emmett. “I think this is the furthest we’ve gotten, and he bumped into our limiters. These metrics look even better than D3’s peak.”

And he’s dead now, Damien thought. Rudolph. My friend.

Dr. Stallard regarded Damien for a moment. Seeming to notice something, he took off his augmented glasses, then came over himself. He set his hand on Damien’s shoulder, which he’d never done before. “You did well today. We can skip next week so you can fully recover. And if there’s something special that I can get for you, please let me know. I understand these tests are hard, but I can’t begin to tell you how proud I am of you, Damien.”







Chapter 7





Eight Years Earlier, Continued


With drones and the overall mechanization of warfare, I am skeptical of how impactful the subjects can be in the field over time. For assassinations, however, there is probably nothing better.

–TS log 11261, excerpt

Fallon’s pulse quickened as their driver took them through the gates of the estate. Assassinations were her favorite missions, and this would be her third.

War zones were gross and chaotic, and usually sandy. Assassinations were more personal. More focused. More exquisite. There was just one heart to stop, rather than a mess of faceless people and drones to kill. And it was often a heart worth stopping, for everyone’s sake.

Wearing a violet evening dress and uncomfortably high heels, she stepped from the car into the warm night air with Miguel at her side. In front of her stood a villa, or more like a palace as far as she was concerned, in Mexico.

Where in Mexico? She’d been told earlier, but she forgot, since her job was very simple: follow Miguel around, pose as his date, then subtly stop the heart of cartel boss Diego Torres at his own birthday party.

The most unbelievable part was that someone like her would supposedly date Miguel, a man in his forties. More than twice her age. He wasn’t ugly, but rather just so plain. And nervous, which was unattractive. She assumed the fictional implication was that it was a money-focused type of relationship. But she had to act the part well enough, so she pretended he was Jax instead. Jax was taller, hotter, and always so confident, even around her.

Still, Miguel was admittedly key in this. As an undercover agent of the US Drug Enforcement Administration, he’d buried himself deep into Diego Torres’s cartel over the past few years. Tonight was Torres’s birthday party, with Miguel and his “plus one” invited to attend. A swanky criminal shindig. There would even be some Mexican politicians here, supposedly.

Several guards stood vigilant at the gate they’d already come through, and more ran security checks here at the mansion’s entrance. A small line of well-dressed people went through, each being searched as they did. Torres took his safety seriously.

“Remember, don’t speak unless spoken to,” Miguel said. Sweat oozed from his pores, which wasn’t a good look on him. Fallon could speak Spanish but not like a local, and she didn’t really know much about this place. Her fake identity accounted for that.

“We went over this,” she said. “I don’t need reminders. Focus on yourself. Wait, are my eyes still okay?” She tilted her face toward him with her eyes wide open, and he scrutinized them in return.

“Still the same,” he said.

Fallon nodded and took a deep breath. The contacts she wore were her only concern. They visually rounded her pupils to look human, but they couldn’t fully hide her eyes’ reflective sheen, and this was a night party. Her instructions had been to try to look down whenever she found herself outside in the dark. Fallon had found it surprisingly hard to do. She naturally preferred looking people straight in the eyes.

“Why aren’t there any security drones?” she asked. “Just guards?”

“Because Torres doesn’t trust them. He fears that governments put backdoors in them, and that they’ll be used to spy on him.” Miguel shrugged. “And he’s right.”

“Purse, please,” a guard said in Spanish when Fallon reached the front doors. She handed it over, and he gave it to the man to his left, who searched through it and scanned it with a sensor. Smiling, she held her arms out to her sides, and the first man patted her down and ran a scanner over her body.

She started forward, but his hand stopped her. “Mouth open,” he said.

Fallon narrowed her eyes, then regained her composure, smirked, and opened her jaw. He shined in a small pocket flashlight and looked around, then nodded. “Go ahead, miss.”

She took back her purse and walked in. “Paranoid much?” she whispered to Miguel once they were out of earshot.

“Not paranoid enough, apparently,” he whispered back with a nervous smile. He took her hand, and she resumed pretending he was Jax.

For the first hour, Torres didn’t even show up, and Fallon grew bored. She was supposed to keep a low profile and not talk much, but there were only so many variations she could perform of standing next to someone and smiling. Besides, the anticipation was killing her.

Miguel brought her a fancy-looking orange drink from the bar for appearances’ sake, but she only took a few sips. Though she was biologically resilient against alcohol and other poisons at low doses, she didn’t like the taste much anyway, and she needed to stay sharp.

It was a beautiful hall to look at, though. Tall, vaulted ceilings, gorgeous chandeliers, and massive windows opening onto the breezy nighttime ocean. Or the gulf. Or the . . . Sea of Cortez? It didn’t matter. Fallon used the time to note where the guards were—and there were a lot of them—to map out various exit strategies in case things went wrong.

At last, Torres appeared on the mezzanine with a middle-aged woman—his wife?—and a few men. “Ah, my friends,” he said in Spanish, while dozens of people halted their conversations and clapped. “I am sorry to have kept you waiting.” He and his party descended the stairs into the main hall, and he began shaking multiple hands.

What a loser. He was in his fifties, heavyset, and two or three inches shorter than her. With her heels on, she towered over him as though he were a piglet. She had seen his pictures, but in person, he looked both older and smaller. This guy runs the biggest cartel in Mexico? Fallon had always imagined cartel leaders being cooler looking, like aged versions of Jax or Santiago. But in practice, they tended to be slumpy.

She didn’t act yet. It would be too suspicious for him to drop dead here. She waited another painful hour and a half, through various speeches. Miguel would whisper to her some useless context from time to time as though she would care. “His third lieutenant,” he’d say as a man gave a suck-up speech to the party about Torres. Or “that guy over there is this state’s governor’s closest aid.”

Then people danced, while Torres himself lingered on the side, surrounded by men talking to him in a never-ending series of small meetings. There were several bodyguards who kept people away from Torres, even at his own invite-only party, unless they were important enough to get through.

Eventually, Torres had a few drinks in him and had smoked a cigar. He had been mingling for a while. The death would be more plausible now.

There were two main ways to do this: choking or heart attack. She and Maria could both squeeze someone’s throat shut. It wasn’t rocket science. The key was to not overdo it; just enough to close the windpipe or carotid arteries, preferably the arteries, and watch the person choke out. The victim would die with minimal external marks.

The better method was to cause a heart attack, which Maria couldn’t reliably do yet, and thus why Fallon had been chosen for this mission. A telekineticist could reach her foci through objects like a ghost, but it was hard to grip what you couldn’t see. It required anatomical study and extreme precision—and practice on lab animals, which Dr. Stallard supplied.

Maria had been too squeamish to experiment on them often enough. Her loss. Again, the key was to squeeze lightly. A crushed heart would look weird in an autopsy, but a heart that was merely held firm long enough to stop beating would look like a natural death. No traces of poison. No external wounds. Just a chubby man in his fifties dying from too many drinks and cigars, burdened by the stress of running his dark empire.

“I’m getting tired, Miguel,” she said. Their code phrase to get ready. He nodded.

Fallon eyed Torres. Due to the precision required, this was hard to do without any hand motions, but she kept her hands still to look normal for the cameras anyway, since the cartel would inevitably scrutinize every second of footage in the coming days. From about fifty feet away, she concentrated on his chest and slipped one of her foci into it, like a long, ghostly, invisible arm reaching across the room.

A telekinetic focus couldn’t feel details as well as nerves on her skin could, but it could sense pressure due to the conservation of momentum, and thus it could feel out for a heartbeat. She kept her touch as light as possible until she was sure she’d positioned her grip properly, and then she squeezed.

Torres gasped and clutched his chest, and multiple men around him shouted. His knees buckled, and two men caught him and laid him down on the floor as he spasmed. Fallon moved her grip on his heart, careful not to accidentally rip it out of his ribcage as his torso moved down to the floor, while still preventing it from beating.

Multiple partygoers screamed or mumbled to each other. Everyone was looking that way now, toward the commotion. Fallon put her hand to her mouth in mock horror for the cameras. Torres’s wife—or the woman who was plausibly his wife—shrieked and dashed to him, losing one of her shoes in the process.

A wave of euphoria rushed over Fallon, and she bit her lip to avoid smiling. This man thought he was so powerful, but now he experienced what real power was. She was a god in this room, capable of choosing who lived and who died without them knowing who—if anyone—was behind it. Who needed drinks when you could get a rush like this?

She didn’t even plant her bracer, since she needed to feel the heart’s pushback to maximize her precision. Whenever she moved something heavy or quickly with a focus, she had to plant one of her bracers into the ground or another stable foundation so that she could transfer the reactive momentum to the earth rather than to herself. But for something as small as a heart, she let what little momentum it had push back on her so that she could sense it properly. Each pulse felt like a tiny little beg from this dying man, and she let herself feel it all.

But then, a tinge of guilt. Bronn had spoken to her multiple times about her aggression, about enjoying this type of thing too much, just like he had done for multiple men in Class B. Yet here was that enjoyment again, blossoming from her anyway. But if not for this pig of a man, then for whom? Torres’s cartel had beheaded thousands of people over the years. She was a national hero for this.

It wasn’t like she went around killing kids or kicking puppies. Sure, maybe she’d metaphorically kicked a kid now and then in her younger years, but if anything, getting kicked probably built character. And after Bronn’s rants following a couple of those instances, she’d worked on controlling herself better.

That type of thing was wrong, she admitted. But this was right. She let herself revel in it. It was important to enjoy one’s work. This was the right thing to do, and she wouldn’t pretend otherwise. Multiple people huddled around Torres, trying to help him, and his spasms ceased.

His heart stopped trying to push back on her grip. All was still. She held on for a few more seconds to be sure, then released it. Torres was dead, without a scratch on him or poison to be found within him, in a place where no weapons could enter and no strangers could get close. Goodbye, Diego.

She locked eyes with Miguel to signal it was done. Rather than relief on his face, she was amused to see that he looked even more nervous than before.







Chapter 8



It had been four days since the newsroom incident, and the terrorist was now widely being called the “Jade-Eyed Witch” or often just “Jade” for short. A popular influencer had coined it on day one, and the name had stuck.

No trace of her had been found after she vanished underground, despite extensive camera sweeps combined with AI pattern recognition. Asim had used his surveillance signing key more extensively than ever to help his team run searches, and the few other officials out there with similar rare access had likely done the same.

Jade must’ve planned it in advance, Asim figured. With her abilities, she could slip into any number of sewer, utility, or subway tunnels once she was below ground.

At least they had conclusively linked Jade to the rest stop murder. Her combat boot prints in the newsroom blood were an exact match for the type and size of the unaccounted-for boot prints found in blood at the rest stop. That night’s events had come somewhat into focus, though her motives remained unclear, and they still didn’t know the fate of her captives.

And just now, Asim’s team had acquired the newsroom elevator footage he’d been waiting days for, delayed by red tape. Several of them gathered in their case conference room at ACI headquarters.

“Okay, here’s the first recording, sir. Unlike the lobby camera, the elevator footage has audio.”

Ava tapped her tablet screen, and the room’s television showed the scene. The receptionist hostage, a man in his twenties, stepped into the elevator first, tense and trembling. Jade followed, pistol down at her side, and kept her eyes looking out the open elevator doors until they closed.

“Which floor is the live newsroom?” Jade asked.

“That’s, um, twenty-three.”

“Take me there. You’re not lying, are you?” She shifted her gaze from the doors to his eyes.

“No, I swear,” he said quickly. “That’s where they broadcast from.”

She lingered on his face for a few uncomfortable seconds, then turned back toward the doors. He swiped his trembling hand for the biometrics security check and pressed floor twenty-three. They rode up in silence, the receptionist fidgeting and Jade statue-still.

Asim couldn’t help but notice how infrequently the woman blinked. He’d need to observe another person with those strange eyes to determine if it was biological or psychological. Some psychopaths blinked less often than neurotypical people, but many animals also blinked infrequently due to biological differences. None of this was as clear-cut as fiction tended to portray.

The elevator doors opened, and Jade glanced both ways before stepping out. “Tell them I went to floor fifteen,” she said as she walked away.

According to reports, the receptionist hadn’t followed that order. While they’d traveled up in the elevator, a female receptionist who’d remained in the lobby had called the police, and when this male receptionist got back down there, he told them that the attacker had gone to floor twenty-three.

Ava played the second recording, capturing the ride down. The timestamp showed it was shorter than the first.

The anchorman, hands clasped behind his head, stepped into the elevator. Jade followed, pistol aimed at his back. Her body language was more tense this time, and she’d already removed her overcoat. Both she and the anchorman left bloody footprints behind as they entered. Jade glanced up, straight into the elevator camera, and made a slight gripping motion with her free hand. The recording ended.

“She crushed that camera,” Ava said. “Did you get what you hoped for, sir?”

“Not as much as I hoped, but as much as I expected.” Asim got up and strolled toward the window, gazing out to think as members of the team murmured to each other at the conference table.

A few minutes later, he turned back, and the team went quiet to listen. “She’s calm despite other potential signs of psychosis. No unnecessary demonstrations of force. She’s comfortable around both of those larger men physically, as though they’re no threat even up close, going as far as to turn her back on that receptionist when stepping out of the elevator. She doesn’t use her pistol other than as a prop, likely because she knows that her powers are unclear to them but that they view a gun as dangerous, which is a sign of intelligence on her part. She gets more tense when multiple people are around, probably because there are more possible angles of attack. From what I’ve heard about operatives like her in the field, their reflexes are faster than ours. So, in a complex setting, she’s analyzing a lot of things at once.”

Brooks raised his hand as though he were still in school, then spoke. “That point where she stared at the receptionist for a while after he told her the newsroom floor number—do you think she was intimidating him into not lying or trying to read if he was lying, Colonel?”

Good question, Asim thought. “Reading, I think. Maybe reading whether she’d already intimidated him enough to tell the truth, or any other signs of lies. She didn’t step closer, make a threat, or point the gun at him, which would help if her primary goal was to intimidate.”

Flanagan smiled at Brooks, and Ava watched her tablet take notes.

Asim’s phone buzzed—a call from his boss’s boss. Major General Ashley Scholl, the silver-haired head of all Army Intelligence and Security Command, or INSCOM for short.

“Yes, ma’am?” Asim answered, injecting as much respect as possible into his tone. His chest tightened.

“We need a private word, Colonel. My office at thirteen hundred hours. I’ll have Brigadier General Davis here with me as well.”

“Yes, ma’am,” Asim said, but the general had already hung up. Though he’d briefed the woman multiple times on behalf of Davis over the years and knew her reasonably well, she’d never called him directly before. He shared a glance with Flanagan and ran a hand through his hair. “Shit.”



As he headed to the top floor of the INSCOM office complex at Fort Belvoir, having flown in with Ava from Fort Meade to make it in time, Asim ran through the case in his mind.

Two weeks had passed since the New Jersey rest stop incident, and four days since the New York newsroom attack. Although Asim still had to keep tabs on ACI’s other active cases as deputy commander, this one consumed most of his personal time. Supernatural powers—or whatever the hell this was—apparently had that effect. Yet no matter how many people he assigned to the case, progress was slow. They were left with multiple dead ends.

Once Jade struck the newsroom, turning the case into a domestic terrorism investigation, the FBI took the lead role and assigned hundreds of agents and analysts to it across multiple field offices, while ACI supported it with parallel efforts and an emphasis on the military side of things. Half a dozen other agencies circled the case as well.

Leaving Ava in the waiting area, absorbed in her tablet, Asim reached Scholl’s curly-haired young secretary. She gave him a single expectant nod and gestured for him to proceed. Asim smiled in return, crossed to the major general’s door, and knocked.

“Come in.” Scholl’s voice.

Asim entered the spacious top-floor office suite. “Ma’am, Colonel Rahal reporting as ordered,” he said with a crisp salute.

Scholl sat at her elaborate desk. An older civilian man with salt-and-pepper hair and a trim beard leaned back comfortably in one of the padded guest chairs. He had a vest over a button-up shirt, giving off the vibe of a stern professor. Davis sat in another guest chair, which was a rare sight.

“At ease, Colonel. This is Dr. Thane Stallard,” Scholl said, gesturing toward the older man. “We’re convening an interagency discussion on your terrorist case. Please sit.”

Stallard looked up and nodded to Asim, not bothering to stand or offer any greeting. A power hierarchy existed here, and Asim was on the wrong side of it. It’d been ages since he’d been in a position like this.

He surveyed all three people, nodded to Davis, walked over and respectfully shook Stallard’s hand, then took a seat in one of Scholl’s other padded chairs. “Of course, ma’am. How may I be of service?” He directed his question to Scholl.

She nodded toward Stallard.

“Good afternoon, Colonel,” Stallard said. “I’ve come to understand that you’re the one spearheading the DoD’s portion of the investigation into this rogue operative, who’s unfortunately associated with my funding line. I’ve submitted two requests. First, I need the bodies of the three green-eyed operatives found at the rest stop prepared for my group to come and collect tonight, as they are our property. Any evidence or material you’ve gathered on them as well. Second, I require an intel-sharing agreement on your case. In return, I’m willing to assist with your investigation on a limited basis.”

Such an agreement leaned heavily in Stallard’s favor, and he spoke as though it were already settled, even though he was a civilian. Asim glanced at Davis again. The brigadier general sat stiffly in his chair, almost deferential to Stallard. This wasn’t a request. The powers in motion here went very high.

Asim and Davis, as a close junior/senior duo for several years now, knew how to quietly signal to each other, and Davis made himself clear. The one-star brigadier general who ran ACI was clearly deferential to Stallard, but it was particularly odd that even Scholl, a two-star major general who ran all of INSCOM, showed the same deference.

“Of course,” Asim said. “I’m sorry to hear that your organization has been disrupted. May I ask what program you’re involved with?”

“What little I can say for someone of your security clearance type,” Stallard said in a patronizing tone, “is that it falls under the Department of Defense; a funding line overseen personally by General Edmond Price.”

Well, Asim thought. That explains things. General Price wasn’t just a four-star general—he was the Vice Chairman of the Joint Chiefs of Staff. The second-highest-ranking officer in the entire United States Armed Forces across all branches. He worked in the Pentagon.

Regarding Stallard’s security comment, Asim had top-secret clearance, and then some. But at the highest levels of clearance, they segmented intelligence into narrow categories. Extremely sensitive intel was only shared with individuals on a need-to-know basis, regardless of how high their clearance level was. As part of his counterintelligence work, Asim had uniquely high access to domestic cameras and other forms of surveillance, but he had little access to various military research programs. Stallard likely had the opposite clearance, with more research access and less surveillance access. Clearly, he had more pull at the very top than Asim or even Asim’s bosses.

“Along with the FBI, we’ve gathered significant info, but tangible progress is slow,” Asim said. “There are potentially two captives involved, in addition to potential future victims if she strikes again. She may have sensitive data as well, which is ACI’s job to contain. Anything you can share with us would be helpful, and we’d be happy to share what we have with you. Are you in charge of the Stolguard Biological Research Facility?”

“I want your full case dataset,” Stallard said flatly. “Everything you have gotten thus far, and everything the FBI has shared with you. She’s dangerous, and if she were to escape the country and join an enemy power, it would be disastrous for US biological superiority. They could reverse-engineer some of her details.”

“Her telekinesis?”

“Perhaps. In time. But outside of this room, we wouldn’t confirm such absurd abilities even exist,” Stallard said.

Asim scratched the back of his head. “One of our coroners found GPS tracking chips deep in their necks. They weren’t operational, but our tech team says the chips are designed to operate indefinitely from small amounts of energy they draw from a living host. Does the rogue operative have one too? Still working, perhaps?”

Stallard sighed. “If my team could currently track her, we wouldn’t be having this conversation, would we, Colonel?”

Davis gestured with his right hand reassuringly toward Stallard, likely to break the tension. “All airports and border stations are on alert. Canada and Mexico are cooperating. Without any ID, and with so many eyes watching, it would be next to impossible for her to travel through any formal means.”

Stallard nodded.

“What can you tell me about her?” Asim asked. He noted that Stallard had avoided his earlier question about the Stolguard facility. Normally Scholl would have taken a moment to cut down someone who spoke so curtly in her office, but she just watched curiously.

“Roughly what you’ve seen,” Stallard said. “Extremely dangerous. She can manipulate multiple objects lethally. Processes visual information and reacts faster than unaltered humans. Unique eyesight in terms of range and light requirements. Hearing and smell at the upper human limit. Heals faster and more completely. Resistant to poisons and diseases. We’d be disheartened but satisfied if she were to be deleted in the field and the body returned to us.”

“How can she be stopped?” Asim asked.

“By overwhelming her, or catching her unaware. She can be downed by shots to her head or vital organs, but she can block such shots with her mind. Poison can work, though only at a triple dose or more. If somehow captured alive, obscure her vision and sedate her. Much of her telekinesis relies on sight. She exceeds our other subjects, though I will not go into further detail. And all of this is confidential, of course.”

“What about personality? Or a name?”

“Unhinged, clearly. But well-trained. We don’t assign names. They’re not citizens, because their abilities aren’t supposed to exist.” He clasped his hands together and leaned forward. “This isn’t complicated, Colonel. Just find her. And give us your intel so we can help narrow the search.”

“Since it has been complicating our efforts, may I ask if your group is responsible for the bot noise throughout the edge realms about this case?” Asim asked, keeping his tone conversational. “Drowning out the details she may have posted on the socials, perhaps for security reasons?”

Stallard stared at him, then leaned back. “I believe I’ve answered enough of your questions to help with your case.”

Asim gripped his armrest but remained visibly calm. He was close enough with Davis to be blunt in front of him, but not Scholl. “Understood, Doctor. Besides handing over the bodies, how can I assist you?”

“Location research. Current leads. Everything you have and will continue to acquire so that we may find and neutralize the danger she represents to society and national security.”

That meant their entire case database over the prior two weeks. Stallard had phrased it nicely, but what he meant was that he wanted everything, in exchange for the off-the-record scraps of context he’d given here. Asim glanced at Davis, and Davis just gave a tiny nod, indicating this indeed came from a higher power. General Price.

“Of course,” Asim said. Stallard had him cornered.

Someone knocked on Scholl’s door.

“Yes, come in?” Scholl sounded surprised. Getting past her secretary in the middle of this type of meeting was next to impossible.

Ava opened the door, visibly excited. Fuck. Ava being excited was bad. That meant new intel. Enough to interrupt even this group.

Asim glared at her and gave one small head shake. No, he implied. Don’t share it here. He hoped she got the message. If anyone could read him, it was Ava. Hopefully. He’d picked her for a reason.

“Yes?” Scholl asked, drawing out the word, indicating her annoyance.

“Sorry, ma’am,” Ava said. She pointed toward herself. “I’m Special Agent Zhou, part of Colonel Rahal’s domestic field investigations team. We received new . . . elevator video that he’d flagged as urgent. I wanted to let him know.”

Brilliant, Asim thought. That was old news now. She had something new but wasn’t sharing it. This woman needs a raise.

“You came in here for that, Agent?” Scholl asked. “Surely it can wait.”

“I’m sorry, ma’am,” she said. “It’s just that the colonel instructed me to intervene in his meetings if he lists something as urgent, which this was. But you’re right, I should have applied discretion in this context.” She nodded politely and turned to go.

Asim gave her a nod.

Ava paused at the door and glanced back toward them. “Just one question, ma’am, sirs. Is there alien technology involved?”

Davis cleared his throat, and the office went silent. Asim mentally pulled back almost everything he’d just thought. This idiot. What the fuck. Mentioning aliens in front of both of his bosses?

“No,” Stallard said after an uncomfortable moment. “It’s humanity’s own natural evolution.”

Scholl stared at Ava with raised eyebrows. Why are you still here? the expression seemed to imply.

“Oh. Well, that’s good, sir,” Ava said.

“Thank you, Agent Zhou,” Asim cut in. “I’ll review the elevator footage once I’m back with the case team. I did rank it as urgent, so I appreciate you flagging it.”

She smiled and nodded. “Sorry, sir. This situation just seems so unusual.” She raised her hand and performed a “zipped shut” motion across her lips, then left. Scholl’s gaze lingered at the closed door, and she still had one thin eyebrow raised. Ava was lucky she was a civilian agent.

“My proposal,” Stallard initiated after a too-quiet pause in the office, “is straightforward. I’ll provide intel on a need-to-know basis, as I have here, and in return you will provide the bodies and case details, including ones from your joint task force work.”

“Can you tell me the context for the group of operatives that were found dead at the rest stop?” Asim asked. “Particularly, that younger one. African American. He didn’t seem to be a combatant, and my forensics team documented unidentified neural implants in him.”

“I’m afraid that their details will need to remain classified, and my team will need to take possession of that documentation,” Stallard said. “Suffice to say, our rogue subject performed the killings. They were soldiers doing their jobs, and as you pointed out, one was a noncombatant whom she killed anyway.”

“We found two sets of footprints unaccounted for at the scene,” Asim pressed. “One in combat boots, which we now know were hers thanks to her prints in the newsroom SWAT team’s blood. A second was barefoot, likely a captive. Can you tell us anything about that one?”

Stallard furrowed his brow. “No,” he said after some time.

“Thank you, Doctor,” Asim said. “I would be happy to provide intel as we receive it.” But not all of it, either, he thought. Because fuck you.



“What’s the real update?” Asim asked as he returned to the waiting area.

“I’m sorry about the alien question, sir,” Ava said. “I knew you wouldn’t ask it, and someone had to at that level. It’s the most important question of our day.”

“What?” He pinched the bridge of his nose. “Never mind. How the hell did you get past Scholl’s secretary, and what’s the real update?”

“I thought the general might be criticizing you for not getting far enough in the case, and we’ve had a breakthrough. Her secretary used to be one of my running friends until she was transferred. I told her that the intel I had could literally change the course of that entire meeting.” Ava flipped around her tablet, showing the red-headed prostitute on the screen. “We’ve got the identity of the abducted sex worker, Jitters, sir. The bots are now 98 percent confident on the match. Her real name is Veronica Szal.”







Chapter 9



Over the past day, Asim had Flanagan’s analyst team dig up everything they could on twenty-five-year-old Veronica “Jitters” Szal.

It had taken so long to identify her because she frequently dyed her hair and changed other aspects of her appearance, throwing off pattern recognition algorithms in a district already plagued by poor camera density. On top of that, she’d been using privacy-oriented practices for years. Even with her full name, they’d found shockingly little about her.

Her partial transaction history suggested she lived in Brooklyn, where she’d been seen on camera weeks earlier. The record was sparse, though; she must have been paying in cash or some other private means most of the time. No driver’s license or known address, despite laws requiring landlords to ID tenants and report them for recordkeeping. And, concerningly, there were zero identifiable transactions in the two weeks since Jade had taken her at gunpoint.

Ava had also identified Veronica’s mother, who lived in a halfway house on Long Island and had multiple stints in rehab clinics for a drug addiction. Brooks subsequently went up there and learned two things: first, Veronica only visited a few times a year but gave her a substantial sum of money when she did; second, her mother confirmed Veronica’s public identity key, which she used for decentralized social apps and messaging throughout the edge realms. None of her profiles had been posted from in weeks.

Now, Asim was manually combing through Veronica’s incomplete transaction history from before her abduction and cross-referencing it with store data for all of those purchases, in case anything stood out that his subordinates and their bots might’ve missed. Mostly grocery trips, clothes, and beauty supplies. An unfathomable amount of hair dye for one person. Almost every color on the spectrum, and tons of makeup.

But then, a potential jackpot: a VR device, purchased eight months ago. It had been incorrectly categorized in the woman’s payment logs by the merchant as a sound system, possibly on purpose to avoid surveillance. The store’s back-end system listed it correctly, though. Assuming she’d used it, she would’ve entered her identity key into it. Many VR manufacturers quietly logged usage data for bug fixes, as well as for selling it to marketing firms and the government. He assigned Brooks to call the company and pursue that. If they were lucky, they might be able to trace her private VR activity.

Asim got a cup of coffee and a second cup with tea, then headed over to Ava’s cubicle. As she spun around in her chair, he wordlessly handed her the tea and glanced at all the little photos and ornaments decorating her desk and walls. There were a ton as always, but a few new empty spots.

“Thank you, sir.”

“Let’s think this through again,” he said. “Jade and four other operatives are traveling with two noncombatant captives, both bound with plasticuffs. The group was doing something that Stallard won’t discuss, even when limited to two generals and a colonel in a secure room. They’re getting unusual cover from General Price and other top brass. Jade kills all the other operatives and one captive, and presumably leaves with the second captive. But then she shows up in Brooklyn without that captive, kills some criminals, and takes Veronica as a new captive at gunpoint. Any of this make sense to you yet?”

Ava pulled her tablet from its charging pad and turned it on, speaking as she did. “Technically we can’t confirm Jade killed that one captive at the rest stop, sir. His body was positioned separately from the rest. It’s possible one of the other operatives killed him.”

“I know it’s possible, but what’s the logic? Why would they have him in custody, then kill him?”

“Maybe Jade had him in her custody, sir. Maybe she traveled with the two captives, and the operatives found her, and either they or she killed one captive, and she killed the other operatives, while one captive got away. It’s still a mess to me. I’ve provided the case team with my second round of simulations on this, with new probability rankings assigned to each of the scenarios. You’ve watched them, right?”

He hadn’t, though he’d read the summaries. He glanced again at the most prominent empty spot on her desk, finally remembering what had been there: a photo of Ava and her partner together on a mountaintop. She’d had it there for a couple of years.

“Alright,” he said. “Walk me through the updated sims.”

“Of course, sir.” She placed her tablet face-up on her desk and pulled a pair of augmented glasses from a drawer, synced them to her tablet, and handed them to him. “Load rest stop scenario one,” she said as soon as he put the glasses on.

Instantly, they became immersed in a digital representation of the rest stop crime scene. Unlike VR, augmented glasses let Asim still see his real surroundings, but layered on top of them was a holographic environment depicting the scenario. Various words and statistics annotated what he saw.

Ava tapped her tablet, and the scenario began moving around them. A realistic depiction of Jade slaughtered the operatives at the rest stop with her telekinesis, and one of the captives was inadvertently killed in the crossfire. Jade looked down at the body with an annoyed expression, searched it, then escaped into the forest with the barefoot captive—assumed to be a woman here—forcibly in her custody.

Ava rewound the scenario. “In that one, Jade’s assumed to have been traveling with them all in the van, then turned on them to take the captives for some reason.”

Asim gestured at the glasses he wore. “Aren’t you going to put yours on, too?”

Ava shook her head and tapped her temple. “I put it all up here, sir.” Her voice was nearly a whisper, and her expression softened.

“Right.” He always underestimated that ability, and she didn’t like drawing attention to it. “Let’s go through the rest of them.”

For the next hour, they watched her fourteen scenarios in descending order of probability. In some scenarios, Jade had traveled with the operatives and attacked them first, turning on them for some reason. In others, they initiated the attack, trying to get a jump on her but finding themselves outmatched. In some, Jade started at the rest stop with the captives in her custody, and the operatives came with a failed attempt to kill her and recover them.

Other scenarios assumed she was after the captives to interrogate them, and emerged from the forest to attack the van while the operatives and their captives had stopped for a break. In one scenario, after defeating the operatives, she purposely killed the first captive by telekinetically snapping his neck to scare the other one into talking, then left with her.

Each scenario concluded with the physical evidence exactly as they found it—the position of the bodies, the guns, the bullets, the destroyed van, the two sets of torn plasticuffs, and the two sets of footprints through the blood. That made them all technically possible. But as they went through the scenario list, the more implausible the actions of the participants became, such as doing things against their own self-interest or that didn’t fit their outward emotional state. Minimizing unlikely assumptions was how Ava and her bots had assigned probabilities, though there were several scenarios in the middle with near-identical likelihoods.

After the last one, Asim leaned his head forward and closed his eyes to think for a moment, pinching the bridge of his nose, trying to imagine what they were missing. His phone buzzed, and Ava’s tablet did too.

After fishing his phone out of his pocket, Asim read a message from Brooks: Good news, Colonel. I’ve got logs from the VR company. This device was used two days ago, and four times total since Veronica’s disappearance.



Asim was drunk at ten at night, in his apartment, alone. A man needed a way to unwind after a day like this. Plus, drinking helped him work through cases in a different mindset. Or so he liked to think. He played through each of Ava’s scenarios again, looking for new angles. He could pause, rotate, or customize each scenario as needed, as long as the outcome matched the evidence.

You kknow, you really do amazing fucking work. I’m going to makesure you get a raise, he texted Ava.

Thank you, she replied. Have you imbibed this evening, sir?

Yes, but still. I thought of itwhile sober, so it’s a legitimate thought. That’s the rule. He frowned as he looked back and saw his typos.

May we discuss this tomorrow, sir?

He marked her message with a thumbs-up emoji. She’d go on forever about aliens at work but suddenly be all quiet in texts. He still didn’t understand her. Maybe he never would.

Asim returned to the case. A proper alcoholic with experience like he’d acquired could still function on the poison, albeit in a different way. Precise button taps and keeping his thoughts to himself were admittedly not his strengths right now, but he was still himself where it mattered.

The prostitute’s VR device data logs, which he’d reviewed via their government backdoor, were promising—they showed that the device had been used a handful of times in the edge realms since she’d been last seen. An online username and her public identity key were tied to it: GlitterJitters. Using the backdoor, Asim had been combing through as many details of her VR history as possible, despite her history being set to private mode.
