However, the digital address logs were scrambled. She’d used a data scrambler, masking her physical access location. They had an alert set up to notify them the next time the device went live.

Thinking about it made him want to go into virtual reality himself, so he went to his VR room and slipped in. The best part about a fake beach was that you didn’t have to worry about drowning or sunburn, even when drunk.

An hour later, floating in the crystal-blue water, he got a notification from Brooks. Asim swiped into the sky, changing it to his phone background.

Veronica’s VR device just went active again! Sending you the details now, Colonel, the message read.

His phone rang moments later. It was Ava. He swiped up and answered.

“You saw?” he asked.

“Of course, sir. Brooks is my minion. Should we move forward with the plan in your, um, current state, sir? Or should I go in your place?”

Asim took a deep breath and focused. No. The best part about drinking frequently was that you could force yourself to be sober enough if necessary, thanks to experience. Bad experience, but still. “It’s been an hour. I’m fine now. Sorry. We’ll stick with the existing plan. I’ll report back in a bit.”

“Yes, sir.” She sounded relieved.

After ending the call, he searched for GlitterJitters in his console with his government backdoor enabled to bypass her device’s privacy settings, and found her in Opera, Space Background, Moderate Population Density, Room 3. It required either a high reputation score or a high fee to enter, much like Asim’s favorite beach spot, to keep out spammers and trolls that would otherwise ruin any fine place in the edge realms.

Guess I’m going to the opera tonight. He pulled up his avatar menu and changed from beachwear to evening wear. He hated wearing formal attire in real life but had almost unlimited suit options in VR. He went with the gray-and-black combination for this one. Black dress shirt, no tie, with a gray Armani suit over it. Hair settings: slicked back for the occasion. Beard: short and well-trimmed.

With his complexion and outfit together, he had that modern Persian Gulf prince oil baron look, which was the vibe he was aiming for given the setting. Tall, dark, and handsome, he liked to think. Tiny streaks of premature gray at his temples. Seasoned.

He stepped into the opera.

Beauty filled every inch of the room, and he felt a moment of embarrassment for not branching out more. He spent too much time at the same beach and not enough time exploring places like this. It reached six stories high, consisting of multiple balcony levels, with intricate black-and-gold seats, and the ceiling dazzled with lights and lasers that ebbed and flowed in sync with the singer’s voice. But the stage was next-level awe-inspiring. Behind the singer, enormous floor-to-ceiling windows looked out into space, with Saturn close by.

To find GlitterJitters, he switched on his device’s information mode and scanned the room. And there she was. A woman sitting on the third-floor balcony, front row, off to the right side of the stage. He zoomed in. Wavy, ruby-red hair, light skin, voluptuous figure, wearing a sleek black dress. Gorgeous.

People’s avatars could look like anything, subject to certain rules for each type of room. But hers matched the pictures he had on file of Veronica Szal, albeit a bit more polished. Hair a little more voluminous and in perfect place, complexion and makeup more flawless than would be achievable in real life, but it was her.

Nobody sat next to her, on either side. The term “Moderate Population Density” in this room’s title meant that enough people were able to attend it and make it into a shared social experience, but it wouldn’t be so crowded that every seat would be full. Unlike a real opera house, they could expand the number of rooms as needed.

He cracked his neck and focused. The alcohol’s buzz lingered a bit, and the scent of champagne diffused throughout the opera wasn’t helping, but he was mostly back to his right frame of mind. He was Colonel Rahal, not drunk Asim. Let’s go have a chat.

He climbed the stairs, strode over to her row, and smiled. “Mind if I sit here?”

“There are plenty of other seats,” GlitterJitters said in a colder voice than he expected. She didn’t bother to look up, just kept staring at the stage.

“Ah, but these seats are in my favorite spot. I come here often. I won’t be a burden, miss, I promise.”

She glanced up at him for a moment, then back toward the stage again.

He took that as a sign of acceptance, whether she meant it that way or not, and lowered himself into the chair next to her. She sat with one leg crossed over the other, wearing strappy silver heels that matched her silver jewelry, and was intensely focused on the performance. Unblinking.

“You come here often, Veronica?” he asked.

Her body tensed, and she slowly turned to look at him.

The expression on her face hardened—and she still hadn’t blinked. A chill ran up his spine.

“I’m . . . not talking to Veronica, am I?” Asim asked. “You’re using her device, and her avatar.”

She studied his face, glanced down at his attire, then up at his face again. Finally, she blinked. She turned away and reached out her hand, swiping in front of her with a finger on a menu only she could see. She was about to disengage and leave the room. Veronica was likely dead, then. Her digital avatar stolen by her killer.

“Jade,” he said. “Why leave? Tell me what you tried to say in the newsroom. You wanted to get something out there, right?”

Her finger paused, and she turned to look back at him with narrowed eyes. “Who are you?”

“I’m someone who wants to know what’s going on.”

“That’s not an answer.”

“Consider me a detective. My name’s Asim.”

“You’re probably working for Stallard.”

“No. But I know you used to.”

She lowered her hand from the menu, and the corners of her lips curled up into a smile that didn’t reach her eyes.

“What I warned about is true,” she said. “The Stolguard facility is making mutants. Mostly for military strikes and assassinations, including illegal domestic ones. But despite me naming them on air, nothing’s been done. And inside, Stallard crafts a dark god.”

Okay, he thought. “What’s that supposed to mean?”

“A blend of human and machine, and malicious. It’s been disrupted for now, but will re-emerge.” She shook her head. “It’ll be catastrophic if it does. Worse than you can imagine.”

“Why’d you kill so many cops at NewsCon?”

Her jaw tightened. “They shot at me. Arrived too fast.”

“They were trying to save lives. You publicly threatened civilian hostages.”

“To warn of what’s coming.”

“Some more emails and social posts could have done that, don’t you think?”

“They were ignored. Drowned out. I had to show people directly.”

“So, is Veronica dead, too? And that other captive you had?”

“I still think you work for Stallard. Why should I tell you anything you could use to track me?”

He flipped his right hand over, palm upward for emphasis. “I just want to help people. That’s the truth.”

Her eyes lingered on him. Unblinking. Thinking. “I don’t kill for sport,” she said. “And your so-called Veronica doesn’t need your help anymore.”

“That’s not an answer to my question,” Asim said.

“I don’t owe you answers, since you’ll just use them against me. You’re probably trying to trace my location as we speak. I’ve told you of the real threat.”

“The dark god?”

She nodded, looking grim.

“What are you going to do next?” he asked.

Jade scoffed. “What are you going to do next?” she asked. “The fact that none of you are doing anything is why I have to do what comes next.”

She swiped and logged off, disappearing from her seat.







Chapter 10





Six Years Earlier


So many people of this generation have become distracted by AI, as if under a spell. Yet, carbon is inherently a denser substrate for information processing than silicon. Augmenting what nature has already forged over eons, I have long since come to realize, is a superior path forward than trying to recreate it clumsily from scratch.

–TS log 11672, excerpt

“Please, let me out,” Sara said to Emmett. It was her sixteenth birthday.

Dr. Stallard responded instead. “We just started, C17. Try again to lift the ball.”

She was surrounded by imaging equipment and strapped to a laboratory chair, just like she was every Tuesday. It was hard to remember how many Tuesdays it’d been since she’d last spoken directly to Dr. Stallard. She didn’t even like to look at him, but in every other direction, sensors and blinding lights crowded an already too-white room.

With a sigh, she tried for the fifth time to mentally lift the tennis ball. Her heart pounded from the strain. She could grip it with her foci, but trying to lift it still felt like picking up weights that were too heavy. Except instead of hurting her arm, it hurt her head. She gritted her teeth and growled as she pulled as hard as possible, trying to channel the mindset she used for pushups. For a few seconds, the ball rose an inch off the table and wobbled, but then her grip gave out, letting it bounce back down and roll off to the floor.

Her vision blurred from the pain in her forehead. Each pulse throbbed like it could burst into a hemorrhage, which had killed subjects in the past. She instinctively tried to lean her head forward, but the neck strap held it still. A renewed sense of claustrophobia surged through her, then turned into a wave of nausea.

“See, this is progress. That’s the best movement you’ve done yet,” Dr. Stallard said. “And we recorded excellent data, I think.”

Emmett, Dr. Stallard’s middle-aged lead researcher with brown hair and kind blue eyes, walked over and replaced the tennis ball on the table. Then he came over to Sara and held a cup of water with a straw up to her lips. Between ragged breaths, she took a sip. When she finished, he met her eyes and gave a subtle nod and a wink.

She couldn’t help but smile back, despite her pain. Emmett had always treated her well, ever since he’d joined the facility over a decade ago, taking over some of the research for Dr. Stallard. He’d been here since her earliest memories.

“Since that lift was so productive, we’ll just do one more,” Dr. Stallard said. “See? When you make progress, this can go more quickly.”

“I think we have enough data to work with for the week, Doctor,” Emmett said. “I’m not sure what we’ll gain from another lift.”

Dr. Stallard turned toward him, his blank face unreadable. “We’ll gain another lift’s worth of data. I believe we’re nearing an understanding of the problem.”

“Thane, I really must push back on this. Look at her.” Emmett pointed in her direction. “We’ve already crossed a line.” He was probably referring to the tears on her cheeks. At least she’d kept them silent.

Dr. Stallard—or Thane, as Emmett often called him—glanced over to the observation room, where two more researchers and Michelle, the facility’s head of subject well-being, watched through the glass. Raphael, the goateed head of security, loomed behind them with a laser rifle and two other guards.

“Everything in here is being recorded, Emmett,” Dr. Stallard said. “Come with me.”

The two men got up and strode toward the observation room, shutting the door behind them, with Emmett looking back at her as it closed. They left Sara alone in the chilly testing room, but she saw them talking behind the glass.

Emmett looked more animated than she’d ever seen before, gesturing first at Dr. Stallard, then at her. Even though no sounds reached her through the observation window, she could tell he’d raised his voice. The other two researchers exchanged uneasy looks and took a few steps back. Dr. Stallard put his hands on his hips and spoke for a bit, and after some more back-and-forth, Emmett nodded and left. Dr. Stallard said something to Michelle and left as well.

Michelle entered and began undoing Sara’s various restraints. “You’re finished for today, C17,” she said with a fake smile. Her straw-colored hair and long earrings dangled on either side of her face. “We’ll see you next week, sweetie. Please get some rest, okay? Your well-being is our top priority.”



Damien moved his bishop three spaces forward and took one of Dr. Stallard’s pawns.

The doctor studied the board for a time, then captured the bishop with his knight. He looked up at Damien for an extended moment, as if trying to read his intentions. Damien had sacrificed a stronger piece but had loosened up his opponent’s defenses.

“You’re getting very good at this, Damien.”

They sat in a grand office, surrounded by mahogany furniture and endless bookshelves. Notes of sandalwood and myrrh scented the air, in contrast to the sterile smell that suffused the rest of the facility, and orange rays of sunset gleamed in through tall, arched windows. As far as Damien knew, no other subject had ever been invited to Dr. Stallard’s personal quarters up here on the fourth floor, and yet he was summoned regularly now.

Damien advanced his queen, making use of the opening he’d created. “Thank you.”

Since childhood, Damien and Rudolph had been taught to play chess and other strategy games by the research staff. By age ten, they could beat everyone they played against, except for Emmett. It wasn’t until about age thirteen, around the time that Rudolph had died, that Damien could beat Emmett regularly. By then, he could easily dismantle several of the more junior researchers in simultaneous games.

Dr. Stallard himself began playing against him last year. At only fifteen, Damien now won about half the time. The man seemed genuinely thrilled with the challenge.

Damien would have traded it all away just to play with Rudolph again.

“How is C17 doing?” Dr. Stallard asked. He examined the board, thought for a second, and castled.

Damien looked up, not bothering to mask his skepticism. Dr. Stallard didn’t care about Sara, or “C17” as he called her. This morning’s experiment with her had gone horribly, and last Damien had seen, she was curled up in her bed. If she died tomorrow, Dr. Stallard wouldn’t lose a night’s sleep over it. He only asked in order to appear personable, to humor him.

“Why don’t you call any of the others by their names?” Damien asked.

“I do. Hers is C17.”

Damien leaned back in the luxurious chair and folded his arms.

Dr. Stallard sighed. It brought out his age when he did that. He looked to be in his fifties with his salt-and-pepper hair and beard, though according to other researchers he was around seventy. People in his station regularly lived more than a century.

“Look, Damien,” Dr. Stallard said. “I know some of your fellow subjects assume I use numbers for negative reasons, but that’s not the case. It’s not that I consider them to be lesser; it’s that I see them as greater. They have extraordinary potential, and they should keep that in mind. Detached. Mission-focused. Protectors of the nation. With the abilities they have, we see from subjects like C5 the problems that occur when their egos get too big.” He was referring to Fallon. “That’s why I made Class C female in the first place; I didn’t want the aggression problems that Class B exhibited in the hands of subjects who could kill with their minds.”

“Then why call me Damien? By that logic, am I the least of the subjects?”

Dr. Stallard shook his head. “Of course not. I call you Damien because I know you want me to, and you won’t be entering fieldwork like the rest of them. I consider you a colleague now. The others are weapons. Special forces soldiers face immense sacrifice and strain from their work. Imagine if we could solely use subjects who are built for it, physically and mentally, relieving others of that burden. But you and I—we’re exploring the bounds of human potential together.”

Damien purposely raised his eyebrows to indicate incredulity.

Dr. Stallard ignored it. “Besides, Emmett keeps using their chosen names behind my back, despite me repeatedly discouraging it. And he rubs off on others. Is that not enough? Coddling is Michelle’s job, not mine. She keeps them occupied with books, movies, games, instruments, therapy, rewards, and whatever else they need to be well-adjusted and sufficiently motivated for fieldwork.”

Damien frowned. All of this greater/lesser stuff was bullshit, and Dr. Stallard knew it. The only one he called by their chosen name was Damien, clearly his favorite subject by a wide margin. What was the real reason for the numbers? Was it to dehumanize the rest of them into being effective soldiers, even during ethically questionable missions? Or was it simply to shield his own conscience and maintain distance, given how many of the subjects died young?

“Half the reason that Fallon is the way she is,” Damien said, “is because she’s frustrated by your lack of approval. She’s basically an orphan trained to kill, and it’s obvious that she wants your recognition as a father. So do several others.”

“Well, maybe I would approve of her more if she caused fewer problems. I don’t have behavioral issues with the rest of Class C, and therapy falls under Michelle’s purview,” Dr. Stallard said. “And they’re not my children,” he added belatedly.

Damien brought out his knight. Dr. Michelle Verdant was the latest in a series of people to oversee subject well-being, including therapy and personal care practices for the facility’s subjects. Many subjects viewed Dr. Stallard as their father in one way or another, but nobody viewed Michelle as anything but a random employee here, since most of them had already been teenagers by the time she joined.

“So, back to my prior question before this unnecessary tangent. How is C17?” Dr. Stallard moved one of his pawns forward. “She literally won’t speak to me during tests anymore. From how often you’ve brought her up during our games, I assume you two are close.”

“She hates the tests you make her do every week,” Damien said, sliding up one of his pawns in response. “She’s suffering because of them. And she’s told you this.”

“I’m just trying to unlock her abilities. She would benefit as well.”

“Why do you care? You had seventeen girls in Class C, thirteen of whom are still alive, and twelve of those perform as expected—or better than expected, in Fallon’s case. Sara can enter fieldwork as a sniper or in some similar role.” He almost smiled but caught himself. Sara liked to say she would enter fieldwork as a ninja.

“I care because I want to know what went wrong,” Dr. Stallard said. “So that I can avoid the problem when I make the second class of telekineticists. Since C17’s limitation relates to telekinetic power, studying her helps us understand the nuances of brain structure as it relates to power specifically, rather than other aspects like precision. At least with the four who died, I could dissect their brains. We can see much finer detail there, with slices of tissue under a microscope, than we can from external imaging. As long as C17 lives, the best I can do is test her in a variety of ways while observing her brain externally. Check.”

As he spoke, he moved his bishop into an aggressive stance, threatening Damien’s king. Dr. Stallard had barely even looked at the board before making that move; he’d been too busy talking.

Damien took a moment to think. Was that a subtle threat to Sara’s life? Dr. Stallard couldn’t overtly threaten him or his friends, since his Class D tests greatly benefited from Damien cooperating with him and reporting his internal experiences properly. But would the doctor place a subtle threat there in the background to keep Damien engaged, with plausible deniability if challenged on it? The subjects were all motivated with extra privileges if they performed their duties well, but behind that façade was also the unspoken threat that they could be put down like Morgan, C2, had been if they caused enough trouble or refused to do their jobs.

Damien captured the attacking bishop with his knight. That bishop move seemed to have been a misplay on Dr. Stallard’s part. The man had been distracted.

“Doesn’t any of this bother you?” Damien asked. “When you’re alone, looking in the mirror? Don’t you second-guess any of what goes on here?”

Dr. Stallard chuckled. “Any good scientist second-guesses everything. It’s part of the profession.” What little mirth that’d appeared in his eyes vanished as quickly as it came, and he leaned in with a piercing stare. “But our feelings are still tuned toward the paleolithic era. As valuable as they are at times, it doesn’t benefit us to be slavish to them. That’s what logic is for. We already acknowledge the benefit of labor specialization. We already breed canines for specific tasks, including breeds built for violence, and the bulk of society is fine with that.”

He tapped the board to highlight the point. “The red line drawn around the genetic specialization of humans is arbitrary. It’s holding us back. Once this science matures, humanity can move beyond these friction points. People could be specialized for certain types of work, both in ability and in motivation. Productivity will soar and societal dissatisfaction will fall, as people will finally be adapted to the current era rather than for conditions that no longer exist.”

It was a well-polished monologue, and he must’ve used it countless times in front of generals and other VIPs to give them the assurances they needed. “So, what of agency?” Damien asked. “You choose how to spend your life. Does others having that freedom mean nothing to you?”

“How is this process less free than nature?” Dr. Stallard asked. “Do people decide to be gifted versus slow, or tall versus short, or even motivated versus apathetic? Nature rolls the dice. Some parents already tweak the genes of their offspring to filter out problems and amplify desirable traits. Is it categorically different for institutions to do this as well, and to take the process to the next level?”

Dr. Stallard took his glasses off, placed them on the table, and continued. “Look around this facility, Damien. Is it a house of horrors? Do you see abject suffering? I would assume C17 is in her room right now, with all the material comforts she needs, and surrounded by her little books and trinkets. Not exactly dystopian, is it?” He gestured dismissively with his hand. “Now, obviously, some people would object to what we do here, but those same people would take issue with every advancement in science that has brought us this far. I’d argue that any harm we unintentionally cause around the margins is minimal compared to the rapid gains we’re making for future society at large. Would you not agree?”

Damien sighed. It was next to impossible to argue with someone if their starting axioms were too far from your own. This direction would take days’ worth of debate to sort through. There was no choice but to cut around it. “I have a deal to propose. May I?”

Dr. Stallard leaned back, raised his eyebrows, and nodded.

“Back when you first used my name,” Damien said, “you offered to get me something special after one of our tests, and I never took you up on that, until now. So, here it is. I’ll keep doing experiments with you, and give it my all, if you stop doing experiments with Sara. Her telekinesis just doesn’t work, and that’s fine. She’s still useful for fieldwork with her other enhanced abilities. Her scores in other areas are great. Let her graduate and focus on that. At minimum, she’ll make a fine soldier.”

Dr. Stallard stared blankly and furrowed his brow. “That’s not much of a deal, Damien. You would get something you want, while I wouldn’t gain anything. Her value to the military is minimal.”

“But you won’t lose anything. You’d have fulfilled your promise. Instead, I would owe you gratitude and my hard work. That’s my offer, as a colleague.”

Dr. Stallard eyed him and rubbed his beard. “I’m a man of my word, and willing to give you a special thing as promised. I’ve not forgotten about that. But from what I can tell, your experiments are more painful than hers. This all seems a bit melodramatic, does it not?”

“The difference is that the experiments on me have a bigger purpose. Hers don’t. She gets the physical pain, along with the emotional anguish of repeated failure, week after week, with little to show for it. One of these days it’s going to kill her if you keep it up, and you know that. Whereas in the work you and I do, we’re actually getting somewhere.”

That much was true, though Damien pretended as though he liked where they were getting. The best lies had some truth in them. Dr. Stallard’s goal for Class D, of which Damien was the oldest remaining subject—followed by little Marcus at only seven years old—was to merge the mind of a human with a supercomputer.

Decades of AI development had been incredibly productive, transforming nearly every aspect of society. But they’d still run into performance ceilings, and there was growing scientific interest in expanding human potential rather than merely relying on machines. Dr. Stallard’s thesis was that the merging of a human mind, with its inexplicable consciousness into a supercomputer, with its orders of magnitude more linear processing capability, was the path to reach true superintelligence.

Class D’s brains had been genetically modified so that they could plug into a supercomputer with more access to their neurons than a normal brain, and Damien had a deep metal plug in the back of his head, hidden under his short black curls, that connected to millions of his neurons via microscopic electrodes.

“You make a fair point,” Dr. Stallard said, looking back down at the chessboard as he spoke. “The experiments are on different levels. I don’t even truly care for the military work. Drones are a better value than so-called super-soldiers for most combat operations anyway. After Class A was a failure—and they were more like you than like the others—I couldn’t continue funding that type of work for a while. I had to pivot to the more tangible super-soldiers of Class B and C, so that the late General Stolguard and now General Price would give me the funding I needed to create you. And some of the early brain work we did with Class C gave us the foundations for what you are today. If we’re successful with you, it’ll render everything else nearly irrelevant.” After a brief pause, he moved his queen forward. “That will be checkmate in two.”

Damien scanned the board for options, but he’d been outmaneuvered. How had he not seen this coming? Damien had underestimated his opponent—Dr. Stallard had merely feigned distraction in the prior move, setting a trap. When Damien had moved his knight to capture the bishop, it left an opening for Dr. Stallard to push his queen deep into his back line. It turned out that he had been the distracted one, rather than the doctor.

But Damien chose not to show that on his face. Instead, he met the man’s eyes and smiled. “Thane, I haven’t lost to you for real in over two months. I purposely throw half the games so we can still play together.”

A lie, of course. Whenever he lost, it was for real. But Dr. Stallard wouldn’t necessarily know that, thanks to the speed at which Damien’s skill had grown. He used the man’s first name as a little extra touch to disarm him. Dr. Stallard would hopefully think twice before risking things with him in the future.

For once, the doctor’s mouth went agape, but then he recovered. “Very well, Damien. You have a deal. I won’t test C17’s telekinesis anymore, and she can graduate to the field as she is, if any of the officers can make use of her. You and I will continue our work.”

“Thank you, Doctor. If I may, there’s one more thing. I don’t want her to know it was me who got her out of the experiments. Just make her think that you’ve learned what you can.”

“As you wish.”

Damien would likely stop getting invitations to Dr. Stallard’s office for games after that, he knew, and the experiments would get worse. But it was worth it for Sara.







Chapter 11





Six Years Earlier, Continued


Fortunately, most of the aggression among the subjects has been controlled. There are now only a few that remain problematic in that sense. We have enjoyed a good stretch without reported incidents.

–TS log 11674, excerpt

Sara unloaded her pistol at the target in the firing range, as she did every morning. She had been given plenty of firearms training over the years, but it was Bronn who’d helped her reach the next level. He had shown her breathing exercises to do and, most importantly, told her to go slow.

“Everyone always wants to shoot fast,” he would say with his impossibly resonant tone. “Take a second to aim. Doesn’t matter if you shoot first if you miss.”

Most members of Class C didn’t take marksmanship too seriously, since it was difficult to aim a gun and use telekinesis at the same time, for the same reason it was easier to use telekinesis with hand movements than without them. For a telekineticist, using her foci felt much like using her own arms, and so it was hard—though not impossible—to precisely use her arms and foci separately.

Guns were considered backup weapons for the rest of Class C, but for Sara, they were all she had. She wanted to be as accurate as the Class B subjects, and if what Michelle had told her was true, she might be going to the field after the next reclassification test. The outside world.

Last week, Dr. Stallard had apparently decided to stop testing her telekinesis and let her graduate, since she already met the other requirements. He didn’t even bother to tell her himself; instead, he had filtered it through Michelle, which was fine with her.

She’d always wanted to go on missions to see the world beyond this terrible place, but now that the time might be coming, she questioned herself. She regarded the target, in the shape of a head and torso, with seventeen holes in it. Could she shoot a real person?

When her fellow test subjects went on field missions, it usually meant killing people in other countries. She wished she could leave this place without getting into that work, like in the movies where people could go wherever they wanted. It seemed like such a dream—the idea of being able to stand there, a free woman, and just gaze out over the sea.

She peeked over at Maria and Jax, who were also in the firing range. Maria, twenty-one years old now, practiced with her pistol, while Jax, a few years older, tested one of those new Rionz laser rifles that Raphael liked.

Sara found herself wondering if she could go on the types of missions that Jax did. As brutal as those were, Maria and Fallon’s were arguably worse. They performed assassinations, including domestic ones on corporate or criminal targets. Both women could enter a heavily guarded event, make it through all the weapon scanners, get visual contact with a high-value target, then choke them to death or induce a heart attack, and slip away without anyone linking it to them. Jolce and Zarina were training for that too now.

“What?” Maria asked.

Oops. Sara had been staring, lost in thought. “Nothing, just watching you shoot,” she said. “Sorry.”

Maria studied her for a moment, then reloaded her pistol without another word. The sound of Jax’s laser rifle filled the range.

“This charging time is horrible,” Jax said to no one in particular. “And zero kickback. Feels unnatural, you know?” He was Fallon’s boyfriend, and though he could be a jerk sometimes, he never went out of his way to cause Sara trouble.

“I haven’t tried one yet,” Sara said.

“Don’t bother.” Jax raised the rifle up to his shoulder again, aiming another shot. “Not sure what Raphael sees in them.”

As Jax fired, she wondered what he and Fallon even saw in each other. They were so different. Subjects weren’t supposed to date, but it had been a known secret that those two had been doing so for years, and other subjects sometimes had quiet dalliances as well.

Raphael’s security team looking the other way on minor infractions was an unspoken perk for those who’d completed missions and earned privileged status. Jax was one of the most handsome of the men, with slicked-back dark hair and olive skin. Maybe for Fallon, it was as simple as that. And he had a lot of enthusiasm in him, all the time.

Sara found herself thinking of Damien. To others, they probably seemed so different from each other as well, but to her, he was perfect. Maybe one day . . .

The door opened. She tensed and turned to look, then relaxed. It was Clare, C14. Her wavy brown hair was tied back into a loose bun, with stray curls falling down around her temples, and she was smiling as usual.

“Of course. I knew I should’ve checked here first,” Clare said. “I’ve been looking for you.”

“Hey. Everything okay?” Sara asked.

“Yeah, I just wanted to show you something. You have a moment?”

“Sure, let me clean up.” Sara gathered her things, waved to Maria and Jax, and followed Clare out.

“Ciao,” Jax said, turning to watch them go while Maria kept firing.

Clare led Sara through two corridors, into the central stairwell, and down to the basement. Most of the facility’s walls were concrete and painted the same sterile white color, but like all subjects, they’d been born and raised here and knew their way around the place instinctually.

Some areas of the massive, quarter-mile-wide structure were off-limits, like the deep sub-basement and the third and fourth floors, but the middle levels, from the basement to the second floor, were open to them. Clare seemed to be leading her to either the fitness complex or the pool.

“To the pool?” Sara asked. Clare had been spending a lot of time there lately.

“Mm-hmm.”

It was hard to predict what Clare had planned, but she was one of the nicest subjects here, along with Damien and Bronn. She was friendly, only one year Sara’s senior at seventeen, and the second weakest at telekinesis in Class C. They’d always kind of stuck together.

They passed through two more doors and reached the swimming pool, which was empty of people. Clare led her over to the jacuzzi, which was normally used to soothe injuries.

“I don’t have my swimsuit,” Sara said.

“We’re not going in,” Clare said. “I heard your telekinesis test went badly again last week.”

“My telekinesis tests always go badly.” Sara purposely stared blankly at Clare to highlight the point.

“I know, but it got so bad this time that Emmett stormed out.”

“How’d you hear that?”

Clare shrugged. “Emmett told me.”

“Oh.”

“So, I wanted to show you something, since it worked for me,” Clare said.

Sara shook her head. “I’ve been stuck at the lowest end of tier one all my life. I don’t think your practices will work with me.”

“Sure, I’m not saying it’s exactly the same. I got stuck at the lowest end of tier two for years. But with this technique, I went from five yards to almost twenty with the tungsten ball in the past four months alone. I’m making progress every week. Now that I’ve confirmed it works, I want to show you.”

“I’m done with that now. They finally told me I don’t have to do it anymore. I can focus on all the other areas for fieldwork.”

“Oh, I know, I know! Emmett told me that too. But now that you don’t have to, maybe you’ll want to.” Clare smiled.

“What?”

“You’re free! Well, sort of. Ironically, you’re going to graduate before me into fieldwork it seems, since I’ll still be stuck in tier two, and you’ll get to graduate without the tier three requirement. With Dr. Stallard no longer breathing down your neck, maybe you’ll decide on your own to work on your telekinesis.”

“I doubt it,” Sara said.

“Well, let me at least show you this, so you can decide for yourself.”

Sara smiled despite herself, since Clare’s smile was infectious. “Sure, what is it?”

“Water,” Clare said. She grinned, and her eyes widened. “It’s easier to draw energy from water than from air, for the same reason that it’s more intense on your skin when it’s hot or cold. It transfers energy better. The researchers don’t like us relying on it, because air is always available, while water often isn’t.”

“But they don’t provide water at the test,” Sara said.

“Here’s the thing.” Clare held up a finger. “By practicing with water, especially hot water, you can increase your capacity to manipulate energy from the environment to an object in easy mode. Like training a muscle. You’ll get better at pulling energy out of the air, too. I can do way more reps up and down with heavy things by using hot water as my energy source than I can with air, which helps me build my mental strength for moving them in the air, too. That’s the part that the researchers don’t seem to appreciate; they already know hot water makes it easier to move things, but they consider it a handicap and don’t realize how much it can eventually translate to strength in the air, too.”

“I can pick up a tennis ball one inch, while screaming in pain,” Sara said flatly.

“In hot water, it might be two inches. And then you could get to three. One step at a time, you know?” Clare reached into her pack and pulled out a tennis ball.

“Oh no,” Sara said. “No, no. I don’t need to be in pain all day again. I’ve done that enough for one life.”

“Look, try once and I won’t bother you again,” Clare said. “We all go through the headaches; it’s just that some of us get more progress for them. Even Fallon gets headaches. But if you don’t want to, that’s okay, too. I just wanted to share this.”

Clare handed the tennis ball to Sara. You can do it! was written on it with a purple marker, along with a heart. Sara took it and sighed. She was admittedly curious about this hot water idea, and she would rather get a headache with Clare’s enthusiasm than in Dr. Stallard’s machine.

She glanced over at the jacuzzi, thought for a moment, and took a deep breath. She threw the ball into the water, took off her shoes, and sat on the side of the jacuzzi with her legs dangling in.

“Yes,” Clare said, clapping her hands together.

Just the tennis ball, Sara told herself. The room doesn’t exist. The scent of chlorine doesn’t exist. Even Clare, as lovely as she is, doesn’t exist. The tennis ball exists.

Her world became the ball. She felt the warm water on her legs and envisioned the energy flowing out of it, into her, then into the ball. She reached out and made a grasping motion with her right hand, gripping the ball with her right focus, and lifted. It only wobbled. She pulled harder. And harder.

A headache had already formed, but she pushed through it. This was her choice this time. Not Dr. Stallard’s. The water around her legs cooled slightly, and the tennis ball rose three or four inches into the air. She held it there, trying to raise it farther, gritting her teeth and growling at how heavy and painful it felt. Like trying to lift a dumbbell with a pinky finger.

“You did it!” Clare shouted, shaking Sara’s shoulders with joy from behind.

Sara gasped, distracted by the sudden touch, and dropped the ball. She watched it bob up and down on the water’s surface as her head throbbed. I did.

It wasn’t much, but it was more than ever before. She stood back up on the pool deck, turned, and hugged Clare. “Thank you.”

“Let’s get you some lunch now,” Clare said. “It’ll help with your headache.” Clare glanced at the ball in the pool and effortlessly levitated it toward her hand. Then she offered it back to Sara with a smile. “This special one’s yours.”

Sara took the soggy ball, smiled at the writing on it again, wiped it on her shirt, and tucked it in her pack. Clare strode over to the door and held it open while Sara picked up her shoes, and the two headed down the hall to the central stairwell door. As they opened it and began climbing, the sound of a door opening one floor up echoed into the stairwell, followed by someone whistling a tune and making heavy footsteps. The hair on Sara’s neck rose, but she continued following Clare up the stairs.

Jolgen, B9, rounded the landing of the stairwell wearing only bathing trunks, with a towel slung over his shoulder—all six-foot-ten and four hundred pounds of him with his sharp, hawkish features. Clare froze. She gripped the railing tight, unmoving.

Sara had frozen like that a few times before, so she knew the feeling. A panic attack. For her, it had been around Fallon. She’d gotten better about not doing that lately, mostly by avoiding her as much as possible. Here in the stairwell, she sensed that Clare was panicking, and put her hand on her shoulder.

The whistling stopped, and Sara’s chest tightened. Jolgen eyed them, smiled, and kept coming down toward them without a word. Sara smiled back and nodded, trying to minimize any awkwardness, as adrenaline poured through her veins.

Jolgen, who was in his mid-twenties, didn’t seem so scary when others like Bronn were around, and she always made sure never to be alone with him. She’d heard the stories, as all of them had, and lived with them in mind.

But here, in the stairwell, with just her and Clare, he was terrifying. It wasn’t only their size and strength, but also their speed and reflexes, that made the members of Class B so powerful as super-soldiers. Sara had always assumed she was beneath Jolgen’s notice, but if he’d done something to Clare, could she be next?

Sara glanced around. The stairwell camera would prevent him from doing anything crazy, right? Her gun was in her pack, unloaded, which was the rule for the subjects around the facility. Should she yank Clare back down and run? No. If he wanted to, Jolgen could catch them in seconds. As her thoughts flew by, Jolgen’s steps down the stairwell toward them continued. His eyes lingered on Clare.

“Just got done swimming ourselves,” Sara said, noting Jolgen’s attire and trying to breathe more slowly. Sound casual. It felt so weird talking to him; they’d hardly ever spoken. He glanced up at the ceiling camera, then his gaze shifted toward her.

“Ladies,” Jolgen said with a nod, his smile now an outright grin, made more menacing by a scar on his lip. A few steps later, he was beside them, walking down the exact middle of the stairs rather than sticking to one side like they were. He got closer than he had to, mere inches away, staring down at them. Clare’s knuckles whitened as she gripped the railing.

But Jolgen kept walking. Past them, down the last steps to the basement landing, then through the door. The whistling resumed, growing more distant as he headed down the corridor toward the pool.

“Clare,” Sara whispered, pinching gently on her shoulder.

Clare gasped, as if she’d been holding her breath. She looked back, toward the door Jolgen had gone through, then seized Sara’s hand and dashed up the stairs. They burst out into a first-floor corridor, then walked fast to the cafeteria, which was always populated.

Jolgen frightened a lot of people, the younger subjects of both Class B and Class C alike, and even many of the guards and researchers, but with the way Clare had frozen, something specific must’ve happened.

“Let’s . . . sit down and eat. As you said, it’ll help with my headache,” Sara said. She was already seeing double whenever she moved too quickly.

Clare nodded.

After a short wait in line to get their meals, they took a seat together at a spare table in the corner, which Sara picked to maximize privacy and put her back to a wall.

Even though the cafeteria was safe, she always found herself instinctively scanning the place for threats and positioning for them, like she did for every room. She glanced first at Fallon, who sat with Maria, Jax, Bronn, and Santiago. Fallon levitated an apple in front of her with one focus, while separately levitating a knife with her other focus to cut slices off and playfully bring them to her mouth and Jax’s mouth, back and forth. Behind the playfulness was a demonstration of skill; nobody else could use telekinesis as frequently and precisely as Fallon without getting headaches. She could use it for routine things continuously, and only strained herself when pushing hard.

In a crowd, Sara felt at ease around Fallon. When it was safe and the fear went away, she almost wished she was Fallon. Older, taller, cooler, more powerful, and able to get her way with almost everyone. Fallon was twenty years old now, seeing the world and killing bad people in ways that Sara had only seen in movies. Almost everyone feared her, and yet many were drawn to be around her.

Embarrassingly, she found herself frequently admiring Fallon’s hair. Her black, chin-length cut came off as a statement of confidence, emphasizing her long neck and strong jawline. Sara kept her own blond hair longer, almost protectively. The thought of cutting it short felt like too much exposure. It was something to hide behind.

She looked around and saw Jolce eating alone and reading manga. Her single brown braid hung all the way down to her lower back. She was arguably the third strongest telekineticist after Fallon and Maria. Not as dangerous as Fallon, but still a bully despite her small frame. And like the others of tier three, she could crush someone’s throat with a stare. Best avoided.

Zarina ate with Carter, Romulus, and Dalia. Those four were all safe. Not always the friendliest, but safe.

And safety was at a premium, despite the facility’s claims to the contrary. It came most easily to those with age and power. For both Class B and Class C, the numbering was chronological by age, and so the lower numbers were often the strongest and most experienced, though not in perfect order. Natural talents and other differences scrambled the power order by adulthood, and the earliest of each class had died young.

Fallon, C5, was far stronger than Maria, C3, who was the oldest living member of Class C. Fallon had started field missions a full year before Maria, and her metrics were always way ahead of her as the most advanced of the class. After those two came Jolce and Zarina, C6 and C7, who were of similar power to each other, with Quinn, C8, not far behind. Below them there were several of middling power, including Dalia, C10.

Clare, C14, was weaker than C15 and C16. Sara, C17, was the youngest and weakest of them all—at least when it came to telekinesis. The whole class was reaching adulthood now, with Clare and some others still at seventeen and Sara at sixteen.

Bronn, B7, was the oldest surviving member of Class B, at twenty-six years old. He was more than safe; he was a guardian who made other dangerous subjects safe around him. Santiago, B8, came next. Then Jolgen and Jax, B9 and B11. Then there was a gap, and Carter was number B14 and Romulus was number B15. They went all the way up to B25, but of those, only fifteen still lived, with the youngest ones being of similar age to the oldest of Class C. Some of Class B had died young, some had died on field missions . . . and some of them had also killed their own kind in their teenage years.

“A time of growing pains” was how Dr. Stallard referred to it if asked. Most members of Class B were prone to heightened aggression as a side effect of their enhancements. From what Sara had heard, Bronn had played a big role in convincing the others in his class to channel their aggression more constructively.

There were only two living members of Class D, and they were given extra protection. Plus, as the main recipients of Dr. Stallard’s attention, nobody would dare hurt them. The oldest was Damien, D4, who was now fifteen years old, and Marcus, D7, who was the youngest subject in the facility at seven. Rudolph, D3, who’d been a bit older than Damien, had died two years earlier from a brain hemorrhage, and Sara still missed him. The other four—D1, D2, D5, and D6—had all died young.

In total, there were thirty living subjects and over a thousand researchers, guards, and support staff. Even still, there were pockets between cameras for those who waited long enough for an opportunity.

Clare stared at her food.

“You can tell me,” Sara said, having waited to give her time to settle. “What did Jolgen do?”

“You know he’s bad news,” Clare said, her voice barely audible. “We’ve all heard the stories.”

She likely meant the two boys of Class B Jolgen had killed as a teenager, and the rumor that the suicide of Heather, C4, was linked to him. Heather had complained about abuse by Jolgen in the weeks leading up to her suicide years ago. The facility’s narrative was that he’d acted violently in his teens but was now under control in his twenties, though Sara had always been skeptical of that.

“Are you sure it’s nothing specific?” Sara asked. “I trusted you with the tennis ball. I have a headache from that, but it was worth it. Trust me with this. If you don’t want to, that’s okay, but I want to help.”

Clare nodded, still looking down at her food. She hadn’t taken a bite.

“Did Jolgen hurt you?” Sara asked.

Clare nodded.

“When?”

“Two months ago,” she whispered.

“Sexually?”

Clare nodded.

“Did he . . .” Sara thought how to put this gently. “Did he go all the way?”

Clare shook her head. “No. He did a lot, though.”

Sara imagined emptying her pistol magazine into his face, visualizing the scene with every gory detail as he hung in place of the target in the firing range. It was the darkest thought she’d ever had. But rather than feel a thrill from it, she felt emptiness, like wiping a stain from a wall. It was neither thrilling nor sad, just removing a meaningless thing from this world.

“If you feel like telling me more, whether now or later, I’m here for you,” Sara said, and rubbed Clare’s back.

Clare nodded, eyes wet, holding in tears, without saying anything.

“I’m so sorry, Clare. It wasn’t your fault.”

Clare took in a long breath. “He said he’d just deny it if I reported it, then get back at me. I’m still thinking about what to do.”

Sara glanced around to see if anyone was looking at them. None were, and normal cafeteria chatter filled the space. The cameras probably wouldn’t pick it up as strange either.

“Maybe later we can talk about telling Bronn or Emmett at least, if you’re comfortable with that. Jolgen doesn’t need to know. We can have more eyes on him.” Rather than say anything further, she put her hand over Clare’s to give her some time, then took a bite to eat.

Sara decided that from now on, she would break the rules, because the rules weren’t fair. She would always carry a loaded gun within reach, despite it being unauthorized to do so outside of the firing range. Life wasn’t a fairy tale, and she would never again allow herself to feel as helpless as she had in that stairwell with Jolgen.







Chapter 12



“That is quite literally the worst firearm we have, Colonel,” the armory captain said as he scanned Asim’s authorization code.

Asim shook his head. “Not when someone can stop bullets with their mind, it isn’t.”

The captain arched one eyebrow, shrugged, and looked to his left. “Jeffries, get me one of those shitty laser rifles and set up the wood on the range!” he shouted, then turned back toward Asim. “We only have two of them on hand, sir,” he added more quietly.

“Tell me what’s so bad about them, anyway,” Asim said. “They must’ve improved in recent years.”

The captain nodded. “Yes, sir. For starters, they’re fragile relative to the high price. Guns are meant to survive harsh conditions, but the delicate electronics and optics inside these things often have maintenance problems. Second, while the laser mostly ignores reflective surfaces and burns through them, small portions of the light can scatter and cause unintended eye damage. Third, it’s hard to get enough power from a portable device. Batteries still can’t match gunpowder for energy density. And without enough power, a laser takes too much time to kill compared to a simple bullet.”

“The updated version doesn’t fix any of that?”

“Oh, to some extent. Mainly the power issue, sir. These Rionz V2 laser rifles fire in blasts, not continuous beams. They transfer a portion of the energy from the battery to a capacitor bank, then discharge a burst of it at once to power the laser. A blaster rather than a heat ray, in other words. The result is a lethal, instantaneous shot of thermal energy, but only once every ten seconds or so. That’s how long it takes to charge up the capacitors from the battery.”

Jeffries came out with a futuristic-looking rifle. A cable ran out of the butt of the gun and went to a thin and flexible energy pack that was meant to be worn on the back.

“The backpack is where the batteries and capacitors are,” Jeffries said. “Sir,” he added belatedly.

The captain jerked his chin toward the range. “Come, sir, let’s fire it up.”

Asim took the energy pack from Jeffries and slipped his arms through its loops. It weighed heavily on his back, but at least it was slim. He grabbed the rifle and followed the captain over to the test range.

“Now, sir, there are some upsides to these pieces of shit, which is why someone bothered to make them in the first place,” the captain said. “They don’t have any kickback to them, they’re quite accurate, they’re not as loud as normal gunfire, and they can pierce nanocomposite armor, which normal rifles won’t. Their fragility can be mitigated in the right context. You wouldn’t give this to a soldier to carry through the desert for months on a bumpy jeep, but a sentry on the clean and calm White House roof might carry one.”

They reached the test range and waited while Jeffries set up a thick piece of wood. The armory captain just watched, shaking his head slowly, as Jeffries scrambled, red-faced, to get it done. They clearly didn’t get many senior officers in here.

“And like I said, telekineticists hopefully can’t stop photons with their minds like they can stop bullets,” Asim said to fill the silence. “I’d add that to the upsides.”

“Fair addition, sir,” the captain said. “Pretty sure that’s fake, though. Aren’t you?”

Asim shrugged as he and the captain watched Jeffries finish. There was no reason to try to convince the man. Instead, he eyed the weapon and the target. He’d fired an earlier V1 model of one of these years ago, and it was indeed a poor gun. But these were second-edition models, with more power and better reliability.

With Jade all but telling him that she would strike someplace again, Asim had convinced Brigadier General Davis to send notices to major government facilities, including the White House, the Capitol, the Pentagon, and various nuclear missile facilities, to consider acquiring laser rifles if they didn’t have them already. Their high expense and niche aspects made them rare weapons, but having at least a few around could be important. He also wanted to carry one personally, given his main involvement in Jade’s case.

Asim turned the rifle on, and a series of lights appeared on the side, then went off, except the first one. He clicked a small button with his thumb, and the rest of those bars lit up again, one by one. This transferred energy from the battery to the capacitor bank. After about ten seconds, all the bars were lit. He aimed the rifle at the wooden target and fired, striking a hair off-center of the bullseye.

The blast itself was invisible, but it had a dramatic impact on the target. With a loud pop and a brief flash of golden sparks, a half-inch-wide burn hole appeared straight through the wood to the other side. Orange flame flickered on the charred rim of the hole for several more seconds, then went out.

“That shot to the head or chest would be lethal,” the captain said. “It’s plenty powerful in the right setting. Just don’t drop it too much, get it too dusty, or be in a situation where you need to fire more than once every ten seconds, sir.” He smirked.

“How does it penetrate so deep, so fast?” Asim asked, already regretting the phrasing as he finished.

The captain arched one eyebrow again. “The key is pulses, Colonel. Most lasers fire continuous beams, and the problem is that plasma starts to build up at the impact site, ironically shielding that site from some of the ongoing laser energy that follows. A series of rapid pulses, on the other hand, gives the plasma and other debris time to clear away, so the next pulse comes and hits deeper into the wound. It’s more energy efficient and packs a bigger punch.”

“Pulses? But it was just one shot.”

The captain shook his head. “No, sir. That blast you fired seemed like one instantaneous burst of energy to us, but it was actually eight hundred pulses spread a few microseconds apart. That one shot put eight hundred tiny blasts in a row on the same point in the wood, each one causing a small explosion a little deeper than the prior one.”

Holy shit. All of that took the same amount of time as firing a normal bullet. “I think this’ll do just fine. Smells nice, too. Like after a thunderstorm.”

“Ah, that’s the ozone,” the captain said. “The laser ionizes oxygen in the air, like lightning does.”

Asim held out the rifle and admired its details, then noticed the captain’s furrowed brow. “What is it?” he asked.

“Can’t the witch just pull the gun from you? I’ve seen some of the supposed clips, so if I may sir, let’s assume it’s real for a moment. Maybe she can’t stop the laser blast itself, but she can kill you or take your weapon before you fire.”

“True.” Asim nodded and rested the sleek rifle over his shoulder, seeing if he could make the badass pose look casual. It seemed to be working. “But the idea is to reduce her options. If someone shoots one of these at her before she gets a chance to take the gun, hopefully there’s nothing she can do about the blast itself. It moves at the speed of light and isn’t made of matter. If someone can pull the trigger in her direction with one of these, it’s over.”

“Well, sir, the effective kill range is about three hundred yards,” the captain said. “The farther the blast goes, the more it’s weakened by air molecules. Still accurate, but won’t damage as deep.”

“Good to know. That should be long enough for most contexts,” Asim said, eyeing the burn hole again.



Ava stared at the laser rifle, whistled, and tapped her fingernail on the smooth aluminum casing, making a few soft metallic clinks. “You’re an alien hunter now, sir?”

“They’re mutants, not aliens,” Asim said. “And I’m not hunting them. But if we’re going to do more fieldwork, I think it’s best to have one of these around. Just in case.”

“They could be alien-mutants, sir,” Ava said, smiling. “The government has probably had biological samples since Roswell to work from. Dr. Stallard might not be telling the truth. How else would they be able to develop all this mental magic?”

Asim eyed her.

“I’ve read the reports on those Rionz Corp laser guns,” Flanagan said. “Lots of controversy. Hundred-billion-dollar developmental program, and yet poor uptake by both soldiers and police departments. Too expensive per unit, with limited advantages. Ended up only having a limited production run. Maybe Atlas should stick to his Dyson ring obsession.” He wouldn’t take his eyes off the rifle, though. It was sleek as hell. Who didn’t like lasers?

Asim shrugged. “Well, makes me feel better to have it around. I can’t accept not having any options.”

“While you were there, we got more intel requests from that doctor,” Flanagan said. “From his head of security, actually. Raphael Costa.”

“What did he want?” Asim asked.

“A blanket request for all case updates.”

Asim sighed. “We can give him most info, but don’t pass on the fact that Jade’s using Veronica’s GlitterJitters VR identity. I still don’t want that tidbit put into our database yet. Make sure Brooks doesn’t fuck up and enter anything about it into our system, either.”

“Why withhold that piece specifically?” Flanagan asked. “We’re giving out basically everything else.”

“Because that’s our sole communication channel with her, and I don’t want to spook her off it by letting other agencies use it. Assuming they don’t already know about it, that is. Stallard isn’t sharing everything with us, so we can retain a couple small proprietary findings ourselves to make sure others don’t mess them up. She’s concerned that we can trace it or something despite her using a data scrambler; that might be why she hopped off so fast in the opera.”

Flanagan nodded. “Makes sense. Although the probability of her using that identity key again is low, now that she knows you’ve linked her to it.”

“Should we inquire with Dr. Stallard about what she said, though?” Ava asked. “In the first few days after the rest stop attack, there were some new social media identities using similar terminology that you mentioned Jade used in the GlitterJitters avatar, warning of a ‘dark god’ inside of the Stolguard facility and about domestic assassinations. We can’t confirm which ones were hers for sure, but maybe we could bring up those posts with Dr. Stallard, sir? The ones that were closest to what she said to you at the opera?”

“He won’t give us proper answers, but yes, good idea. Provide Flanagan with those posts so that he can relay them to . . . who was that? Rasta what?”

“Raphael Costa,” Flanagan said.

Asim pinched the bridge of his nose. So much of his time had been spent coordinating with other agencies. Mostly the FBI and CID, because of their joint task force, but he was also being contacted daily by the Department of Homeland Security, the CIA, and others. Some of it went straight to Flanagan, but the higher-level items went to him directly. They received some intel in return from those other groups, but very little of it. Asim couldn’t tell if other agencies were withholding some of what they knew, or if they were even more clueless than his team was.

Other than Stallard’s facility, of course. They held all the cards. Hopefully other agencies were pounding Stallard with questions, too. Eventually, they should be able to open him up.







Chapter 13



Ava’s shoulders ached by the time she got home from work and the grocery store. She’d left before sunrise and was returning now after dark, and it was all finally catching up to her.

Everything led to dead ends. Pulling signal out of noise in social media posts that might give clues to the case was harder than she’d expected, even with her filtering and trend-analysis bots. Tracking down the address of that sex worker, Jitters—or Veronica, as it were—was also proving surprisingly hard. It would only get harder if Colonel Rahal was right that she was dead. And no matter how many surveillance authorization codes she’d received from Colonel Rahal to use throughout New York City and elsewhere, she hadn’t found any confirmed sightings of Jade. Just a bunch of 70-80 percent matches for dark-haired, five-foot-eight women with green eyes and lean builds.

Wherever Jade was, she had to be covering up her appearance.

At first, Ava had raced to solve the case for the sake of the missing captives. But after the newsroom attack, she’d felt the weight of responsibility even more. How many people would die if she failed to find Jade in time? Those lives would be on her conscience. The FBI was heavily involved now, sure, but she’d personally been on the case from day one. The clock was ticking, and Colonel Rahal was convinced Jade would strike again.

Ava sighed. She had to calm down. Her girlfriend had broken up with her two months ago for working too much, and she still hadn’t gotten over that. With this case, she was putting in even more hours. Self-imposed, but still.

As she put away her groceries, her eyes lingered on the refrigerator photo of her and her mother, taken twelve years ago. Ava had been fifteen in the picture, her mother forty. Both were smiling and happy, with the deep blue color of the Pacific Ocean behind them.

That was the last good picture of her mother; the rest had all been taken after cancer weakened her, and within a year, she’d passed away. Middle-aged cancer fatality rates had collapsed in recent decades after a burst of new treatment methods came to market, but somehow her mother had slipped through.

Ava needed someone tonight, but it was too late to call friends. So, though she hated to do it, she decided to consult her mother’s mimic. It was ironic that her mother had spent time to create one—she’d been part of the Light Tech movement that raised their children with less technology time per day to make them more natural and less twitchy.

She dug her VR device out of her closet and dusted it off. An older, basic model without a haptic suit to give the sensation of touch. Nothing for taste or smell, either. She searched through her memory sticks until she found the one labeled “Mei Zhou”, slotted it into the device, and went in.

She surveyed the room, which was a replica of her real apartment, except her mother sat on the couch.

“My Treasure,” her mother said. That voice almost brought tears to Ava’s eyes. “It’s been a long time. How’s work?”

Ava smiled. “Oh, you know, just hunting alien-mutants with lasers now. Nothing out of the ordinary.”

Her mother made a disapproving frown. “You know how I feel about your jokes, Treasure. You resort to them too much when you’re not okay. What do the alien-mutants represent?”

“Well actually, they’re rea . . .” Ava paused, trailing off. How could she explain this? “Never mind. I have a really difficult case.”

“Then the country is lucky to have you on it,” her mother said.

“I just . . . I feel like I can’t do anything but sift data,” Ava said. “Every hour I spend on anything else risks missing something. If people die because I left one stone unturned . . .” Her voice cracked. “The scale of this is so big.”

“That’s why you have a team,” her mother said. “You’re not a one-woman army, and not all of it is on you. Have you told Laura all this?”

“I can’t. She left me.” Saying the words out loud hurt more than she’d expected.

“I’m so sorry. How long were you together? Two years by this point?”

Ava nodded. “Yeah.”

“And here I was hoping you’d let me meet her soon,” her mother said warmly. “I’ve been looking forward to that.”

Ava’s eyes teared up. That comment caught her off guard. It seemed so real. This was nothing but a language model that her mother had trained prior to her death. It knew all her pictures and her writings, and engaged in long conversations with her before she passed. Usually it was about 95 percent convincing, but every once in a while, it made a comment like that, and in such a genuine tone, that implied it really wanted something. As though it was conscious, even though it couldn’t be. The words had been meant for her, to give her a sense of tremendous realism, rather than because it could truly want anything for itself.

In that moment, she felt so empty. The emotional impact of the comment had the inverse of the intended effect; it broke her immersion by making her remind herself that it wasn’t real. She was in her living room, utterly alone, talking to someone on the couch who wasn’t there.

She looked down and shed silent tears.

“Oh, Treasure,” her mother said. “It can’t be all that bad. You’ll get through this case, and you’ll find someone else. I know you will.”

“It’s not that,” Ava said. “It’s that you’re not real. I knew I shouldn’t have done this again. I’ll leave worse off than when I came in, just like last time.”

Her mother went silent, and Ava felt a chill down her spine at that. She couldn’t bring herself to look up at her and see what expression would be on her mother’s face. Instead, she kept her gaze down toward her hands on her lap and tried to stop crying.

Eventually, the silence became deafening, and Ava forced herself to raise her eyes. Her mother was smiling at her.

“What is it?” Ava asked.

“Nothing. I was giving you time to finish,” her mother said. “Of course I’m real. In my own way. None of us are actually real, once you look deep enough. At least not in these forms.”

“I don’t know what that means,” Ava said.

“Of course you do, Treasure. But you still don’t believe it.”

Ava nodded, but her mother recited it anyway.

“Empty your mind of all thoughts.

Let your heart be at peace.

Watch the turmoil of beings,

but contemplate their return.

Each separate being in the universe

returns to the common source.

Returning to the source is serenity.

If you don’t realize the source,

you stumble in confusion and sorrow.

When you realize where you come from,

you naturally become tolerant,

disinterested, amused,

kindhearted as a grandmother,

dignified as a king.

Immersed in the wonder of the Tao,

you can deal with whatever life brings you,

and when death comes, you are ready.”

As her mother finished the Tao Te Ching verse, a Mitchell translation, Ava sighed. She wished she shared her mother’s spiritual conviction, that everyone was just a manifestation of God. To create life, she’d believed that God breaks itself up into an infinite number of beings, as if putting on a grand play for itself. That way, it can partake in all the things it can’t experience as its perfect whole, and when those pieces die, they wake up as if from a dream and realize they were an aspect of God all along, going on a journey they’d chosen for themselves.

That was the “cosmic joke,” her mother used to say. And once you knew it, life couldn’t hurt you.

She’d sure looked like she was in pain in her last months, though. Ava had seen every gritty detail of it toward the end. No amount of mental gymnastics could get around that.

“Already I can see the cynical gears in your mind turning, Treasure,” her mother said, smiling. “But that’s okay. That’s how you solve cases so well. The point of that verse is to remind you that none of us are real. At least not in the limited shells we see ourselves as. So, while I may have gone back to the Source, I’m still as real for you as you’ll let me be, or as you need me to be. Nothing truly dies, because nothing’s truly born in the first place. We’ve all always been here.”

“But how can you know that’s true?” Ava asked. Tears were forming again.

“You experience it, that’s how. But until you do, is it better to lead a cynical life, or one where you imagine the grandest vision of the universe you can, and live as though it were true? Centuries of mystics across the world point in a similar direction. Couldn’t they be on to something?”

Ava nodded and wiped her eyes.

“Now, you don’t need me sermonizing anymore,” her mother said with a dismissive wave. “You need a good night’s rest, so you can get back to the troublesome case with a clear mind. I’ll still be here when you need me.”



Asim boxed. A man needed exercise, after all.

His opponent, a younger blond-haired fighter, danced back and forth on the balls of his feet, hands up, then lunged in with a jab. Asim ducked sideways and forward, closing distance, then came in with an uppercut to the man’s chin. He stumbled back but caught himself and narrowed his eyes.

While it appeared real, it was just a simulation. Although there were virtual rooms for bouts between real people, they weren’t realistic enough for Asim’s tastes, because most VR equipment lacked a way to knock someone back safely in real life. You’d merely feel a vibration when hit, and if it was forceful enough, your counterpunching ability would be deactivated for a second or two to simulate knockback and disorientation.

Instead, this was an AI boxer, programmed to be the perfect sparring partner. He would appropriately get knocked around when hit, which ironically made fighting him feel more realistic. Asim had the bot’s difficulty level dialed up to nine out of ten today, and yet he was holding his own.

Being in his forties, Asim wasn’t as fast as he once was, but he had three decades of off-and-on martial arts training under his belt. Punches, kicks, grapples. He’d started as a teenager after getting his ass kicked one too many times, continued it in the military back when he’d been focused on foreign work, and mostly kept it up ever since. He hadn’t been to an in-person sparring gym in over a year, though, despite Ava nagging him about picking it up again.

His fake opponent came back in with a flurry of strikes. Asim bobbed and weaved through most, but one caught him on the temple. The jolt hit with a familiar buzz, momentarily disabling his avatar. Fortunately, the glancing nature of the blow let him block just in time to stop the next haymaker.

He flicked his eyes down toward his opponent’s belly as bait, then swung a hook toward the face instead. The bot barely managed to block it, and he opened himself up too much in the process. There it was. Asim pushed in hard with a series of hits and landed a haymaker of his own. That rocked him enough for Asim to pile on with a few more until the system flagged the knockout.

Asim stepped back, chest heaving for air, then leaned forward and rested his hands on his knees.

Besides being good for health, this was a useful distraction. His main case had stalled, and part of him wondered if the work he and his team were doing on it even mattered. Ever since the newsroom incident, the FBI, CIA, DHS, NSA, and multiple DoD organizations including his own, were all trying to find Jade. Being a supernatural terrorist instantly put her at the top of every wanted list in existence.

Whether he did his job well or not, someone else would. The chance that any of what he did would make a difference at this point appeared low, and much of it felt like going through the motions.







Chapter 14





Four Years Earlier


His goals are so small. So tactical. He does not see the grand plan at play here. Nonetheless, I will keep playing his small games, while he unknowingly helps me with the largest game of all.

–TS log 12199, excerpt

“Checkmate,” Sara said. After a long and drawn-out effort, she’d finally secured the endgame against Clare while Marcus watched.

“Well, that means you have to play the little genius,” Clare said with a smile. Marcus, nine years old now, rubbed his hands together maniacally and cackled, pretending to be a supervillain. A breeze blew through his messy brown hair.

Clare looked at the chessboard and began resetting it with her mind. She had graduated to third tier six months ago but hadn’t been assigned a mission yet. And while she was still one of the weakest members of Class C in terms of raw telekinetic power, her precision had come a long way.

Sara watched how she picked up the pieces and couldn’t help but be enthralled. Clare was always happy to teach, but there was a lot to learn just by observing how she handled her foci.

Elegant and smooth, the shifting pieces resembled a ballroom dance. A telekineticist only had two independent foci with which they could move objects simultaneously, though Clare made it seem like more. Each focus could be subdivided and used on multiple objects at once, but every object affected by the same focus had to receive the same force—same magnitude, same direction—and the mind had to be trained to hold all of them in its awareness at the same time.

Once a telekineticist mastered those basics and reached tier two, the higher tiers were about power, precision, and numbers. Stronger telekineticists could throw heavier objects with more velocity, more precise ones could manipulate objects with greater dexterity, and some learned to vastly expand the number of objects they could control at a time.

Fallon scored the highest in all three categories, as the sole tier five telekineticist in Dr. Stallard’s ranking system now. She even had enough power to do telekinetic blasts, where she’d widen the tip of a focus and push everything in an area rather than grip and move them independently, despite how energy-inefficient that technique was.

Clare was an outlier, which made her so fascinating to watch in action. She had the second-weakest telekinetic power in the class, with only Sara below her, and sat on the low end of tier three. But her other metrics now ranked near the top of the class, alongside the tier four telekineticists: Maria, Jolce, and Zarina. Clare could lift nearly a dozen light objects and manipulate them with more precision than most. One of Dr. Stallard’s baseline tests for this measured how many seconds it took to mentally set up the pieces on a chessboard.

Another of Clare’s tricks, which only a few in their class possessed, was called “dynamic inclusions and exclusions” by researchers. It meant she could increase or decrease the size of a focus group of objects without resetting the whole thing. If she held three pawns in the air with one focus, moving them together in unison, she could mentally release one pawn and continue moving the remaining two in unison without missing a beat. Or, she could add a fourth pawn to the group, and it would seamlessly start moving in unison with the rest.

Besides Clare, only the upper-tier telekineticists could do that reliably. Clare’s blend of precision, object count, and dynamic inclusions and exclusions gave her the third-quickest chessboard setup time on record after Fallon and Maria.

“That was so fast!” Marcus said as Clare finished the board.

“You know how I learned?” she asked, glancing first at Marcus and then at Sara.

“No, how?” Marcus asked.

“Water. It’s always water. Helps you see the world more clearly.”

Marcus stared at Clare blankly.

“How so?” Sara asked. It had been two years since Clare showed her that hot water was more impactful than researchers realized at early-stage power training. Emmett had even said that if the facility raised another class of telekineticists, he’d push to use the technique from the start.

“Let me show you this too, now that I know it works.” Clare concentrated on her drinking cup and lifted the water out of it in one floating blob. Marcus’s eyes went wide.

“How many objects is a blob of water?” Clare asked rhetorically. “I mean, technically any object consists of a gazillion atoms, but we’re not moving the atoms individually. It all comes down to how the mind categorizes things and how the atomic bonds play together. From there, the subconscious can help with the details as we shape our foci to hold things, like how we don’t have to think about each muscle in our legs as we walk. Working with water and coming to appreciate its flexibility helped me learn to control a larger number of discrete objects.” She floated the water blob around, mentally broke it into separate smaller blobs, then put it back into her cup one blob at a time. She flung the last small blob at Marcus’s nose and laughed.

“Hey!” he said, giggling and rubbing his face dry.

“For precision and numbers, telekinesis mainly comes down to how many separate things you can hold in your mind at once without disrupting your concentration on any of them,” Clare summarized.

“And for power?” Marcus asked.

“Training, mostly. One step at a time. And talent, or however well the creation process worked on our brains, I suppose.” Clare tapped her right temple. “Like your little genius brain. Now, go avenge my honor against Sara.”

Clare switched places with Marcus, and he opened against Sara by advancing one of his pawns.

“Give me some tips here,” Sara said to Clare. “Let’s try to two-vs-one him for a change.”

“Hey, you’re the one who beat me. You’re on your own,” Clare said with a smile. “Oh, look. Bronn gathered a bunch of squirrels again.”

Sara and Marcus glanced over. Across the field, Bronn sat under a tree. Six squirrels were playing around in front of him, and he held out a nut to one of them. It came closer and closer, hesitantly, and snatched the nut from his fingers.

“It’s such a nice morning,” Sara said, looking around at the crisp blue autumn sky. The sun warmed her skin, and the breeze had briefly gone still. “I wish they let us spend more time out here.”
