They were on facility grounds, which sprawled over more than five thousand flat acres of grassy and forested area, with a razor wire fence and plenty of security cameras, sensors, and drones around the perimeter. The wide four-story main building stood at the center, while smaller buildings and a runway dotted the rest of the facility grounds.

Decades ago, the facility supposedly had another government purpose, and several thousand people had worked here. It’d been abandoned for economic reasons, and Dr. Stallard with his original patron, General Stolguard, had repurposed it for their ends with a workforce totaling just over one thousand.

That made it feel like a half-empty prison. Sara had never been allowed to leave, and a tracker chip in the back of her neck let the security team monitor her location. The Atlantic Ocean lay less than ten miles to the east, and yet she’d never seen it. So close, and yet so far.

Not all the subjects viewed it that way, but at least they got to travel frequently. And they earned more perks and status by completing missions.

Sara gazed over at Bronn again, still playing with the squirrels, and wondered why he hadn’t snuck away while on assignment. Most of the subjects liked their military work, but Bronn had a disdain for violence, despite being so capable of delivering it. She always partly expected him to find a way to remove his tracker chip and vanish while on a mission. Maybe they’d presume him dead, when really, he’d be free. Whenever he left on one of his deployments, she always made sure to say goodbye in case it was the last time, either because of his death or his freedom.

“Saaaara,” Marcus said. “You’re taking forever. It’s your turn.”

“Sorry,” she said, reaching out to ruffle his hair, while he pretended to dislike it. She shifted to sit cross-legged, returned her attention to the game, and unsurprisingly lost within five minutes.

According to Damien, Dr. Stallard aimed for two different intelligence levels among the subjects. Class B and C had both been designed to have intelligence levels of about one or two standard deviations above average. That was what General Stolguard had initially requested.

“Any more than that, and a soldier is too smart for his own good,” was how the general had apparently put it to Dr. Stallard, who’d later told Damien during one of their chess matches.

But Dr. Stallard had boosted intelligence as far as he could for Class D, reaching five or more standard deviations above normal for Rudolph, Damien, and Marcus, the three who’d made it to speaking age.

Speaking of generals, here they are. A small jet appeared on the horizon. It was likely the flight carrying General Price and others to watch today’s reclassification test.

“We should get ready,” Sara said. All members of Class B and C had graduated as of six months ago, but the reclassification tests still ran twice a year to measure ongoing improvements into higher tiers, and for spec-ops officers to observe the subject pool for mission assignments. A few of the youngest subjects hadn’t been selected for any missions yet, including Sara and Clare.

Clare sighed and got to her feet.

“What is it?” Sara asked.

“The more I think about it, I don’t want to be picked,” Clare said. “I’d rather stay here and work on things. Especially since Jolgen and most others are away a lot now. It’s more peaceful here than it used to be.”

Sara was dumbfounded. Clare was nineteen years old and Sara eighteen, and they’d still never left. They hadn’t gone more than a mile in any direction from this spot, all within facility grounds.

“Don’t you want to see the world?” Sara asked. “This is our chance.”

“I do,” Clare said. “But not in combat.”

“I think it would be worth it, at least once. Just to see other people, see another place.”

Clare shrugged. “Well, it’s not like we have much choice in the matter either way.”

On that, she was right.



Fallon sat high on the facility roof with Jax, waiting to see how long it would take someone to find them. Everyone was supposed to be down on the grassy area in preparation for Price’s arrival, and of course the other subjects had done as they’d been told, sheep that they were.

She’d only been back from her latest mission for two weeks, and already the place felt suffocating. It didn’t make sense for them to have to keep coming back here between deployments, as though they were in some stupid adult daycare.

At least the wind felt refreshing up here on the roof.

“How the hell does he do that thing with the squirrels?” Jax asked, watching Bronn below. “They even let him pet them sometimes.”

Fallon shrugged. “By sitting still for like an hour. Apparently if you’re as boring as a tree, they start to treat you like one.”

Jax chuckled and caressed her back. “Well, I like your company better anyway.”

The pleasant feeling was ruined by one of the access doors opening, its telltale rusty squeak coming from about a hundred yards behind them. She sighed. “Looks like you’re going to win the bet. Again.”

“Told ya. They’re getting faster.”

He removed his touch and started to stand, but Fallon put her hand on his shoulder. “Wait,” she whispered. “Let’s see if they actually check.” She’d chosen this spot to sit for a reason; a small rooftop water tower blocked the view from the closest access door.

But soon enough, the sound of multiple footsteps drew closer. Jax smirked.

Fallon rolled her eyes and stood up. It was inevitable that they’d be found due to their tracker chips, but she’d thought the security team would have trouble figuring out which floor they were on, even as they knew the horizontal coordinates. Seemed that wasn’t the case. It was still useful to test their limits a bit, though.

She stepped out from around the water tower, and Jax followed.

“Again?” said Raphael, the facility’s head of security, with a sigh. His laser rifle was casually pointed down in a non-threatening way. Four more guards with regular rifles stood behind him, all calm. “Roof’s off limits. You both know I have to report these instances to Dr. Stallard.”

Such a good, loyal dog you are. “Consider it my contribution to keep you guys on the payroll,” Fallon said with a mock salute. She turned and looked southward into the distance, farther than any of them would be able to see. “Price is arriving anyway. We weren’t going to miss the party, obviously.”

Raphael squinted and gazed out toward the horizon, trying and clearly failing to see the general’s plane. She strolled toward him. As she got close, Raphael looked back at her, and she slowly slid her finger along the aluminum casing of his laser rifle while meeting his eyes. He and his fellow armored dorks tensed up but didn’t do anything. She continued past him toward the access door, putting them in a position to follow.



Sara did her best to remain calm as she and the rest of the graduates of classes B and C—those not currently on missions—stood in line within the enclosed sports and testing field while General Price and Dr. Stallard walked past, examining them. A cluster of military officers trailed behind them, and along the walls more than four dozen guards kept watch.

General Price was an athletic man in his early sixties with a shaved head and face. What really stood out were his piercing blue eyes, aggressive white eyebrows, and impeccable posture. It was even harder to keep eye contact with him than it was with Dr. Stallard, since his face seemed perpetually carved into anger.

Price had been visiting twice a year as far back as her earliest memories; he had been Dr. Stallard’s military financier since his early forties as a lieutenant colonel, almost twenty years ago. Before that, it was General Stolguard who’d provided Dr. Stallard’s initial financing, starting about four decades ago.

Some subjects stood at attention, as they were supposed to. Others had their arms crossed. Jax rested his hands behind his back, his stance more casual. The ones with unique skills tended to be the most informal, since they knew they were irreplaceable. But Fallon stood out most of all, crossing her arms and levitating twelve inches off the ground, bobbing slightly up and down, the air chilly enough around her to make her breath visible. The extra height off the floor put her at eye level with the men of Class B.

She always has to stand out, Sara thought. Using telekinesis on one’s own body was known to be difficult: you had to spread the force evenly to avoid risking injury, it supposedly felt disorienting to move with your own pushes and pulls, and holding the body’s weight aloft drained energy quickly. Fallon could do it with ease, and Zarina sometimes did it briefly. Both Maria and Jolce could probably do it as well.

“The rest of the laggards are in now, eh?” Price said as he walked past Clare. “We’ve got the full pick of the litter.”

“Depending on funding levels, we’d like to do another three classes to expand the pool,” Dr. Stallard said.

The general nodded absently, looking at Sara, who stood at the end of Class C’s line. “You get any better yet?” he asked, stopping to turn toward her. He already had their category scores on the printout in his hand, so he must’ve been asking that as a personality test.

“My telekinetic precision is improving, sir,” she said, forcing herself to meet his icy eyes. “But I continue to emphasize marksmanship, given my severe power limitations. I’m eager to find a point of contribution.”

Price nodded, seemingly finding the answer acceptable. Having reached the end of the lines, he turned around to address the room. “The next mission is a series of assassinations in Iran. A multi-monther, in deep.” Part of why the subjects were never allowed to leave except for missions was because they were constantly exposed to top-secret black-ops details, including those about foreign and domestic assassinations. And because their bodies were government property. “We need it quiet, so this is a Class C job. With C3 and C6 already deployed elsewhere, I’ll take C5, C10, and . . . C14.”

Fallon, Dalia, and Clare. Sara looked over at Clare and saw her jaw tighten.

“With all due respect, sir,” Clare said, “I’m not sure I’m the right choice for this mission.”

“You can say that again,” Fallon added.

Dr. Stallard shot Fallon an angry glance for the interjection in front of the general. She shrugged.

Price cocked one of his aggressive white eyebrows, his intense blue eyes turning toward Clare. “Oh?” He held the printout in front of him and made a show of reading it. “Unless I’m mistaken—and perhaps I am at my age—it says your precision scores are quite high. That’s our key variable for hit jobs. We’ve had a rather narrow pool of your type, so you’ll be a useful contribution, soldier.”

Clare swallowed and nodded. “Yes, sir.”

Sara’s stomach clenched. Clare didn’t want to kill anyone in the first place, yet her first mission would be a string of assassinations deep in Iran? Fallon would likely handle most of the kills, with Dalia and Clare mainly there as backup, but even so . . .

“If I may speak up, sir,” Sara said, “I’m a capable sniper and trained infiltrator. I would volunteer to replace C14 if you deem it suitable.”

Price walked over and stared down at her, eye to eye. “I like the enthusiasm, soldier, but can you choke someone to death yet? Or stop their heart?”

“No, sir,” Sara admitted. “Not with my mind, at least. But I can provide sniper cover and scouting support.” It wasn’t that she wanted to kill, but rather that she wanted to go somewhere. And if she could get Clare out of the mission, then all the better. Clare wasn’t a killer. Sara could be, if she had to. Or so she hoped.

“This is an urban mission, soldier,” Price said. “We want the deaths to look natural. We’re sending three Class C operatives who can deliver that, for redundancy, with some experienced infiltrators to assist them, who already have on-the-ground experience in Iran. They don’t need a marginal super-soldier on this one, but as soon as we have a need for that capacity, I’ll let you know.”

A marginal super-soldier. That was how he viewed her. Worthless, in other words, like both Fallon and Jolce had told her on more than one occasion.

“C5, C10, and C14 will report to Colonel McClellon here for the details of the operation,” Price said, nodding toward a military man in his forties. Then he looked at the doctor. “Stallard, join me for a drink in your office.”







Chapter 15



Jade ascended the stairs from the underground Pentagon metro station.

That was what the media called her, anyway. Jade. She used to care about her chosen name, but not anymore. As far as she was concerned, they could call her whatever they wanted now. Number, name, whichever. After all that had happened, she preferred thinking of her old self as dead.

Her hair was bound tightly into a bun, she had Jitters’s large-framed sunglasses covering the top half of her face, and she wore a medical mask over her mouth and nose. A knee-length coat, fit for the autumn weather, concealed her armored vest. Cameras were all over this world, and everyone was searching for her, so she had to shift her appearance where she could.

Now that she’d reached her target, the cameras weren’t the problem. The guards were. She stripped off her mask to look less suspicious as she approached the main entrance.



“All I’m saying,” Asim said, “is that I think we should—”

A blast went off somewhere on the lower floors, sending a vibration through the room. He stopped speaking as both Flanagan’s and Ava’s eyes went wide, as did the eyes of the three advisors to the Army’s Chief of Staff whom Asim had been briefing.

They all stared at him, as if time itself had frozen. A tightness spread through his chest, and his throat went dry. “Go tell our people to get under their desks,” he said. “Don’t go into the corridors.”

He rushed to grab his laser rifle, then strode toward the doors that led out into the corridor as he put his arms through the loops of the energy pack.

“Colonel. Asim,” Flanagan said, raising his voice, “You can’t be serious. They have a guard division for that.”

“I might be able to reason with her,” Asim said. “I can’t do nothing.”

Flanagan shook his head. “We don’t even know if—”

“It’s her. Almost certainly. Assume it is unless we learn differently.”

Asim peeked into the corridor and spotted two women running. From the other direction came distant shouting. Most people in the building were civilians and would be panicking right now. He wasn’t sure if he was lucky or cursed to have been in the Pentagon today instead of back in ACI headquarters.

“Sir, I can help,” Ava said from behind. She already had her concealed pistol out.

“I know you can. But please, stay here,” Asim said. “That’s an order. We’ve only got one of these anyway.” He powered on the laser rifle.

Despite the Pentagon’s massive size, Asim knew it was possible to walk between any two points in under seven minutes, thanks to its design. And unfortunately, he also knew that security at the military headquarters was weaker than what one might expect against a threat like Jade.

Sure, the building itself was a fortress of security. Made of concrete, steel, and even Kevlar, the walls were resilient against most attacks. The windows were blast resistant. The airspace above was strictly controlled, and nearby military bases could respond rapidly in the event of an outright foreign invasion. But it was ultimately an office building, and the hundreds of guards who worked here were civilian personnel, like regular police officers trained to deal with normal threats.

One person breaking in, who could stop bullets and throw cars? With plenty of walls for cover? He pictured the scene outside of the news building, where she’d killed all those cops and ripped apart the street in seconds. How many waves of guards would die here today?

Instinctively, he rushed toward the outer ring and down to the third floor, where most VIP offices were located. Then he thought more consciously. What would she be going after here, specifically?

The Secretary of Defense on the third floor? The Joint Chiefs on the second? They were located on the middle floors for security purposes. Or was Jade just trying to cause as much mayhem as possible to draw attention?

A second impact of some sort blasted in the distance, and he dashed that way, feeling another vibration rumble through the floor under his feet.

Moments later, the broadcast system sounded. “Active shooter alert,” said the automated female voice. “Personnel on the first floor should calmly exit the building. Personnel on the second through fifth floors should remain in their offices with the doors locked. Shelter in place. This is not a drill. I repeat, active shooter alert.”

That likely meant she was on the second floor. Gunfire echoed up from a stairwell, so he rushed down the stairs toward the sound. Two guards with pistols stood at the intersection between the stairwell and the second-floor corridor, a young woman and a young man. As Asim approached, the man reached out his palm, tense.

“Stop,” the male guard said. “Go back upstairs to your office, sir.”

“I’m Colonel Asim Rahal, the deputy commander of Army Counterintelligence, ACI. What’s happening?”

The female guard peeked into the corridor. Gunshots and yelling sounded from deep down the hall, and she turned back, pale. “It’s that witch. She’s on this floor somewhere.” The woman eyed Asim’s laser rifle, then glanced down at the badge on his belt. “Official policy for floors two through five is to shelter in place unless you’re part of the Pentagon Force Protection Agency, sir.”

“Well, I’m going to go ahead and ignore official policy today,” Asim said. “I’m in charge of her case. What’s the state of the floor?”

“I don’t know, sir. We’re escorting people to safety if they’re in the corridors. The main force is moving to protect the SecDef and the Joint Chiefs. And to drop the attacker.”

Asim nodded and crept out into the corridor, laser rifle ready. More gunfire and screams echoed ahead.



Jade crushed their heads, several at a time, with her right focus, her right fist at her side making a gripping motion each time. As she walked down the corridor, the instant she saw anyone with a gun, their head got crushed. Instantly. Again, and again.

For those who managed to get a shot off first, she used her left focus to maintain a high-energy outward-facing shield around herself, stopping bullets before they struck.

She left behind a sea of red as she moved along the outer ring of the building. It was a shame, but there was no time to waste.



With his laser rifle, Asim crept down a corridor filled with dozens of guards’ bodies, their heads reduced to bloody pulp and bone shards. The rest stop scene had been disgusting, but this was out of a nightmare. Literal streams of blood flowed down the shiny floors of the Pentagon’s halls, reflecting ceiling lights in a grotesque sheen. An unnatural chill hung in the air.

He passed two clerks huddled together against a wall, red-stained and in shock but otherwise unharmed.

“How long?” Asim asked softly.

One stared blankly at the floor as if she hadn’t even heard. The other hesitated, then met his eyes. “May . . . maybe a minute.”

Asim nodded and looked forward. Jade appeared to be heading toward the Joint Chiefs’ office suite. Hopefully they’d evacuated, but the strike had happened so fast, and with a security directive to shelter in place, they might have barricaded in with a retinue of guards. A gut-wrenching thought. He clicked the button to charge his rifle’s capacitor bank and set his finger on the trigger.

Several jogging footsteps sounded behind him. Turning to look, he nodded to an approaching set of four heavily armed guards, three men and a woman, all with determination on their faces. They said nothing, trying to be quiet, and fell in behind him.

A burst of gunfire erupted ahead, close, and a loud bang rang out, echoing through the halls, followed by sounds of shattering glass, grinding metal, and more screams. That was where the Joint Chiefs’ office suites were, with what was likely a concentrated force.

Asim reached a corner, took a quiet breath, and peeked around it. He pulled right back as he glimpsed her about fifty feet away.

Jade stood amid piles of bloody bodies, the floor slick with red. She wore similar attire as last time but with a different coat over her armored vest, and her hair was pulled back into a tight bun, with long black strands starting to come loose. She faced a grand doorway that led to the office suite of General Price—the Vice Chairman of the Joint Chiefs of Staff. Around her, alongside the ruined bodies, lay a series of twisted metal frames from what had once been blast-resistant glass doors in the corridor, serving as an interior VIP security checkpoint.

Asim turned back toward the guards behind him, widened his eyes, and put out his hand to signal them to stop. Not a sound, he tried to make clear.

Jade’s appearance differed from how she’d looked at the news station. Thinner. Gaunt, even. But still the same person, with the same scars. Something was wrong. And somehow, that made her more threatening, not less. A starving, haggard lioness was more dangerous than a healthy one. She was speckled in drops of her victims’ blood. Her boots were covered in it.

This was his chance. Hopefully she couldn’t control photons. If she could, he would die, simple as that. As he took a deep breath, his daughter’s face flashed in his mind. He should’ve called her the other day on the train. Now look at this mess.

He exhaled and stepped out to shoot. A spike of adrenaline hit him, and time subjectively slowed down. As he took a half-second to aim, Jade glanced his way, and her right hand started to move. He fired prematurely, prioritizing speed over accuracy. All the energy stored in the rifle’s capacitor bank discharged into a powerful, invisible laser blast.

A sharp crack split the air.

Jade staggered back and gasped as a jet of golden sparks burned out from her right shoulder, straight through her armor. Then she bared her teeth with a savage glare and clenched her fist. Asim had already started ducking back behind the corner after the shot, and he felt the air implode right next to his head as he lost sight of her. There was no other way to describe it.

After a few seconds, a delayed growl of pain or frustration echoed from around the corner, and the air filled with the scent of ozone. Asim took several silent steps back and crouched low to change his position. He wasn’t sure how precisely her telekinesis could strike from around corners, without a sightline.

“Jade!” he shouted, clicking the button with his thumb to recharge the rifle’s capacitor bank. “I’m Asim, from the opera. Surrender yourself and I’ll do everything in my power to get to the bottom of this and help you if I can!”

Silence. Then the sound of wet bootsteps coming toward him. Fuck.

He raised his rifle, ready to fire as soon as the first glimpse of her came around the corner. The guards behind him did the same. But then more gunfire sounded from near Jade’s position, followed by an explosion of rubble and screams, rattling the floor itself. A squad had likely attacked her from the other direction, and she was fighting back.

Asim forced himself to step up and peek around the corner again to see if he could land a second shot, but as soon as he did, the barrel of his laser rifle crushed in on itself from a strong invisible force, rendering the gun useless. Nearly at the same time, a jolting upward force on his chest pulled him off the floor and around the corner like a rag doll, whiplashing his head back.

Jade stood in the corridor, one arm outstretched, her face locked on him. His body flew toward her. Part of the ceiling had collapsed in the corridor behind her; she’d ripped down concrete and steel to crush an oncoming guard squad and block the path with rubble. Debris still clattered down the pile, settling. Those predatory eyes studied him as he drew closer to her, then widened. He dropped to the floor, prone, released from her grip. Gunfire rang out overhead. Two guards who’d been with him were firing, their rounds pinging uselessly off an invisible barrier around her body. Then, both of their helmets ruptured into a crimson spray.

Asim clenched his jaw, furious. There were still two more hiding around the corner, unless they’d already fled. “Stay back!” he shouted, heart pounding. “Wait for backup.”

He tried to stand but was struck by an invisible blow to his chest, knocking the wind out of him. He gasped for air on his hands and knees, but his lungs wouldn’t take it in. Behind him, the crashing sound of concrete and the grinding sound of steel filled the corridor, and the floor rattled beneath him from the impact. Hopefully the other two guards had fled in time. She was ripping down part of the ceiling to block the direction he’d come from, too. With this, she’d be able to go through the doors here and assault General Price’s office suite without being flanked by more waves of guards from either side of the corridor.

As the sound and vibration of crashing concrete and steel came to a pause, Asim finally managed to take in a gasp of air and rise to his knees. Smelling burnt flesh, he slowly raised his eyes.

Jade stared down at him, her shoulder scorched, with a look of hesitation on her face. Then her expression hardened, and an invisible blow struck him on the side of the head, knocking him back down.

Through his blurry vision, he watched Jade walk away from him toward the doorway. One of the bloody bodies under the collapsed rubble on the far side moaned and stirred. She paused, glanced at the squirming guard, and crushed his head even though he was no longer a threat. Then she turned back to the doorway.



Jade pushed, and the thick wooden double doors splintered inward, broke into several pieces, and flew with fatal speed into the office. She kept her second focus ready to block any gunfire that might come, and as expected, some did.

She crushed one guard’s head, then two more. They fired from behind desks, tables, and chairs in the grand office. A fourth ducked back behind a desk before she could get a mental grip on him, so she pushed the desk with enough force to crush him against the concrete wall instead.

Stepping inside, she levitated a table, turned it on its side, and put it against the door frame she’d come through. In doing so, she revealed a young woman, likely some sort of secretary, covered in blood and weeping next to the headless body of one of the guards.

“Price,” Jade said, raising her voice.

Quiet, except for the helicopter blades outside and the stifled sobs of the woman. Jade’s right shoulder flared in pain, masked only by the adrenaline flowing through her veins, which wouldn’t last much longer. This had taken too long already.

After a few seconds, the general stood from behind the room’s main desk. The tall, imperialistic-looking bald man, with his blue eyes as piercing as ever. He seemed defiant and tense, rather than afraid.

“All this for me?” he asked. “Stallard’s right. You’ve lost your damn mind.”



Disoriented, Asim crawled through the muck past several bodies toward the doorway of the Vice Chairman’s office suite. He could stand, but he didn’t want footsteps giving him away. As he reached it, he found a large round table blocking most of his path, but could hear through it and peek in at the bottom corner, where the round table didn’t fully plug the wide, rectangular doorframe.

“Are they all still at the Stolguard facility?” Jade asked. “Or have they relocated?”

“Why should I tell you anything?” General Price responded.

“Because I can kill you slowly and gruesomely, one question at a time, if you don’t.”

Price eyed her and somehow stood up a little straighter than he already had been. “There’s no need for that. Yes, they’re still at the facility. And they’ve recalled more from the field to defend it. It’s impenetrable now.”

Asim slowly unholstered his pistol, trying to remain as noiseless as possible, but Jade glanced back toward the doorway, straight at him through the gap. The hair on his neck rose.

“Stand down, soldier,” Price said. “It won’t do either of us any good.”

Asim nodded slightly, his pulse racing.

Jade’s unblinking glare lingered on his face. “Throw it away,” she said.

Asim tossed his pistol down the hall.

Jade looked back toward Price. “Who else knows about the program details?” she asked, her voice low and tense.

“Mostly senior officers at the Special Operations Command,” Price said. “SOCOM.”

“Do you know what Stallard is doing with Class D?”

“The computer hookups? That’s Stallard’s pet project. I let him keep it in exchange for his other output. Class D hasn’t had military applications yet.”

“It isn’t meant for military applications. It’s worse, and closer to completion than you think. What he’s doing could disrupt the world. It needs to be stopped, or people need to be warned how to prepare for it.”

“More of you are delusional than I thought,” Price said.

“It’ll turn all your weapon systems against you. Against everyone. Pull funding from the facility, let the subjects go free if they want to, and have Congress review what’s happened with Class D.”

“You’re right. Will do, soldier,” Price said.

Jade paused. Even from behind, Asim could tell she was breathing heavily. The back of her shoulder had a burn hole in it; the laser blast had gone straight through. The fact that she was still standing was inhuman. “You’re lying,” she said after several seconds. Her voice deepened further, almost like a growl.

“Of course I am,” Price said. “You think I can just magically disappear a trillion-dollar program? We don’t negotiate with terrorists, and for every one of us you kill, there’s another—”

His head imploded into a bloody mess, splattering the windows behind him, and his body fell to the floor with a wet thud.



Jade scanned the ruined office. That secretary was still cowering in the corner, and that so-called detective was lingering back in the corridor. They’d make useful witnesses for what they’d heard.

She pushed against one of the office windows with enough force to break the blast-resistant glass, shattering it outward. Its shards rained down to the parking lot below as she stepped closer, shielding herself.

Within seconds, the sound of rifle shots echoed through the air, and several high-powered sniper rounds streaked toward her at the window. She blocked them with her left focus and pushed her eyes to their limit, scanning the parking lot for the source. She spotted the sniper far away behind a car, a glint of light giving him away, and crushed his head with her right focus.

The helicopter hovering overhead pivoted, and she kept an eye on it while also looking for other snipers. There weren’t any more of them on this side, but six security drones thrummed throughout the parking lot within sight. She crushed them all. Then she planted both of her bracers on the ground below and jumped down, using one of her foci to pull up on her body, slowing her descent and landing safely.

The helicopter’s heavy machine gun opened fire.

Rather than let her shield keep getting pounded, she released it and instead reached out to blast with her right focus. A narrow beam of force lanced toward the helicopter, shredding the hail of fire and hammering the gun. At the same time, with her left focus, she tore the helicopter from the sky. It crashed into the parking lot like an explosive meteor, sending a light through the sky and a shockwave through the ground under her feet. The air turned frigid, and frost spread outward in a widening radius along the asphalt.

A roar of fighter jets thundered in the distance. Then another roar seconds later, rumbling from the opposite direction. There’d be an unassailable amount of metal in the sky within minutes, coming to the Pentagon’s defense.

With her bracers still planted, she used one of her foci to lift herself by multiple points on her body and fly horizontally like a bullet, headfirst, arms tucked tight, skimming the building’s side, accelerating as she went. With her other focus, she ripped several cars from the parking lot. Metal groaned as they surged toward her. Once they got close, she used dynamic inclusion to hold the cars with the same focus she held her own body with, locking them into formation with her, using them to shield her open side as she flew.

She rounded one of the Pentagon’s five corners and saw the metro station up ahead. More sniper rounds echoed through the air, punching into her car-shield with sharp, metallic pops.

A police blockade had set up at the metro entrance, so as she got close, she slowed herself down and released the cars to let them careen ahead of her. Guards scattered, diving for cover as the cars crashed through their blockade at deadly speed, mowing down anyone too slow. Screams filled the air.

She soared past what little remained of them, down the steps to the metro, then entered the underground train track tunnel itself. She released and replanted her bracers to avoid straining their length from where she’d started.

An oncoming train sped toward her. She hugged the far-right wall of the tunnel and kept flying. Concrete sped by inches from her right shoulder, train metal inches from her left. After it passed, she veered back to the center and reaccelerated, eyes forward, breath ragged.

Her adrenaline was gone. Agony radiated from her shoulder, dizzying and raw. Every tiny motion of her arm caused her vest to scrape against the burn, lighting her nerves on fire anew. Even her head throbbed now, from so much use of her powers.

She clenched her jaw and focused, then laughed out loud. Not out of joy or excitement, but to distract herself from the pain. There was more work to do, and she couldn’t rest here.







Part II





The Tapestry








Chapter 16





Three Years Earlier


The generals need to see spectacles from time to time to keep them engaged, and to keep the money flowing toward more important things.

–TS log 12453, excerpt

Damien went to the cafeteria for breakfast before General Price’s visit. As usual, several tables sat empty, since most subjects were away on fieldwork, and far fewer guards and researchers ate breakfast here than lunch.

One table had Maria, Dalia, Clare, and Romulus. A second had Quinn with a few members of Class B. Jolce sat alone, reading manga as she often did. A few other tables held off-duty guards and researchers. Sara was unfortunately in the firing range; otherwise Damien would have eaten with her.

He slid his tray onto an empty table and pulled out a chair. The lights in here were blinding, and he still felt dizzy from yesterday’s tests. It was surprising he had any appetite at all.

“Hey! Over here,” Clare called, gesturing to a single empty seat beside her.

Damien sighed. He liked Clare, of course, though her energy level could be overwhelming at times. And Maria was fine. But Romulus and Dalia had never been comfortable around him. Many subjects felt uneasy around Class D, and some of it was probably deserved; Damien wasn’t exactly the best at relating to most of them. Sara understood him better than the others, and yet he could tell he alienated even her at times.

Still, he walked over, and Clare lit up with excitement. The other three just kept eating, but Clare rested her fork on her tray.

“I’m glad you could join us. I thought you were going to eat in your room. Sara said she would bring you snacks.”

Damien slid into his seat and scanned the table. “I told her I was recovered enough to come myself, so she could still get her morning session in the range before Price arrives.”

Dalia, with her short-cropped black hair, looked up from her food. “She’s obsessed. She thinks better marksmanship will get her into the field, but it won’t.”

Clare frowned. “We don’t know that for sure. She’s being constructive about it. All anyone can do is take—”

“One step at a time, I know,” Dalia interrupted. She looked down at her plate again and took another bite. Romulus chuckled.

“She’s training as best as she can,” Clare said with a rare edge to her voice.

Dalia nodded. “I know. Her range scores are already up there with Class B, somehow. Good for her. That’s not the issue. The telekinesis is. No getting around that.”

Romulus still looked amused. His brown hair draped past his chin as he glanced around the cafeteria, and he brushed some of it back behind one ear.

“What?” Clare asked.

“I’m just picturing that time Carter nearly flattened her. None of you ladies bother to spar anymore, except Dal here sometimes, so Sara tried to train with some Class B guys. That lasted all of thirty seconds once she got in the ring with Carter. So, she went to Raphael and asked to spar some of his normie guards. She fights a few of them each week in the gym now, mostly men. Beats the shit out of some of them, too.”

“Still won’t get her into the field. What’s she going to do, punch drones?” Dalia said.

“She fucked you up last time you boxed her, darling,” Romulus said.

Dalia glared back. “You know what I mean. She’s barely more than a regular soldier. They’re not going to risk losing her body and all its genes in enemy territory. They only send in our kind when we’ll actually make a difference. It’s not like I’m saying it’s her fault. Just reality.”

Damien thought for a moment. It would be socially optimal to change the subject. “I wonder if anything will change, now that General Price has been promoted to the Joint Chiefs,” he offered as a conversation piece. The news had been circulating around the facility.

“I doubt it,” Maria said after she finished chewing a bite. “At least for us. But him moving from SOCOM to the Joint Chiefs probably ties this facility closer to the President and shields us from the few generals who contest our existence. He’ll probably bring his SOCOM replacement today. I heard it’s General Rodriguez. Supposedly Atlas Rionz is coming too.”

Romulus shrugged. “Yeah, we don’t work with the generals in the field anyway. We deal with spec-ops colonels and their subordinates.” He eyed Dalia’s tray and gestured toward an untouched piece of sausage with his fork. “You’re not going to eat that?”

“No, go ahead,” she said, and caressed the back of his shoulder. He speared the sausage with his fork and brought it to his plate, then leaned over and pecked a kiss onto her cheek.

According to what Sara recently told him, Romulus and Dalia had started dating a month ago. Sara had been amused when reminding him that Romulus had dated nearly a third of Class C by now. Damien wouldn’t normally track such things, but her keeping him informed certainly helped for social situations like this.

There was only one woman Damien desired in that way, and she was in the firing range right now. But he needed some sense of the future before pursuing anything like a relationship. What could he even offer her, stuck in a prison as they were? He had agonizing experiments with Thane every week, spent days in bed afterward, and found it increasingly hard to interact with others. He needed to understand where his future lay. Would he die like Rudolph? If not, what would success even look like for his class? Class B and Class C both led to fieldwork. Class D only led deeper into machines.

“Did you hear me?” Clare asked, her hand already out, touching his shoulder.

Damien blinked and looked up. Had he zoned out again? “No, sorry. Can you repeat your prior statement?”

Romulus rolled his eyes, and Dalia glared at him for doing so, suggesting that she wanted him to conceal his social frustration for Damien’s sake. That was nice of her. Maria just watched Damien with quiet curiosity.

“Of course,” Clare said, patient as ever. “I said General Price’s event is in ten minutes. Want to walk there with us? We’re going to pick up Marcus and head there now.”

“Thank you,” Damien said. “But I’ll stop by the range and get Sara, then meet you there.” He smiled in a way that he hoped looked warm enough.



Sara stood in line in the enclosed sports and testing field with the other ten subjects of Class B and Class C who weren’t out on missions. General Price, General Rodriguez, and a group of other military officers and government administrators observed. Damien stood off to the side watching as well; he had accompanied her here from the firing range. Marcus stood next to him.

For the first time, a famous tech mogul named Atlas was attending. He wore neither a uniform nor a suit, just a white sweater in a room full of formally dressed VIPs. But what really stood out was his gynoid companion. A robot in the shape of a woman, she was visibly metallic with no fake skin, and she moved with such grace compared to any other bot Sara had seen. She spoke to other people around Atlas charismatically when addressed, her facial expressions shockingly genuine. At several points she outright laughed, and even lightly touched a man’s shoulder once in conversation. Atlas himself spoke little, except with Price and Rodriguez. The gynoid seemed to serve as his assistant, but the lines on her forearms suggested concealed weaponry. A bodyguard too, perhaps?

The gynoid glanced at Sara and smiled, catching her staring. Oops. Sara looked away toward the field. Through her peripheral vision, she could see the gynoid’s gaze linger on her for a few more seconds before returning to Atlas.

These semiannual events were shorter now that all subjects had graduated. Rather than test them all as he used to, Dr. Stallard would just have a single member of each combat class demonstrate their abilities for first-time visitors. This time, it was Maria and Romulus.

Officers had previously used these events to pick subjects for missions, but now that they were familiar with the program, spec-ops officers came whenever they had missions to fill. They never seemed to need Sara, though. She was now the only member of Class B or C who hadn’t been deployed at all.

The frustrating event was ready to wrap up, but then General Price spoke. “I’d like to see a duel between two Class Cs,” he said. “The only duel we’ve ever seen was back when C5 roughed up C17, which doesn’t count.”

Sara cringed.

“That would . . .” Dr. Stallard began, then paused. “A serious duel comes with significant safety concerns,” he continued after a moment. “Both for the subjects and for the audience. Their power levels leave little margin for error.”

“Stallard, we send these soldiers to the worst parts of the world to kill. They live for a little margin of error, don’t you ladies?” Price scanned the subjects, then turned back toward Dr. Stallard. “You’re telling me they can’t whack each other a bit? What’s the dueling rule again? Just take the other off their feet, right? Let’s show Mr. Rionz.”

“I challenge C3,” Jolce announced. The room went silent. Sara looked over at Jolce, feeling her own eyes go wide. That would be epic. Maria and Jolce were the two strongest telekineticists after Fallon, who wasn’t present and whom nobody would dare challenge anyway. Maria had always been considered the second strongest of Class C, but Jolce, Zarina, and Quinn were all close behind, classified alongside Maria in tier four.

Maria looked down to her right at Jolce, annoyance clear on her face. She hated duels. They were standing right next to each other in numbered order, and Maria stood half a head taller. Jolce looked up to her left, meeting Maria’s eyes, and smirked.

Between telekineticists, size didn’t matter. Only their mental powers did. Jolce was a bully, much like Fallon, though less dramatically. Hopefully Maria would win.

Price gestured toward Maria and Jolce and raised his eyebrows at Dr. Stallard, as though he’d just won the argument.

“General, I can’t allow a duel between C3 and C6 on the spot like this. I wouldn’t be able to guarantee the safety of anyone present. Each of them can channel more horsepower than a supercar.”

Price said nothing, just crossed his arms and looked at the doctor with those fierce blue eyes and impossibly aggressive white eyebrows.

Dr. Stallard stared back at Price for a moment, as stone-faced as ever, then glanced toward the line of Class C subjects. “C10 and C14, please step up here.”

Dalia and Clare. Sara shook her head. Dueling was the last thing Clare would want, and Dalia was the more powerful of the two. At least Dalia wasn’t cruel; she wouldn’t play with Clare after winning.

“I have no dispute with Clare, sir,” Dalia said. “We have no reason to duel.”

“That’s precisely why I want you to do so,” Dr. Stallard said. “And as third tiers, your duel will be milder and more contained than one between C3 and C6. Please step out here, twenty yards apart.”

Jolce grinned down the line toward Dalia and Clare. Sara sighed. What a backhanded compliment on Dr. Stallard’s part.

“Yes, sir,” Dalia said, ignoring Jolce and marching out onto the field.

After a moment, Clare followed, looking calmer than Sara expected, given the situation. Dalia was not only stronger at telekinesis and a year older than Clare, she was more competitive, too. She had the second-highest marksmanship score in Class C, and kept up with martial arts better than most of the class. That made her one of the most well-rounded telekinetic soldiers. Not a heavy hitter like Fallon or Maria, but versatile. Comfortable with killing, too. Clare had told Sara all the stories from Iran.

“C3, please come out here to help as well,” Dr. Stallard said. Maria raised her eyebrows, then stepped onto the field without a word.

“The number one goal is to do no serious damage,” Dr. Stallard said, looking at Dalia and then Clare. “Not to each other, not to this room, and especially not to any observers. The second goal is to try to safely knock your opponent to the ground. Do you understand?”

“Yes, sir!” Dalia shouted.

“I do,” Clare said.

“And you, C3. Since you can see their foci, you may intervene if it gets too dangerous.”

“I’ll do my best,” Maria said. “Damage can happen quickly.”

Dr. Stallard sighed and turned back to the audience. “Unfortunately, you won’t be able to see most of the duel, which along with the safety risk is why I don’t typically offer them to observers. Telekineticists can directly see each other’s psionic limbs while we cannot, and they have reflexes that are as fast as cats’. The rest of us will mostly only see the end result.”

“Well, that’s a damn good sales pitch, Doc,” General Rodriguez said. “Let’s let it play out.”

Price smirked and looked over at General Rodriguez and Atlas Rionz.

Sara watched them. So that was Price’s angle? Marketing their abilities?

“Prepare yourselves,” Dr. Stallard said.

Dalia narrowed her eyes, stood straight with her hands clenched into fists at her sides, and planted both of her bracers onto the ground like ethereal legs that only members of Class C could see. Clare bent her knees slightly and kept her palms open, looking more relaxed. Then she planted her bracers, too.

Dr. Stallard held out his hand. “Three . . . Two . . . One . . . Go.”

Dalia shot both of her foci straight toward Clare like twin punches, ethereal arms reaching out nearly as fast as bullets. Sara gritted her teeth. With their power difference, Clare couldn’t block them, and if she were to try, Dalia might push too hard and break through with enough force to injure her. Or worse.

Instead, Clare flicked her palms up and used her two foci to push up hard on both of Dalia’s oncoming foci. Clare ducked into a deep crouch, and Dalia’s two foci glanced upward, flying inches over her head by the time they reached her position.

Quinn gasped. Sara felt her own mouth open in surprise as well. Clare knew she couldn’t block, of course; she’d redirected Dalia’s push slightly upward, which took far less energy. Ducking had minimized how much redirection she’d need to apply, too. It was risky, though—a miscalculation would have resulted in her taking the full unblocked push to the face.

Clare stepped forward, still low, moving smoothly like water. She thrust her arms in front of her, fingers out, and pushed her two foci straight toward Dalia at full speed. Dalia’s eyes went wide, and she pulled back her foci to “grab” Clare’s foci, stopping them a few yards before reaching her.

They were now locked in a full telekinetic grapple, with Dalia using both of her foci to hold back both of Clare’s. Then, Clare splayed her fingers on each hand, and her foci split into five parts each like ethereal fingers, with all ten of them spearing toward Dalia.

Dalia, despite being more powerful, clearly had trouble splitting her concentration into that many parts. As she gritted her teeth and made grasping motions with her hands, each of her foci split into only four “fingers” and grabbed eight of Clare’s to block them. The last two parts slipped through and struck Dalia on her chest.

Dalia gasped and staggered back from the impact, but stayed upright. Clare didn’t have enough power with those remaining two partial foci to knock her off her feet for the win. Still, Sara considered the duel won. Clare could have narrowed those two “fingers” into needle-like spikes and pierced them through Dalia’s flesh. The same power would have been condensed into smaller points. But Clare was too kind for something like that, and she’d followed Dr. Stallard’s directions to prioritize safety over winning.

Dalia bit her lip, clenched her fists, and pushed back hard against Clare, overpowering her. Yard by yard, she moved the connection point of their foci toward Clare and away from herself. Clare merged her mental fingers back into just two foci to concentrate on power for defense, clenching her jaw as she struggled to hold back Dalia’s oncoming push. Sara winced at how badly both of their heads must be throbbing from the effort.

When the connection point was only a few yards away from Clare, Dalia yelled and punched her fists forward. Her two foci turned into thick, sharp, ethereal spears and broke through Clare’s remaining hold, straight toward her chest.

“No!” Sara shouted in the quiet field. Several observers turned to look at her in surprise.

Maria had already clenched both her fists, and her two foci flashed outward. Dalia’s ethereal spears stopped inches from Clare’s chest, held back at the last second by both of Maria’s foci. Clare had taken a big step back as well; if she hadn’t, Maria likely wouldn’t have stopped them in time.

Sara exhaled. Thank you, Maria. Several of the observers were looking at Sara in response to her outburst, not having seen the nearly fatal hit on Clare. Other than the few hand gestures and steps the duelists made, and a slight chill in the air, all of this had been undetectable to them.

After a few seconds, Dalia’s eyes went wide, and she dismissed her foci. “I—I’m sorry,” she said. “I didn’t mean it.”

Clare appeared to be in shock, taking a few deep breaths and still staring down at where Dalia had nearly impaled her chest moments ago.

“What happened, C3?” Dr. Stallard asked. His voice was raised in a way that was rare for him.

“It . . . I’d say it’s a draw,” Maria said. “Both of them had fatal opportunities.” She eyed Dalia, and Dalia’s face paled in response.

Clare eased her shoulders, looked across the field at Dalia, and nodded with a relieved smile. Dalia nodded back, furrowed her brow, and silently mouthed, Sorry.

Dr. Stallard took off his glasses, rubbed his eyes, and stared back at Price. “This, General, is why we don’t do duels.”



Fallon was supposed to have killed Mr. Gordy fifteen minutes ago, but it was just so unsporting. Not a single thing challenging or interesting about it.

The fat man sat two rows up next to his wife, with part of his left butt cheek hanging over the edge of an aisle seat. A likely candidate for a heart attack, and they’d wanted this one to be in public to minimize suspicion. The crew had served the in-flight meal, which was when she’d been told to strike. If she hesitated much longer, they’d start collecting the trays before the deed was done.

Earlier this year, after all sorts of outcry, Congress had passed the Digital Rights Act to protect citizens’ supposed privacy with a bunch of legal constraints. But this guy, an engineer at a government contractor, had recently started a whistleblower campaign against some secret surveillance backdoors that the government had put in place as a workaround. The NSA had quietly offered him a payout to shut up about it, but he’d refused.

Now, here she was. Whether right or not, the moron should’ve taken the hint that yes, the suits had some fangs.

They’d originally picked Clare for this, given how easy the mission was on the surface. But she’d asked so many questions during the briefing that they worried she’d chicken out. And they were right—the girl still hadn’t popped her murder-cherry yet, even in Iran, and this wasn’t the right type of target to start with. So, the suits had decided to swap in Fallon at the last minute, for a mission that was beneath her.

She sighed, eyeing the man. There was nothing more to contemplate. As displeasing as this hit was, they knew she’d get it done. Between bites, he glanced at his wife and said something about the pasta, then smiled. Fallon reached out with one of her foci, felt for his heartbeat, and squeezed.







Chapter 17



Despite his concussion, Asim kept reviewing the camera footage in a spare conference room, preparing for his upcoming questioning.

That seemed like half of what he’d been doing lately: reviewing footage. Always on the back foot. He’d seen parts of the Pentagon attack closer than anyone else, but watching various angles of the strike had helped him piece together more details in the following days.

The nation was in an official state of emergency, the Secret Service had transferred the President from the White House to an undisclosed location, and pressure had mounted, with ACI becoming the scapegoat.

Jade had emerged from the Pentagon metro station and blasted her way through the main entrance with sheer force, wrecking concrete, metal, Kevlar, and human lives in the process. The cameras there were destroyed, but she reappeared on the second-floor cameras a minute later. From there, she moved rapidly around the outer ring, murdering waves of Pentagon police and making her way to General Price. They never stood a chance. Then she broke through an unbreakable window and flew down into the metro tunnels just before heavier reinforcements arrived.

Because the attack had unfolded so quickly, shelter-in-place orders had been issued for VIPs and most of the building. The Secretary of Defense and the various military chiefs had barricaded themselves in their offices, with forces concentrated there to protect them. Other teams were directed toward Jade’s position as cameras tracked her movement. But all prior procedures for handling an attacker assumed no supernatural abilities. Normally this defense would have been more than enough, but the power density brought through these halls exceeded what anyone thought was possible.

Sixty-four Pentagon police, seven civilian contractors at the entrance, three occupants of the attack helicopter, and General Price were dead. Seventy-five people who had just been doing their fucking jobs.

And yet, Asim found himself dwelling on the fact that she’d spared him for some reason, even after he’d shot her. He was only here today because she allowed it. Little else about her was merciful. Had it been simply due to recognition, from the opera?

“It’s time, Colonel,” Brigadier General Davis said from the doorway, his tone unusually formal. “They’re waiting for you.”

Asim sucked in a breath and stood up. “Yes, sir.”

They walked through the undamaged portion of the Pentagon, toward a large room on the third floor flanked by six guards with laser rifles. Davis briefly put a hand on the back of Asim’s shoulder in encouragement as they reached the doors. One guard saluted crisply and led them inside.

The Secretary of Defense, seven of the eight Joint Chiefs, and a dozen aides filled the room; a flag and portrait of Price stood in place of the late Vice Chairman’s chair. Stallard and Major General Scholl both stood off to the side.

Asim saluted. “Colonel Rahal reporting as ordered.”

“At ease,” said the Chairman of the Joint Chiefs. General Rodham Dirk, the highest-ranking officer in the country.

“Yes, sir,” Asim said, as deferentially as possible.

“I find this situation . . . unique, Colonel,” Dirk said. “Your division is being reviewed for potential deficiencies in investigating this attack, yet your name personally has also been put forth as a candidate for either the Medal of Honor or the Distinguished Service Cross, the highest two decorations in the Army, for your insistent actions to go after the attacker above and beyond the call of duty.”

“I don’t deserve either one, sir. I just had the right weapon for the job, so I wanted to help if I could. And my division has done their best. Any fault in their investigation rests with me.”

“If I may, sir,” Davis said. “ACI issued notices days before the attack urging key government sites to acquire laser rifles, based on Colonel Rahal’s personal recommendation. He even insisted on getting one himself, having analyzed the nature of the threat.”

Davis, Asim thought. What a goddamn American hero, and a proper gentleman.

“And yet,” Dirk said, fixing his piercing eyes on Asim, “the attacker rampaged through these hallowed halls without warning, Colonel. Why was her next target so unknown?”

“She appeared fewer than three weeks ago, and has powers we don’t understand, sir,” Asim said. “The FBI was also blindsided by this attack, according to our joint task force contacts, and they’ve got a far larger team on this.”

Dirk laced his fingers in front of himself, elbows on the table, and raised an eyebrow. “Surely you have more to say than that, Colonel.”

Asim glanced at Stallard, then back toward Dirk. Alright, then. “Yes, sir. My division has been working overtime on this since the beginning, with as many resources as we could assign, but we were blocked by military courts and the Stolguard Biological Research Facility in accessing key intel. That slowed us down. I stand by everyone under my command, and I take the responsibility, but we were also impaired by external decisions outside our control, sir.” He rested his gaze on Stallard to make a point. The man didn’t flinch.

“Dr. Stallard. Remarks?” Dirk asked.

“Few that are safe to say in this full room, General,” Stallard said. “We’re selective about those who need to know, and not everyone here is vetted.”

Dirk just stared at him.

Stallard cleared his throat, then swept his eyes around the room before fixing them back on Dirk. “Over four decades ago, I convinced Liam Stolguard, a special operations colonel at the time, later a general, to join me in spearheading with the Joint Chiefs’ support, what was effectively a Manhattan Project for bioengineering supremacy over China and other nations. Back in the 2030s, when our country ran into a stalemate against China in the Pacific War due to their superior industrial base, we pushed senior leaders to lean into innovation. With breakthroughs in AI, gene editing, and brain mapping, we knew that with enough funding we could create super-soldiers, super-engineers, and others with superior capabilities for national security.”

“And I take it this clandestine program worked? That’s what this is?”

Stallard nodded. “It required time to mature, but over the past fifteen years, as subjects reached adulthood, it has borne fruit in the field. Hundreds of elite missions have been successfully carried out by the subjects, and they turned the tide in the flare-up between Belarus and Lithuania as well. This recent incident was our first and only security breach, and it’s related to an outlier biological success that exceeded our expectations. The late General Price helped ring-fence the program so this one-time problem wouldn’t compromise sensitive data.”

“We’ll discuss particulars of the program’s continuation later. I’ll say for the purposes of this room that I was aware of the program, though I didn’t know it was quite this extensive. What we need to know now,” Dirk said, rubbing his eyes with one hand, “is how we can bring this thing to justice. It’s only one person.”

Asim opened his mouth, but Stallard cut in. “Army Counterintelligence wasn’t the only group to release a memo about laser defense. My facility also provided similar guidance. We’ve been using such weaponry at our facility ever since they were developed. I recommend all top areas of government adopt laser rifle technology for this specific threat, since she cannot manipulate the movement of massless particles. In addition, I request broad intel sharing from other groups, particularly Army Counterintelligence, to track and eliminate her.”

“We already offer you intel sharing, Doctor,” Asim said. He hoped he didn’t sound as frustrated as he felt.

“We see that it’s discretionary, and not forthcoming,” Stallard said. “My team has to keep reaching out to yours for updates. For the sake of national security, I request direct core access for my facility to your full case database, so we can see it in real time.” He turned to Dirk. “I also request that increased surveillance capabilities be provided to my team, similar to those that ACI has access to. We have the resources necessary to eliminate the threat, but we need more tools to find her, which they’ve failed to do.”

Fuck, Asim thought. He still wanted some leeway to hold certain pieces of intel back. He didn’t trust Stallard.

“Generals,” Asim said. “I requested significant intel from Dr. Stallard, but he refused to provide almost any, beyond what was mostly obvious by that point. We’ve already given them more than they’ve given us. They only released laser guidance to other facilities after we did, likely because they had no choice at that point. And I’ve submitted a report describing the conversation between the attacker and the late General Price, which brought up additional national security concerns related to their facility.”

“Our research is worth trillions to foreign entities,” Stallard said. “We can’t release more than is needed to other divisions that could be compromised. It’s more fitting to give our facility additional surveillance capabilities than give the colonel’s division unnecessary access to proprietary biological research. From there it would spill into the FBI and elsewhere.”

Asim flinched. That argument would sway most here.

“All I can say, generals, is my agents have worked tirelessly on this,” Asim said. “But we haven’t been given key intel to complete the case on a fast enough timeline. The blockages have been unprecedented. We’ve been operating blind here.”

“I move to give Dr. Stallard’s facility core access to ACI data and surveillance capabilities for this case,” Dirk said. “And I will also recommend that the preliminary process moves forward for considering Colonel Rahal for either the Medal of Honor or the Distinguished Service Cross, pending all the proper reviews.”

“Agreed, General,” Secretary of Defense Sethram Goddard said.

Asim pinched the bridge of his nose and blurred through the next few minutes as they unfolded. This was too political for his taste, and his head was pounding. How was this possible? They were dangling a prestigious medal to pacify him while empowering Stallard’s facility.

“I will respectfully refuse any medal, sir,” Asim said, probably interrupting something important. “I can’t accept praise like that while my group is shamed. I would consider it a conflict of interest at this point.”

Dirk turned toward him, eyebrows raised. “You would decline the Medal of Honor or the Distinguished Service Cross in the name of the President of the United States, if awarded?”

“In this case, with full respect to all those present and to the President, yes, sir,” Asim said. “My personal efforts amounted to little. I shot the attacker, but I failed to protect the Vice Chairman. And I’m nothing more than what my division provides, while my division’s failure is what’s being highlighted here.”

There were various murmurs, then agreements by the other generals to delay the recommendation. To keep searching for answers, Asim couldn’t let anyone have a ceremonial grip over him. If that meant giving up external honors to retain his real honor, then so be it.



Ava wondered what was going on over there. Colonel Rahal was taking heat for her own failures. She had focused on his division’s data analysis for this case, and they lacked enough data to predict the attack, which was on her.

She wanted to drive over to the Pentagon and burst into the Joint Chiefs’ meeting to say the fault was hers, but that wasn’t how procedure worked. If there was one room she couldn’t find a way into, that was it. What if the colonel were to be removed from ACI? She had only joined because of him.

Every time in the past few weeks that she’d gone for a run, or read a book, or spent time with a friend, she could have been spending it on more analysis. She might have found a new clue that would have saved lives. She’d built custom pattern models, and cross-referenced street cameras with metro data and edge realm social posts, but none of it had been enough. Some of these deaths were on her for failing to figure out this case in time, and she nearly had a panic attack thinking about it. Brooks had brought her tea an hour ago, and she realized now that she’d left it untouched the whole time, letting it grow cold.

Finally, Colonel Rahal returned. “Well then,” he said, standing at their conference room doorway. “Not my best day.”

“Sir?” she asked, standing up. “What happened?” Several other analysts and investigators looked up as well.

“Nothing good,” he said with a smile, and raised his shoulders. “But not because of any of you. More of our remaining data is being shifted to Stallard’s facility. It’s political, and I’m shitty at politics, so this is on me. I’ll be paying the bar tab tonight, for any of you who want to join me.”







Chapter 18





Two Years Earlier


The earliest of our next three classes have been made. For a while this place began to feel almost like an empty nest, but no longer. The vision is beginning to take shape.

–TS log 12740, excerpt

Sara sat cross-legged on the bed in her dark room. The lights were off, but her eyes could see just fine in the faint glow of moonlight through the window.

In front of her, a small paper crane flapped its wings. It took off, flew in a circle, and landed back on the sheets. A headache was already forming, but she pushed herself like this a few times each week. For years now, she’d practiced with tennis balls or blobs of water in the jacuzzi, just like Clare had taught her.

Sara could now lift a tennis ball a few feet, and recently she had turned her attention to paper. A sheet weighed less than a tenth as much as a tennis ball, which let her practice precision movements with more repetition before exhaustion set in.

She ignored the pain and flew the crane again. With one focus, she lifted and moved the paper body, and with her second focus, she grabbed both wing tips and delicately flapped them up and down to make the flight look realistic. But as her headache sharpened, she sighed and landed it onto her bookshelf beside her other paper creations: a rose, a dragon, a butterfly, a frog, a chameleon, a fish, a plane, and others.

Once more. She gripped the crane with a focus again and lifted it an inch off the shelf. Then she split that focus to also include the dragon, lifting it an inch so the crane floated two inches high and the dragon one. Her headache worsened, pounding behind her eyes. Ignoring it, Sara split that focus twice more to add the butterfly and then the plane. She lifted all four, so that the crane levitated four inches off the shelf, the dragon three inches, the butterfly two, and the plane one. Then she dropped the plane back onto the shelf, followed by the butterfly, the dragon, and the crane, one at a time. She winced in pain, lightheaded. That was enough for now.

She had become proficient at dynamic inclusions and exclusions, able to add or drop objects without resetting a whole focus group. With Clare’s help, Sara wasn’t half bad at precision and advanced movements anymore, now that she practiced with water and paper so regularly, but she still had almost no power. Suitable for parlor tricks, basically.

Twenty years old and nothing to show for it. She’d graduated from development four years earlier, at sixteen, by skipping the telekinesis requirement, and yet had never once been assigned to the field. The officers who selected mission candidates had little use for a marginal super-soldier, as General Price had once called her.

He wasn’t wrong. Beyond moving tiny objects for seconds at a time, her only real powers were sharp eyesight, quick reflexes, and fast healing. But she was smaller than the average male soldier, let alone the giants of Class B. Her marksmanship was elite, but one skilled sniper didn’t interest spec-ops groups when they already had elite teams of their own. Risking her body and its genetic secrets getting into enemy hands wasn’t worth it to them, not without something bigger to gain from that risk. And every year without field experience left her further behind.

In her younger years, she’d imagined using her training and abilities to work around her limitations and become something like an infiltrator or “ninja,” but that vision had clearly been childish. It’d been every bit a fantasy as all the silly novels on her shelf.

Fallon, Maria, Santiago, Jax, and four others were on a multi-month mission in Eastern Europe, providing clandestine support for a war the United States wasn’t officially involved in. Sara had hoped to be chosen too, not because she wanted to fight, but because she wanted to see somewhere, anywhere, beyond this stale place. She still hadn’t been more than a mile from her own bed in any direction, and if she could help a country defend itself in some small way, that seemed worthwhile. It could matter to someone.

Did she have a team here? A sense of camaraderie? Barely. Bronn was almost always away on missions now, and Clare was gone half the time as well. Sara loved both of them as much as always, but they weren’t here. She helped take care of Marcus, eleven years old now, but he’d become so quiet since Stallard had begun his experiments on him, and his extreme intelligence limited what she could even offer. There were new classes, E, F, and G, for the first time in years, but the oldest among them were babies, and the youngest were still growing in their pods.

That left Damien as the one person she could confide in. But even he had grown increasingly distant as his experiments wore him thin. Sara felt awful about that, but she didn’t know how to help, and that made her feel even worse. They still spent most of their spare time together, but it felt like he wasn’t even present half the time, often lost in his own head.

She’d loved him for years now and tried to give subtle hints via looks or touches to see if he would make a move, but he never did. She considered directly telling him about her feelings, making a move of her own, but it would risk leaving things awkward between them if he turned her down. His friendship was too valuable to risk.

She was probably deluding herself anyway; he obviously had affection for her as a friend or a sister, and they’d been inseparable since they were young. But with their intelligence gap, he likely couldn’t view her as a true romantic partner. Her eyes teared up at the thought, but there was no sense in dwelling on it.

As the only worthless member of her class, made for a single purpose but unfit for it, she was punished with increasing isolation. By now she’d hoped to have seen the world or have some idea of what her future would hold, but she’d long since learned that life wasn’t a fairy tale.

It was on wakeful nights like these that she found herself having darker thoughts. What if she put a bullet in the doctor’s head one day? Why hadn’t anyone done that yet? All of those hyperaggressive Class B subjects over the years, and not one of them just decided to end him? She’d already been sneaking around a loaded pistol in her pack for self-defense for years now, ever since learning of what Jolgen had done to Clare, so the next time Stallard ran some sort of assembly—bang.

She would die too, of course. Any number of the countless guards at the facility would shoot her within seconds. But it wasn’t like her life was valuable enough to fear losing.

Realistically, she knew she wouldn’t go through with it. It was just something she found herself contemplating from time to time during the darkest hours of the night.



Fallon watched as Santiago raised a hand, signaling the group to get ready. He was leading her, Jax, and Maria on a tank-busting mission in Eastern Europe on the warfront, which they’d been grinding through for almost two months now.

She hated every moment of it. Hiding behind a fallen tree near a road in the drizzle, un-showered for half a week, she watched an approaching company of tanks and artillery. Solo assassinations were far preferable to outright war, snooping around in a muddy forest like this for so long. How gross. This wasn’t how she’d envisioned her mid-twenties going.

The tanks were hardened against most armor-busting weapons. Several anti-air artillery units rolled with them as well, warding off heavier airstrikes from above. But they had no defense against telekinesis, other than the sheer number of combat drones and men with guns protecting the perimeter.

When Santiago gave the signal, Fallon as the team’s heavy hitter would crush the barrels of the anti-air artillery units, effectively taking them off the board. That was the key mission. If there was time afterward, she’d also break a tank’s sensor array, flip over a truck full of soldiers, or otherwise wreak havoc where possible.

If it went well, the enemy wouldn’t even spot them here in the forest, since Fallon’s strikes were invisible. If it went wrong, though, that was where the others would step in. Santiago and Jax were armored juggernauts, carrying .50-caliber heavy machine guns as if they were standard rifles and able to wield them with incredible reflexes and aim. Maria filled the gaps, striking where needed and keeping track of the whole battle from the backline.

Still, the four of them couldn’t finish the armored company themselves. Eight tanks, four anti-air artillery vehicles, and several trucks filled with soldiers was too big of a group to engage directly. Their job was to cripple the air defenses so an allied helicopter force could swoop in and finish them off. On Santiago’s orders, Jax was already whispering over the radio to the local lieutenant colonel, giving him coordinates for the cleanup strike and confirming that they were about to start their work.

Five seconds, Santiago signaled silently with his hand. Four, three, two, one.

Fallon planted her bracers on the ground, drew warmth from the air, and crushed the first anti-air gun, then the second. She was about to crush the third when an officer shouted in his language something like, “Hidden targets, two o’clock!”

Shit, she thought. That’s faster than normal.

Maria crushed his throat an instant after he shouted, but it was too late. A hail of gunfire came in their direction. Armored soldiers jumped off their trucks and spread out over the road like bees from a kicked hive, shooting into the forest. One of the vehicles launched a flying, buzzing swarm of combat drones as well, each one capable of fatal shots.

How’d they see us? The four of them had done this several times over the past two months, and the enemy was getting better at responding, but this was a leap. They’d grown wise to their strategy, seemingly to the point of setting a trap.

“Back, back!” Santiago shouted. Maria retreated first. Jax nodded to Fallon, and she backed up several yards, finding cover behind the thickest tree nearby.

Santiago pulled up his heavy machine gun and opened fire, mowing down the front wave of oncoming soldiers and drones. Bullets rained back at him. Some struck his armor and grazed his helmet, forcing him to drop back down behind cover. His heavy nanocomposite armor covered his body from head to toe, several times thicker than the slim vest Fallon wore, but he still had to back down when focus-fired. The flexible spaces around the joints had their limits.

Two more enemy soldiers from that wave dropped dead as Maria crushed their throats. She then slammed three of the closest drones hard into the ground, smashing them apart.

There were more waves though, continuing to pour off the trucks toward their position. As Fallon peeked from around the tree she used for cover, a bullet whizzed past her cheek. She snapped back, blinding herself to what was happening. Heavy gunfire shredded the tree’s wood, threatening to pierce all the way through if she let them continue endlessly. From what she’d last seen, Santiago and Jax were still pinned at the front, behind their fallen tree. Their options were dwindling.

She had to step up now.

“I’m going to blast!” she shouted over the sound of gunfire. “Stay low and fall back! Three, two, one!” Even Santiago as the team leader would defer to a blast order. She was about to use her strongest attack.

Planting her bracers and broadening the tips of both foci, Fallon pushed as hard as she could, sending a horizontal blast of force toward the enemy, straight through the tree she was using for cover. Hopefully high enough off the ground to go over Santiago and Jax’s crouched position. The air turned frigid, and frost spread across the ground in as wide of a circle as she’d ever made.

Normally she focused on gripping sets of individual objects and either moving, twisting, or crushing them, but for this rarer and less energy-efficient method of telekinesis, she channeled environmental temperature to push everything in their direction, every square inch of space. None of the others in her class could hit this hard, and it would leave her with a severe headache and drain her options afterward.

With a crack as loud as a lightning strike, the thick tree exploded outward toward the enemy and her foci surged forward from there. Nine soldiers flew back screaming, torn apart in a spray of misted blood. Their bullets reversed course midair, shooting back the way they came. Over a dozen of their flying drones got caught in the blast and shattered, spewing their metallic pieces backward as well. Several trees fell in the enemy’s direction as though a tornado tore through them, while branches and bark ripped off others on the periphery. The forest for eighty yards in front of her vanished into utter ruin.

Capitalizing on the opportunity, Santiago and Jax stayed low and fell back to Maria’s position, leaving Fallon as the most forward member of the team.

“Come Fallon!” Jax shouted. “We’ve got the opening.”

Fallon released her energy-draining blast, her vision blurring as pain blossomed in her head. At the far reach of the damage, one of the tanks flew back and toppled onto its side in a cloud of dust and debris.

She staggered back into the forest toward her team. Jax covered her with fire from his machine gun.

“Tank!” Maria shouted.
