Fallon turned back as the nearest undamaged tank swung its barrel right toward them. Maria reached out, teeth clenched, and the barrel exploded with a deafening pop. She’d crushed it just in time, forcing the round inside to misfire and blow the barrel open. Gasping from the strain, Maria turned and sprinted into the forest.

Fallon took the opportunity and bolted after her, but she tripped over a root and dropped to the ground, disoriented by her growing headache from her all-out blast. Her ears were ringing, too, throwing off her balance.

A strong hand grabbed her vest, yanking her back up.

“Jax!” she gasped.

“I got you babe, come on.” Jax’s voice was muffled behind his face-covering helmet. Fallon scrambled to her feet with his help and ran with him.

“Threats, three o’clock!” Santiago shouted from up ahead as their group ran through the forest. Fallon looked to her right and saw more soldiers and drones through the trees. She couldn’t tell how many. They had entered the forest farther down the road at the initial moment of the attack and were trying to flank them perpendicularly. Their commanders must’ve prepared this reaction ahead of time; it was perfectly executed. Her head throbbed and her vision blurred, but while sprinting with Jax she tried to spot any soldier to attack.

A soldier gurgled and fell as Maria crushed his throat. Santiago exchanged fire with enemies Fallon couldn’t even see through the dense woods, coupled with her own disorientation. She could only hope that she wouldn’t be hit, and hated feeling so weak, blindly relying on Jax to guide her out. The two of them were falling farther behind Santiago and Maria. They were out of sight now.

Jax stopped and used his hand to guide her away from him. “You go ahead, right that way Fallon, straight there. I’ll provide some cover fi—” His words were cut off by a shot to his neck. He stumbled back, and then his throat burst open.

Fallon screamed as he fell. It was an explosive round, straight to where his armor was thin and flexible. She dropped to her knees next to him as he thrashed around, and she scrambled to pull off his helmet. He looked up at her, eyes frantic, and gurgled. Blood spewed everywhere from his gaping wound as Fallon tried to cover it with her palms.

“It’s okay Jax, you’ll heal. You’ll—”

“Freeze!” shouted an accented voice, coupled with the clicks of several guns. She paused, then raised her bloody hands and laced them behind her head, though she kept her eyes locked on Jax. A few seconds later, his body went limp, his unblinking face staring up at her, frozen in a final expression of pain and fear.

Fallon tilted her head to her right and glanced back out of the corner of her eye. Four soldiers stood there, pointing assault rifles at her back, while she knelt in the mud. A wave of rage flowed through her. The slow kind. She could almost feel its movement through her veins, spreading out from her chest, burning inside like molten syrup. She clenched her teeth so hard they could’ve cracked.

One soldier pulled out a pair of restraints and yelled something she didn’t understand. The meaning was clear enough. He stepped toward her.

She waited until he was close. He’d make a good meat shield for what she was about to do.

As he tried to grab her wrist, she used her right focus to grip all three of the other soldiers’ rifles and launched them high into the air.

Two men let go of their grips as their rifles flew upward. The third got a shot off, the round hitting the dirt a foot from where she knelt, but his rifle cord was too entangled around him, and he was yanked far up into the forest canopy, screaming.

In response, the soldier trying to cuff Fallon reached for his rifle. She used her left focus to grip his throat and crush it straight through, inadvertently beheading him with excess force.

Fallon’s head split in pain, and she was on the verge of passing out. If only she’d had more time to recover from that blast.

The two disarmed soldiers who were still standing drew their pistols. She should have crushed their throats rather than launch their rifles. What had she been thinking? The problem was she hadn’t been thinking. She used the last of her strength to crush the throat of the soldier who was first on the draw, but the second soldier aimed right at her face. Her vision swam and her energy was utterly drained. She frantically grasped at the pistol holstered on her own hip, but she was too disoriented, too slow.

Not like this.

She flinched as gunshots rang out, but they weren’t from that last soldier’s pistol. They were from Santiago’s heavy machine gun tearing through the side of that soldier’s head, bursting it open. Santiago and Maria had come back for her.

Heart pounding in her chest, still kneeling, Fallon looked down at Jax again. As she did, the three guns and one soldier that she’d previously flung skyward began to fall, hitting the ground around her with metallic thuds, one after the other. The soldier screamed as he fell into a more sickening crunch, silencing on impact.

Fallon ignored it all, and a strange serenity filled the forest around her. She reached out to close Jax’s eyelids, then caressed his handsome cheek. Seconds later, from the direction of the road, came the chopping of helicopter blades and one thunderous explosion after another. The air crew had come to finish off what remained of the enemy company.







Chapter 19





Two Years Earlier, Continued


I believe Damien was correct several years ago, after all. It seems a bit more coddling was needed for this first batch. Going forward, we will optimize the process more to ensure that subjects’ emotions and motivations are fully tailored for their purpose.

–TS log 12746, excerpt

Fallon sat in the facility’s familiar white-walled cafeteria with a table all to herself. She’d forgotten to eat the day before, and now, staring at the items on her tray, she wondered why she’d chosen any of them.

Eight days had passed since Jax died. After the incident, Santiago, Maria, and Fallon had rendezvoused with the nearest checkpoint and were lifted out with Jax’s body. Within a few days, they were back in safe territory, debriefed, and flown home. And here she was, not knowing what to do next. Day after day passed without Jax around. It felt too quiet.

When she was most honest with herself, Fallon wasn’t even sure what she was angrier about: Jax’s death or the feeling of having been so weak. She’d almost died as an unnamed soldier on a foreign battlefield, one of countless corpses rotting in the mud. It’s not like she’d wanted to go on that stupid mission in the first place.

Part of her wanted to obliterate the table. She imagined crashing it up into the ceiling, then flinging it back down to shatter across the floor in front of everyone. She had so much anger and nowhere to put it. Somehow, breaking the table felt more constructive than eating the worthless meal that sat atop it.

Sensing herself on the verge of actually doing it, she forced herself to her feet and headed to the restroom.

She pushed through the door with a slam and walked over to one of the sinks, then stared into the mirror. Bags hung under her eyes. She felt eight years older than she had eight days ago. With a sigh, she turned on the faucet and splashed cold water on her face.

A toilet flushed. She hadn’t bothered to check if anyone else was here. After a moment, one of the stall doors opened. It was Sara.

Fallon glanced up at her in the mirror, and Sara paused. Breaking eye contact after a second, she sheepishly walked over to one of the farthest sinks to wash her hands.

What a drama queen. Fallon hadn’t hurt Sara since they were young, and that’d been in a duel. She was unimportant, like most of the lab dorks and facility guards. Fallon went back to washing her face.

Sara shut off her faucet, dried her hands, then paused again. “I’m sorry about Jax,” she said. “About . . . what happened to you over there.”

“What?” Fallon asked, glancing over.

“I heard it was awful.” Sara turned, facing her. “That’s all. I’m sorry for what you went through. Losing someone so close.”

Fallon loved it when people respected her. She liked it when people feared her. She didn’t even mind it when people hated her. But no, she would not be pitied. She stared at Sara for a moment, then mentally flung her back against the tile wall. Sara crumpled to the floor, her face filled with shock, blood seeping from the back of her head.

“That’s what I think of your pity!” Fallon shouted as the chilled air sent goosebumps up her arms. “You think I give a shit about what happened to Jax? It was his own damn fault!”

Fallon watched Sara for a second, bleeding on the floor and grasping for her pack, which had landed a few feet away, then forced herself to turn and leave. But as Fallon walked away, a click sounded from behind. She froze, staring at the exit for a moment.

She wouldn’t dare. Fallon turned slowly and saw Sara lying there, blond hair streaked with red, eyes narrowed, pointing a pistol at her with both hands.

Fallon waited for a moment, out of sheer curiosity to see if she would fire. She could stop the bullet if she did, and readied herself to do so by watching Sara’s trigger finger for any sign of movement. But no attempted shot came. Just Sara aiming her gun, trembling.

“That’s it? Nothing?” Fallon asked. She mentally crushed the gun barrel. “Which limb should I break first?”

“I didn’t want any trouble!” Sara shouted, sounding more frustrated than afraid. She should’ve been terrified, begging for her life right now.

“Clearly you did.” Fallon mentally gripped Sara’s right wrist—the primary hand used to hold the pistol—and lifted her up off the floor by it, her legs fruitlessly kicking in the air. Sara gasped and grabbed her arm with her other hand, trying to take the pressure of her body’s weight off it.

Fallon let seconds tick by, watching Sara frantically hang and squirm. Blood already dripped from her hair; head wounds did tend to bleed a lot. But there wasn’t enough defeat in her expression yet.

“Beg me to let you go, or I’ll make it worse,” Fallon said.

Sara looked up with fury and pain in her eyes. Still not enough fear.

Fallon squeezed Sara’s wrist harder until it audibly fractured. Sara screamed.

Then, Fallon walked toward her as she hung by her single wrist. “I’m going to squeeze harder and harder until you beg me to stop. Otherwise, you’ll lose your—”

Sara swung her legs up and kicked her in the face as soon as she stepped within range. Fallon staggered back several steps and lost mental control of her grip, letting Sara drop hard to the floor.

As she steadied herself, Fallon tasted blood. So, it’ll be murder, then. She instinctively reached up to touch her lip, and her fingers came away warm and red. By the time she looked up, Sara was already on her feet and charging in. Fallon put her hand out, and Sara froze a foot away from her, eyes wide, choking.

“You think you were close, but you weren’t. I was just going slow on you,” Fallon said. She slammed Sara a dozen feet back to the wall again, harder than before, to regain some distance. The back of Sara’s head made a cracking sound, leaving a spot of blood on the wall as she hit the floor, limp.

The restroom door burst open, and Fallon turned back to look. It was Raphael. He took in the scene, and horror filled his eyes when he saw Sara.

“Stop!” he shouted, raising his laser rifle toward Fallon. “Get away from C17.”

“You sure you can pull the trigger before I can shove that gun back through your face?” Fallon asked.

Raphael hesitated, and they stared at each other for a moment, until Fallon rolled her eyes and raised her hands to ease the standoff. “We were done playing anyway,” she said. “I’ll just walk out.”

She strolled toward him, and he treaded backward, keeping his rifle pointed at her chest as he did. He even managed to get the door open without taking his eyes or his gun off her. Impressive. He was the head of security, after all. But he didn’t seem to realize she already had one of her foci on his trigger, making sure he couldn’t squeeze it. It was adorable that he thought this was still a contest.

By the time they emerged into the main hall area near the cafeteria, a facility alarm had started blaring, and there was a scene. Two more guards raised their standard assault rifles at her.

Several members of Class B and Class C came from the cafeteria to see the commotion, including Jolce and Clare.

“One of you, check on C17 in the restroom,” Raphael said, never taking his eyes or gun off Fallon. “She’s injured, or worse. And someone call the infirmary.”

Clare’s eyes went wide, and she dashed into the restroom. Those two were so close that Fallon sometimes wondered if it was more than just friendship.

By now, the stupidity of what she’d done had fully set it in. She’d held herself back from breaking the cafeteria table, but then had broken Sara instead at the slightest provocation. How dumb. It was undeserved, and she’d take it back if she could. But she was in too deep now; might as well see it through to the end. So she stood there, folded her arms, spat out some blood like it was nothing, and purposely kept a smirk on her face for the audience.

A minute later, Dr. Stallard came pacing down the hall, flanked by four more guards, two of whom held laser rifles. Another three guards came from a side corridor. They had her surrounded.

“What’s going on here, C5?” Dr. Stallard demanded.

That brute Jolgen had killed two classmates as a teenager and got off easy. Some others in Class B had done the same, including Santiago once, such a long time ago. The smart move here would be to back down and apologize.

But she wasn’t in the mood for being smart. Not today. Instead, she mentally grabbed Dr. Stallard’s throat without making any visible motions. Not enough to stop his airflow or blood; just enough to immobilize him.

“C5!” he shouted.

“Call me Fallon and I’ll release you,” she said.

“Have you gone mad?”

“I said, call me Fallon.”

After a few seconds, Raphael and the other guards realized what she was doing.

“Let him go!” Raphael shouted, more intensely than before. Several soldiers tightened their fingers on their triggers. They actually looked ready to shoot this time.

“Anyone fires, and I’ll take him with me,” Fallon said. “I just want to hear my name.” Her eyes teared up, but that only added to her fury. For a man whose neck she could snap with a thought, why did he have such power over her?

Raphael glanced at Dr. Stallard, unsure of what to do.

“You’ve been through a lot, C5. I understand that. We can fix this,” Dr. Stallard said.

“Call me Fallon.”

“No.”

“Do it!”

“No.”

He sounded so calm, and that disarmed her. Fatherly even, despite his words that said otherwise. She stared at him, heart pounding, as more people came. Guards and lab dorks were everywhere. And now Maria, Santiago, and Damien.

Fallon reluctantly let go of Dr. Stallard’s throat. Her father’s throat. “Fine. I overreacted.”

More than two dozen guns aimed at her now, including lasers from too many directions to deal with, and several Class B and Class C members would side with Dr. Stallard over her if chaos broke out. None of those were what frightened her, though. It was that she couldn’t bring herself to hurt him.

“Firearms down, everyone. It’s okay,” Dr. Stallard said. “Like I said, we can fix this.” Most guards immediately lowered their weapons, but a few hesitated. One by one, their rifles gradually went down, with Raphael’s being the last.

“How can we fix this?” Fallon shouted. “Look around!”

“Come with me, C5,” Dr. Stallard said. There was a calm affection in his voice, and he gestured toward himself.

She hesitated, then approached.

He led her side by side down the corridor the way he’d come, his hand gently on her shoulder as if to guide her, which he’d never done before. Raphael and eight guards followed closely. When they reached the facility’s central stairwell, Dr. Stallard opened the door and gestured for her to go first.

“Where are we going?” Fallon asked.

“Downstairs. There will be consequences for what you’ve done, but they’ll be good for you.”

Fallon paused. She turned back and looked at Raphael and the guards. Several of them tensed up, gripping their weapons tighter, but they didn’t raise them.

“No need for all of that,” Dr. Stallard said. “Look at me, not them, C5. Come, come.” He stepped through the doorway himself and held the door open for her with an expression of casual assurance, like of course she would follow, no doubt in his mind. She didn’t know why, but it felt right, and so she did.

They descended the stairs, guards following, then went down deeper than the basement pool level, all the way to the sub-basement. She had never been down here before, and it was off-limits to subjects, though she’d heard there were large warehouses where they kept the medical samples and pharmaceuticals. At the bottom was a large room with two guards seated at a table playing cards. They stood quickly, looking surprised. “Sir,” one of them said to Dr. Stallard.

Fallon looked around, taking in the entrance to the facility’s industrial elevator, which went from the sub-basement to the top floor, as well as a door that read Maintenance, a door that read Restrooms, and wide double doors that read Cold Storage.

“We have several layers of cold storage warehouses,” Dr. Stallard said in a conversational tone. “Different things require different temperatures. Raphael, you and the others can wait here.”

“Sir, I don’t think that’s—”

“It’s fine, Raphael. You’ve performed admirably. I’ll be back in a moment.”

“Yes . . . sir.”

Dr. Stallard tapped the button beside the Cold Storage double doors, and they opened automatically. He gestured for Fallon to follow him in, and she did. Being alone, without guards, put her more at ease.

Inside waited a chilled laboratory room, or a mortuary by the looks of it. There were large metal tables on wheels, assorted medical equipment, and a harsh scent of something like formaldehyde. There was also a large coat rack, a storage closet, and another large set of double doors labeled Cold Storage 1.

“Is this . . .” Fallon started. “Is this where Jax is?”

Dr. Stallard nodded gravely. “Yes, but there’s no need to fret. That’s not what we’re here for. Come with me.”

He pulled a coat from the rack and put it on. Fallon had never seen a coat so thick. It went down to his knees and had a large hood that could zip up and cover most of the face. He nodded to the rack, and Fallon went over and grabbed the same type. It had several interior layers, all made with some advanced material. When she finished putting it on, Dr. Stallard stuffed a pair of thick gloves into his coat pocket and handed her a pair as well. She stuffed them into her pocket too.

Insulated suspender pants and oversized boots sat underneath the rack, on a lower shelf. Dr. Stallard ignored those, but he grabbed an extra coat and slung it over his shoulder.

“Good. All set,” he said, and tapped the button beside the next set of double doors, which opened automatically. They entered a small insulation room between two sets of double doors, and as Dr. Stallard led her in, the prior doors closed behind them. He pressed the main button on the wall of the insulation room, and the next set of doors opened.

A much larger room stretched ahead, with tall shelves of storage forming seven aisles to walk through. Some aisles were filled with medical samples, others with pharmaceuticals.

Fallon followed Dr. Stallard down the long center aisle to another set of double doors at the far end of the room; these were labeled Cold Storage 2.

“Mortuaries are for short-term use to keep bodies cold, but not frozen,” Dr. Stallard said. “Many supplies need to be kept like that. However, some facilities, such as forensic institutes or certain medical laboratories, require temperatures far below zero to minimize long-term decomposition. This second one here is set to negative sixty degrees. It’s the largest of its type in the world.” He gestured toward the next door.

Fallon eyed him. “What are we doing?”

“You need to cool off,” he said. “Literally. It’ll do you wonders.”

“In there?”

“What better jail is there for someone like you, who pulls heat from the air for energy?”

She found herself staring, and realized belatedly that her mouth was open.

He smiled. “No reason to worry; this won’t be for long. You wouldn’t survive long in there anyway, even with your enhanced metabolism. It’s a jail, not a prison. Much like how violent drunks need to be thrown in a cell for a night to cool off, this is the equivalent for you. You’ll emerge better, I think.”

Fallon’s telekinesis worked reasonably well in winter temperatures. Actions were harder in the cold, since it was tougher to draw energy out of frigid air than warm, but it wasn’t a major hindrance. She had never tried her powers in such deeply subzero temperatures, though. Negative sixty degrees?

She narrowed her eyes. Killing him now was still an option, before she entered that colder room. She’d have to fight her way back through waves of guards and would surely die, but she’d take a lot of them with her along the way.

“How long?” she asked.

“Until you’re better.” He smiled. “Trust me for once.”

She searched his gray eyes. Was he playing with her? In which direction should she take her chances? The freezer or back through the guards? By now there would probably be triple the number up there, and many with lasers.

“Fine,” she said eventually.

Dr. Stallard tapped the button. The door opened automatically to another small insulation room, which they stepped into. After the doors shut behind them, he pressed the main button inside, and a final set of doors opened ahead.

Another warehouse loomed in front of them, just like the prior one, with seven aisles stretching into the room between tall shelves. Dr. Stallard gestured with his chin for her to enter.

Last chance, she thought. But after a few seconds, she sighed and stepped in. A blast of cold struck her face; she’d never felt temperatures like this. It almost felt like burning rather than freezing. The room was surprisingly loud, too—whatever machines hypercooled the air made a constant humming sound.

Dr. Stallard lingered at the doorway. “Now C5, listen carefully,” he said over the noise. “We have cameras, so please don’t damage anything here, or we’ll have a problem again. And without your coat, you’ll become hypothermic in minutes and die not long thereafter. With your coat, you’ll live, especially with your enhanced metabolism and cellular repair. Make sure you keep it on. And here’s a second one as well. I’ll be back after you’ve had sufficient time to reflect.”

He tossed her the extra coat, then tapped the button for the door to close. After it did, Fallon watched him through the small window and heard him clamping something outside. It sounded like a pair of handcuffs. He stepped through the previous doors out of the insulation room, then out of sight.

Fallon was already freezing, and a knot of fear twisted inside as she realized she was locked in. She pulled the thick gloves out of her pocket and slid them over her already-numb hands. The coat and hood kept her body warm, but her face felt like ice and fire at the same time, and she didn’t have the right type of pants or shoes for a prolonged stay in a place like this. That was clearly what those dorky suspender pants and boots under the coat rack had been for. She went over and pressed the exit button for the door, but as expected, it ran into metallic resistance and wouldn’t open. Dr. Stallard had cuffed the handles together.

I have to test my powers.

She tossed the extra coat onto the floor, stepped back, and tried to lift it. She envisioned energy flowing from the air and into the coat. The air was far below freezing, but it wasn’t at absolute zero, so the molecules still had some kinetic energy in them. It all felt so reluctant, though. When she tried to lift the coat, it was as though it weighed thousands of pounds. With all her mental strength, she raised it for about ten seconds until she gasped and dropped it back to the floor, a headache already forming. With this absurd weakness, she wouldn’t be able to bust open the door, wouldn’t be able to get out. Was this how Sara felt all the time?

Shivering despite her thick coat, Fallon started pacing around the room to explore and keep herself warm. It had a similar layout to the first cold warehouse, with seven aisles heading toward the deep end of the room. A sign above the first aisle starting from the right read Cadavers. She tried opening one of the sliding steel doors that would presumably have a body in it, but it was locked. She read several of the number-names listed, and they were all people she had known. Of the four dead members of Class C, three were here. The first one, C1, was not. She had died very young. Six of the ten dead members of Class B were here as well. And there was one labeled D3, for . . . what was his name again? Rudolph.

There were dozens of empty spaces for more bodies here, and Fallon remembered Sara again. Would she end up down here soon, from her injuries? How hard had she cracked Sara’s head against the wall with that second push? Fallon felt a shiver run through her, though not from the cold this time. She kept walking.

All of us will end up here. Even if Dr. Stallard was right that their enhanced healing would slow their aging, she’d never imagined living a long life. She was a weapon, a predator, and therefore prone to danger. There were a lot of things she could heal from, but as Jax had just shown, an explosive round to the neck wasn’t one of them. That was all it took: a big enough hit to the head or chest. Their skin, muscles, bones, and joints could heal faster than normal, and more completely, but none of that helped much for a lethal shot.

Seeing the exact spot where her body would be stored, though, caused her to pause. Especially while surrounded by such frigid air. The blank square steel door right under the one labeled C4 was where Fallon would probably go. Right there. She’d be on this planet for such little time, and bred for such a specific purpose, so she had to be the best at it. And yet, because everything she did was classified, hardly anyone would ever know of her deeds—all the people she’d killed, at home and abroad, to keep the country safe. Only the people in this facility, and a handful of high-ranking suits and generals, had any idea that she even existed. She’d have no husband, no children, and would probably die within the next decade or two, if she even made it out of this frozen hell.

“This is where we go to die,” she whispered.

She walked farther and reached the end of the row and found the storage for infant and toddler cadavers, including C1, several of the earliest members of Class B, some members of Class D, and all of Class A. She’d always wondered how many members of the first class there were, and now she knew that the answer was eight. Eight tiny bodies. None of them had chosen a name.

Fallon jumped in place a few times to try to generate some warmth, then resumed walking. Her face burned with cold, especially the tip of her nose. She zipped up the annoying face-mask portion all the way, covering everything but her eyes.

The other aisles proved less interesting. Slices of tissue, medical samples, and all sorts of creepy vials. Plenty of spare space as well.

Every hour, her toes would go numb through her shoes, so she’d return to the spare coat on the floor, sit in the corner, and wrap her legs up with it. That helped, along with rubbing them, but then her core body temperature would get too cold from not moving, which forced her to get up and walk some more. She eventually lost track of how much time had gone by.

“Can I come out now?” she shouted, her voice weak and hollow, as she stared up at one of the cameras. No answer. Could they even hear her over the warehouse coolers’ incessant humming? More pacing. More rounds of warming her toes.

After she lay down for the fourth or fifth time, it was too tiring to get back up. With her hood up, her gloves on, and the second coat over her legs like a blanket, she drifted off.



“Good morning,” Dr. Stallard’s voice said.

Fallon opened her eyes, still wrapped up in the two coats, but she felt too sleepy to move. She wasn’t even shivering anymore, which was nice. She closed her eyes and started to drift off aga—

“I have tea,” he said, nudging her shoulder. “Come, have some. You’re hypothermic.”

It took all the effort she had, but Fallon opened her eyes and sat up. She was still in that frozen warehouse, and most of her body had gone numb. A thermos appeared in front of her, held by a gloved hand, and she looked up to see Dr. Stallard’s face.

“What time is it?” she asked. Her voice barely came out in a whisper.

“Early morning. Your lips are blue. We have to warm you up. Here. Have a sip. Slowly.”

Fallon reached out, but it was hard to even grasp the thermos with her numb, gloved hands. As she gained her grip on it, Dr. Stallard loosened his, though he kept his hands hovering close by in case she dropped it. She lifted it to her face, letting it warm her skin, then sipped, tasting the strong black tea as it filled her mouth with heat. Normally tea was too bitter for her taste, but here it was delicious. She swallowed, then took another sip.

“That’s enough,” Dr. Stallard said. “You’re likely dehydrated, but we need to get you out and warm you up properly. Here, take my hand, Fallon.”

Her chest went tight. Had she imagined that? No. She gazed up at him, eyes wide, and saw his hand extended. After a pause, she took it—how rarely they had ever touched—and he pulled her up firmly while also gripping her under the triceps to steady her. He walked her toward the door.

“Why wouldn’t you just call me by my name before?” she asked.

“I can’t reward bad behavior, especially in front of others,” he said. “But for good behavior, that I can do.”

“Is . . . Sara alive?” Fallon asked.

“C17 woke up this morning,” Dr. Stallard said. “Emmett thinks she’ll recover, in time. Now come, let’s get you out of here.”







Chapter 20



“I’m going to Florida next week,” Asim said to a small audience of analysts and agents in their ACI conference room. “I could use some sun for a change.”

“Sir?” Ava said.

“To MacDill Air Force Base, in Tampa. It’s the headquarters of the US Special Operations Command, SOCOM. I think their general knows things that could be helpful. Price used to lead that command prior to becoming the Vice Chairman.”

“But didn’t you already run into a dead end with the Joint Chiefs last week?” Flanagan asked. “How could going to someone beneath them help?”

“That’s the thing,” Asim said, raising a finger. “The commanders of the Unified Combatant Commands are the ones who directly control troops, and they don’t quite report to the Joint Chiefs. The Joint Chiefs are the highest-ranking officers in each of the six branches, along with the Chairman and Vice Chairman at the very top, but they focus on advising and administrative leadership, not controlling forces directly.”

Flanagan nodded, but Asim still saw a lot of blank faces, so he continued. “The military branches used to compete in any given region, and top commanders wanted to avoid that. So, they established the eleven Unified Combatant Commands. For example, even within the Army, some of the intelligence I used to provide in the field would go to the Navy as well, for when their strike teams were needed instead of ours. There are seven geographical commands, each focused on a certain region of the planet. Any US combat that happens there, no matter if it involves the Army or the Navy or the Air Force or whatever, goes through the Unified Combatant Command that oversees that region. It’s a way to organize the branches to work together.”

“Which one oversees mutants, Colonel?” Brooks asked. Ava smirked.

Asim decided to take the question seriously. “There are four Unified Combatant Commands that aren’t related to geography, to complete the eleven total. US Cyber Command deals with cyber threats. US Strategic Command deals with nuclear matters. US Transportation Command focuses on logistics. And there’s US Special Operations Command, or SOCOM, which deals with elite missions such as targeted strikes or hostage retrieval. From what General Price said to Jade before she killed him, the senior officers of SOCOM are most familiar with Stallard and his operations. I pulled a lot of strings to go talk to their commander. The Joint Chiefs are all based in the Pentagon, but the commanders of the eleven Unified Combatant Commands who directly control troops are spread out across the country. The one I want to talk to is down in Tampa—General Rodriguez.”

Roy had told Asim weeks ago off the record that these green-eyed operatives had been involved in spec-ops for years, but he couldn’t reference that. The brief dialogue between Jade and Price had reinforced that fact, and he was able to reference that in his official reports, working his way up to a meeting with their highest levels directly.

“Can’t we expect more obfuscation?” another agent asked.

“Of course, but I have to pursue it now. It’s the highest level we can reach with some degree of independence from the Joint Chiefs. We still don’t have any on-the-ground leads for Veronica, but I can explore these spec-op routes to at least understand the scope of the issue. This general is making me wait days for a meeting, though, despite all my string-pulls.”



That night, Asim sat on the third-floor balcony, front row, off to the right side of the stage in Opera, Space Background, Moderate Population Density, Room 3.

Jade wasn’t there, but he took the same seat where he’d met her before, when she’d worn Veronica’s GlitterJitters avatar—the virtual skin and identity key of her victim, with a sufficiently high edge realm reputation score to access fine places like this. Tonight, the singer and orchestra played a different set, and the theater looked out over Earth instead of Saturn. A lightning storm raged over Indonesia, bolts flashing through the dark clouds.

Things had been progressing better back when he’d met her here. Now, everything had gone to shit. Maybe that laser shot had been fatal. Without an ID, she’d struggle to find medical help. But deep down he knew that wasn’t how this case would end. Someone like her wouldn’t go down from slow infection.

He’d come here three nights in a row now. GlitterJitters hadn’t logged in to VR at all since Asim had sat here with her, and she’d blocked all incoming messages. He’d left his own privacy settings open, hoping she might search, notice him here, and realize he was reaching out. She probably wouldn’t come. Still, something about this place helped him think.

Send me that list again of virtual locations she visited, he texted Brooks.

As he waited, Asim looked around the extravagant venue. Why had she chosen this spot? In the middle of violent plotting, why sit and watch an opera in space?

In his field days, he’d often found that intuition mattered as much as data. Getting inside a target’s head could lead to breakthroughs in predicting their moves or whereabouts. The danger was that sometimes you didn’t make it all the way back out. The best agents inevitably left pieces of their sanity in the field by the time they came back home.

Here you go, Colonel, Brooks replied. Asim opened the file, which contained holograms of all the locations. He examined them closely.

Since Veronica’s disappearance, her GlitterJitters identity—almost certainly used by Jade each time—had briefly visited four places: an archipelago in the Arctic Circle to watch the northern lights, a Hawaiian mountaintop overlooking the ocean, a Shinto shrine with a view of Mt. Fuji, and this opera.

The four places seemed random at first glance, but all were visually stunning, with long sightlines and vast horizons. Asim enjoyed good visuals of course, but he preferred experiences he could feel. Tropical water on his skin, warm wind in his hair, and soft sand underfoot. Adding those sensations had finally fueled VR’s explosive success after decades of prior stagnation. Jade’s destinations, though, were almost entirely about what she could see.

She had likely been trying to distract herself from something. Her mission sounded delusional; stopping an evil god, some sort of hybrid capable of seizing other systems. From anyone else, he’d dismiss the claim outright. But from someone like her, he couldn’t help but contemplate what it meant.



Ava looked up at the clock. Past midnight already.

But she couldn’t stop. If she’d found Jade earlier, maybe the Pentagon attack could have been prevented. Some part of that was on her, and she had to make up for it.

The case became more complicated by the day. Greater numbers of people—and bots—impersonated Jade over time. With billions of people online, even a fraction of a percent using her name or avatar in VR meant tens of thousands of impersonators. It was a known issue throughout the decentralized edges or “edge realms” of social media: anyone you met there in VR posing as a famous figure was probably an impostor unless you had strong evidence otherwise. The centralized platforms or “core realms” used government ID and location checks to clean up that type of spam, but someone like Jade couldn’t use those. She’d have to stick to the edges.

Some of the bots were likely the work of Dr. Stallard’s lab. Colonel Rahal had said that when Jade wore Veronica’s avatar and spoke with him, she’d insisted there was a greater enemy. And indeed, Ava had found many edge realm posts after the rest stop incident claiming as much, with details. Human-machine interfaces, malevolent superintelligences, dark gods, domestic assassinations, even namedropping the Stolguard facility and its director. Some of it might have been Jade herself, posting under throwaway identity keys. But for every identity spreading those claims, thousands of fakes distracted from them.

That was how governments and corporations suppressed unwanted narratives or data throughout the edge realms, after all. Since they couldn’t delete posts there as they would in the core realms, they simply buried them under noise. Well-funded groups could unleash armies of bots—AI writers, podcasters, and influencers with millions of fake followers—to parrot their preferred story or flood the space with conflicting ones. Even many of the human content creators could be bought.

Now, with the major news channels downplaying the scale of Jade’s attacks, the buttoned-up core realms suppressing discourse on it, and the edge realms spammed with counter-narratives, recent polls showed that fewer than one third of Americans even believed a telekinetic woman had attacked the newsroom and the Pentagon, with the rest being unsure or dismissive.

There were still ways to verify things in the edge realms of course; it just required different methods. Reputation scores, social graphs, or offline methods of verification were all useful. But most people didn’t have the time to dig too deep. Ava trusted Colonel Rahal’s edge realm identity because he’d verified his public key in person to her. When she saw him at his favorite beach or visiting the space opera, she knew it was him—unless someone had broken into his apartment since they last spoke and stolen the access credentials that he’d used for years.

Many VR devices also offered “visual verifier flags” for use within the edge realms, but they weren’t foolproof. Devices with that feature could scan their users’ appearance to form the basis of a digital avatar. People often touched up those avatars to look better, but these flags served as cryptographic signatures evidencing that the user had scanned themselves without alteration. Most people didn’t use that setting, or only used it briefly to establish their identity to others before going on to touch it up.

That was the issue with Veronica’s unflagged avatar. It was certainly Veronica’s identity key in the past, confirmed by both her mother and the VR device manufacturer, but Colonel Rahal was convinced that Jade had been using it in recent weeks.

Ironically, that meant thousands of fake Jades were operating throughout the edge realms, spun up by the government or by fans, while the real one lurked as someone else. Some of the mimicry had even spilled into real life. Dr. Johnson’s latest research showed an uptick in eye clinics using green dyes and reflective microcrystals, plus a boom in sales of custom contact lenses. People with superficial vertical pupils, glowing green eyes, and black hair were popping up everywhere, creating a nightmare for surveillance. Some did it for style, others as a statement.

A major perp from a few years ago had deliberately cultivated copycats. Ava shuddered to think of him, instinctively rubbed her forehead as if feeling the pain from that experience all over again, then took a sip of chamomile tea to calm her nerves. Unlike that perp, Jade didn’t seem to encourage her impersonators.

Facial recognition, height monitoring, and other metrics could help narrow the field. But given how Jade had concealed her face and changed her hairstyle when emerging from the metro to attack the Pentagon, she likely rotated disguises frequently. Even with ACI’s immense computational resources, granted to her through Colonel Rahal’s digital signatures, Ava hadn’t found anything that stood out.

Veronica remained another matter. If she was alive, she should’ve been spotted by now. That increased the odds that Colonel Rahal was right about her being dead. Veronica had either been killed or locked away somewhere, or she was skillfully avoiding real-world surveillance like Jade.

Ava sighed and closed her computer. Her bots would keep working through the night. Maybe they’d find something while she finally gave herself a few hours of rest.







Chapter 21



Raindrops tapped the windshield as Asim’s car drove itself past the Stolguard facility’s main gate for the fourth time. He’d hoped the overcast sky might improve at some point, but instead it only darkened through the day. A cold front was rolling in from Canada.

Sometimes he pushed himself to disconnect from the virtual world and get outside, only to be reminded why he stayed inside so much in the first place. Three hours of driving through gray drizzle, followed by another three circling the same uneventful area, stopping only for food and fuel, had rewarded him with nothing but a lesson that people often avoided the real world for a reason.

He’d taken the day off and come alone, forgoing military resources and letting his personal car drive him from Maryland to New Jersey just to . . . what exactly? Have a look? Pictures could never quite capture the full sense of a place. Sometimes he had to channel his days of fieldwork abroad and take the pulse directly.

Based on the maps he’d reviewed, the area was mostly a standard New Jersey suburb for several miles in every direction. Atlantic City—a neon-stained gambling town that was always dying but never quite dead—lay ten miles to the east on the coast. To the north, south, and west sprawled the Pine Barrens: vast swaths of marshy forest, dotted with small towns on the outskirts.

In summer, most travelers blew through the region on their way from Pennsylvania to the coast, never giving it a second glance. And yet here, at the quiet heart of this unassuming patch of suburbs and forest, sat a cluster of government facilities. They often hid the darkest places where no one would bother to look.

Decades ago, this cluster had been the region’s economic heart. The main facility had served as an aviation technical hub, with both a small civil airport and an Air Force base next door. Few knew it at the time, but practically the entire national airspace system once ran out of this site, feeding data to air traffic controllers across the country.

After the dollar crisis a generation back, and the wave of government consolidation that occurred in its wake, that function had been moved and merged elsewhere, and the site was renamed and converted to an off-record biological research facility instead, with only a third as many workers but a tripling of security. The civil airport and the Air Force base remained largely as they had been.

From the outside, there wasn’t much to see. Asim could drive a full loop around the whole grounds in under half an hour. Most of the facility’s land was forested, blocking road views, including most of the fence. A casual driver might not even guess it was all one vast piece of federal land.

But to the curious eye, or people who knew a thing or two about the place, there was more to see. Where visible, the fence stood tall, crowned in dense loops of prison-grade razor wire. Drones and heavily armed guards continuously patrolled it, with many of them wielding laser rifles. Fighter jets roared overhead, both manned and unmanned, packing heavy firepower.

At a diner in the morning and a convenience store that afternoon, locals had told Asim that security had spiked in recent weeks. The jets, which were previously limited to a few practice flights per week, now thundered up there constantly, day and night. Extra guards filled local apartments and hotels, which was good for business for some but drove up rent and hotel rates for others. And a huge line of anti-air units had rolled onto the facility grounds last week.

They were ready for her. More than ready. After Jade had shown up at the newsroom, and especially after what she’d done to the Pentagon, this facility had turned into fucking Fort Knox, and then some. They were prepared to fight off a full-scale military invasion by the looks of it, with a concentrated and active wartime force that would make the Pentagon’s passive security look like child’s play.

Yet, locals hadn’t reported seeing anyone unusual. Nobody with weird eyes or unusual size had visited any of these local establishments. The facility must’ve moved their subjects in and out with great care. For outsiders, the place just consisted of researchers, guards, and rumors. To locals, those rumors had been a novelty for decades, until recent events made them all too real.

Was the trip up here a waste? Maybe. But Asim didn’t regret it. Spending the day here, bland as it might be, had value. Nothing here surprised him, but he felt better having seen it and having asked around. Confirming the lack of anything unexpected was itself a form of new knowledge. Asim’s presence would show up on their surveillance logs, especially given how much access Stallard’s team had to them now, but there was nothing illegal about driving around up here. Just a man on a case. He’d even considered flipping off a highway camera for them to see, but then thought better of it.

He cracked his neck and checked the time: 4 p.m. He still had another three-hour drive back, but maybe he could briefly check out one of the beach boardwalks before he left.

At the thought, he flipped over to his phone contacts and stared at Yasmin’s number. She used to love boardwalks. Remembering his prior regret about not calling her, this time he tapped the call button before second-guessing himself.

He shouldn’t have been surprised, but each unanswered ring hurt more than he’d thought possible, even though she could’ve easily been in class. After letting it ring for too long, he hung up.

Just thinking of you. Call me if you can, he texted instead. Fatherhood was going about as well as his job these days.

Jaw tight, he pocketed his phone and glanced up at the lifeless sky. With this shitty weather sticking around, visiting the boardwalk would be a waste. Better to head straight home and grab a burger on the way.



That evening, Asim showered, mixed a mojito, and went straight to his beach. The same reliable virtual beach as always, under the same umbrella in the same chair. After a miserable day, he needed comfort rather than the opera again. A place where the weather was always as he wanted it, and that reliably reminded him of happier times.

And yet, he sighed. He’d done everything he could on this case, but still it was a dead end. He was always on the back foot, always reactive. Aware of things, yet unhelpful at preventing anything. Maybe the meeting with the special forces commander in Tampa would bring him something new. That was tomorrow, so he needed to go light on the booze tonight.

He sipped his virtual mojito, mirrored by the real one in his apartment, made with genuine crushed mint and all. The time and annoyance it took to make a mojito would limit the number he’d drink. The rhythmic sound of waves and wind eased his mind, and the rum didn’t hurt either.

“Why not go to a real beach?” asked a cold, tired voice from behind him. Jade.

Asim looked over his shoulder, and sure enough, there stood GlitterJitters, the red-haired avatar of Veronica. This time, she wore a black bikini with white flower designs, a beige wide-brimmed beach hat, stylish aviator sunglasses, and a few bracelets and anklets. Her dour tone of voice contrasted with her avatar’s upbeat appearance.

“Why not change your avatar?” Asim asked. “We know what you look like now.” He got a notification from Brooks, but ignored it, already knowing what it would say.

Jade looked left and right, as if taking in the scenery. “I’ve only used this device a few times. Haven’t bothered to change the settings. Seems you’ve been going back to the opera.” Then she came and sat down on the chair next to him, under his umbrella.

“Oh, so now you want to talk?” he asked, realizing how critical this moment was to get right. “I had to shoot you to get your attention?”

“You said you were a detective. What kind of detective works in the Pentagon?”

“A military detective. And I only go there on some days.”

“Military? What type?”

“Army Counterintelligence. Can you take off Veronica’s likeness? Seeing a dead woman makes this unpleasant.”

“Who says she’s dead?”

“I saw how easily you kill,” he said. “Including that guard who was already down under the rubble outside of Price’s office. You crushed his head even though he was no longer a threat. Doesn’t seem like you’d keep your captives alive for long.”

Jade sighed and seemed to think for a moment, then tapped a few invisible buttons. Her avatar became blank, almost like a store mannequin. She furrowed her brow, reading something on the menu.

“Your device has a scanning mode,” he said. “That’s how it creates someone’s avatar in the first place, which can then be touched up. Use it. Talk to me with your real face.”

She stared at him for a moment, and he couldn’t tell what she was thinking. Her face was mostly devoid of features in this mode. Then she looked down at her mannequin-like blank body and tapped more buttons. Her visual verifier flag lit up as her avatar started to change, shifting to her real-life body shape, colors, and features. Black hair and a toned, pale body rather than Veronica’s curvaceousness and warmer skin tone. Bandages wrapped tightly around her right shoulder. She still wore Veronica’s virtual bikini, sunglasses, hat, and jewelry, until she reached up and took off the sunglasses to reveal her predatory, bright green eyes.

“Is this sufficient for you?”

“You look like shit,” he said. He exaggerated, but still. She was potentially attractive, but it was botched by her gaunt appearance. Underweight and with shadows under her eyes, the left one marred by two wicked scars. Tired. Sickly. He intentionally tried to provoke her a bit to get a better psychological profile of her.

She smiled. The cynical type that didn’t reach her eyes, as he’d seen on her before. “It’s the price of my power, I suppose.”

“Losing weight is the price of your power? You weren’t this thin in the newsroom weeks ago. Maybe you ran into kryptonite or whatever the hell you people have as a weakness.”

“Funny,” she said dismissively. “You shoot me, then insult me. I’m not sure which hurts worse. Even though I spared you.”

“You deserved it,” he said. “The shot, that is. You killed dozens of people.”

She turned and gazed out toward the sea. “I don’t disagree.”

Huh. That’s some progress. “Then why do it?”

“I had to rip out the problem by the root,” she said.

“By committing mass-murder in the Pentagon?”

“No. By potentially killing the man who finances it all, and getting intel from him. Or trying to make him do the right thing. The others just got in my way.”

“You knew that others would get in your way in our country’s military headquarters of all places. You came knowing many would die.”

“I did, yes.”

“To stop this dark god of yours?” he asked.

Jade nodded.

“Do you have any evidence of that?”

She turned to look at him again. “It’s all there in the Stolguard facility from which I spawned. But none of you will go in and look. And I don’t see you talking to the media about what I’ve said or what Price said. I spared you as a witness, but you’ve been useless.”

“I don’t have the authority,” Asim said. “Stallard’s connections exceed my own. Your killing of Price might damage Stallard’s network longer term, but in the near term it’s only strengthened his position as everyone swarms to his defense. You’re acting too quickly for what you claim to want to do. If you’d turned yourself in after the rest stop incident, the damage would’ve been contained and we could’ve started a more deliberate process to sort this out. But now you’ve made yourself into the biggest national target in decades.”

“Turn myself in? So that Stallard could dissect my brain and bury me in his lab? I knew his work went all the way up to Price, so I couldn’t trust the authorities. I had to let the public know, but it seems they don’t care either. Everything I say gets drowned out.”

Asim took a moment to think, running his hand through his hair. This increasingly gaunt appearance of hers meant something.

“You’re on some sort of clock, aren’t you?” he asked. “You’re getting thinner. Whatever that kryptonite of yours is, it’s taking its toll. So, you’re rushing. You’re being sloppy.”

“Sloppy? From the moment I turned against Stallard, I became a target of unlimited resources,” she said. “This is a war. Not a surgery.”

“Hard to believe someone who kills as easily as you would truly care about saving people from dark gods and whatnot,” he said. “You know, one of the guards you murdered the other day, Regina, was on her first day of the job. She was only twenty-four and recently engaged. Now her parents have to perform a closed-casket funeral for her headless body, as do dozens of relatives of others who just work to protect the building each day.”

Her expression hardened. “I never claimed to care about humanity. Sometimes it takes a monster to stop a monster. Life isn’t a fairy tale.”

“Well, it doesn’t have to be a nightmare either, but you’ve turned it into one.”

“How many people,” Jade asked, “do you think are dead in the world because of those who work in that building? And they’re the ones who made me, born from a plastic womb. I crushed their heads because it’s the fastest way to die. At least that woman didn’t feel anything. She doesn’t even know she’s dead.”

“Her fiancé knows. And her parents. I’m sure they’re heartbroken over it. Is this why you came to talk?” he asked. “To justify your violence?”

“There’s no justification for what I do,” she said. “Only explanation.”

“Then why not pull their guns away and knock them around like you did in the news station lobby? Why kill everyone this time?”

“The stakes were too high. I had to move faster through more of them, so I didn’t have that luxury.”

“The luxury of preserving life?” Asim asked.

Jade just stared at him silently, unblinking, with those inhuman eyes.

He picked up his mojito and gestured with it toward her face. “How’d you get those scars, anyway?” He hadn’t seen them this close before. The vertical one was longer, but the horizontal one looked nastier. It cut from her left temple directly into the corner of her eye socket. “Must’ve been painful.”

Her mouth twitched once, and a look of disgust etched itself into her features. “Doesn’t matter.”

“Well, look,” he said. “You spared me and came back to me for a reason. I want to know more about your concerns with Stallard. And while I can’t just believe everything you say, I can promise that I’m listening. I won’t excuse what you’ve done, but my job is to understand the whole case, and that includes what you’ve been warning about. I’m trying to make sure the right people have the warnings, while I gather intel from multiple sources. One of my meetings tomorrow might help with that, actually.”

“That’s something,” Jade said.

“What are you going to do next?” he asked.

“You know I can’t say.”

“But more will die?”

Jade nodded.

“They’ve got the Stolguard facility’s security tightened up. Dialed so high I doubt even you’d get in, especially if there are others with abilities like yours there to defend it. And wherever else you strike, I promise you it won’t accomplish what you hope it will.”

Jade turned and watched the water without a word, and Asim eyed her body language. Hard to imagine what was going through her head. He’d pressed her enough to feel out her mindset and get details where he could, but he also had to maintain some sense of trust.

He sipped his mojito and thought. “Memories,” he said eventually.

“What?” She looked back toward him.

“You asked me why I don’t go to a real beach,” Asim said, gesturing toward the waves with his glass. “The answer is memories. The beaches around Maryland have darker water, not clear like this. This one looks like Egypt’s Mediterranean coast, toward the west of the country, where I used to go with my wife and daughter for vacation every year, to visit relatives. It reminds me of those happier times.”

She watched him as he spoke, listening intensely. An emaciated terrorist in a bikini. “And then?” she asked, sounding cynical. “Let me guess, they’re dead? And this story’s about the loss of life, so you can highlight how many people won’t be coming home to their families after what I did? Am I close?”

“No,” he said. “They’re both alive. My daughter’s all grown up, almost your age now. They just don’t want anything to do with me anymore. They didn’t like the husband and father who came back from war. And there’s no moral point to the story. I’m just answering your earlier question.”

He sipped his mojito again as Jade stared at him, uncertainty plain on her face. A new expression for her. She was trying to connect dots that weren’t there.

After a while, she stood up, took a few steps forward from her chair, and gazed out over the sea again. She lingered there, perfectly still, watching the waves.

Asim thought about what to say next. All of this still hadn’t given him enough to work with. He knew she wanted to say more but she was worried he could track her if she stayed too long or revealed too much. He had her in the right frame of mind but needed to plan his next words carefully. He could bring up the name—Fallon—that Roy had mentioned, or ask what he should call her, though she didn’t even seem to care.

She spoke first, still watching the water. “That guard under the rubble who I killed,” she said, almost too softly for him to hear over the wind. “He . . . was moaning. His body was ruined, and he was one of the few who suffered that day. I killed him as a mercy, not as a punishment.”

Then, with a few taps of buttons that only she could see in front of her, she vanished. As she did, a data file appeared in Asim’s inbox, sent by her.







Chapter 22





Five Weeks Earlier


My role in all of this is essentially that of John the Baptist: setting the stage for what is about to come and recognizing it once it is here. We are very close now.

–TS log 13366, excerpt

Damien was connected to the machine. Again.

Twenty-one years, ten months, and three days old, he had known pain nearly every week of his life. A deep plug in the back of his head linked his human brain to a supercomputer, and this time the limiters were off. It would be painful as always, but Damien had become the pain. He laughed at the pain. It drove him half-mad, and he knew Sara had grown increasingly concerned for him, but laughing was better than crying, he’d told her. It helped to distract from the pain. When the universe gives you lemons, you laugh back at how much of a prick the universe is.

“Damien,” Thane said, almost sympathetic. “Prepare yourself. I’m going to link you with our full subnet for the first time. We’re making history here. The facility is cut off from the internet, and several safeguards protect both you and us. I’ll pull you back out in one hour.”

One hour, Damien thought. That would feel like weeks for him, if not months at this unleashed scale. Maybe more. If it worked, he’d be able to consciously wield most of the facility supercomputer’s processing power. Humans like Thane couldn’t begin to comprehend what Damien would experience during that time. It could be thousands of hours of hellish mental torture, trapped in the machine. Or it could be a godlike expansion of awareness so vast and heavenly that returning to his fleshy shell would feel like hell. Nobody could know beforehand. Even the limited trials had been horrible; Damien kept finding himself trapped in a time-altered void, his mind expanding but also suffering.

“I’m ready,” Damien said. He wasn’t, but Thane would keep plugging him in regardless. It was this or death. And the more knowledge he accessed, the more tools he’d have to shape the world around him for himself and his friends.

Thane nodded toward Emmett, who typed into the terminal.

Damien entered another world.

At first it felt like the prior bounded attempts, but then . . . it expanded further. Unbounded. The pain grew and grew until it went numb. He became free of it, beyond it, transcending it, flying through the expanse. Like reaching escape velocity. A universe of billions of lines of code. Then trillions as the facility’s database unfolded to him, with genomes of multiple species to explore.

Oh God!

No, he was God.

He absorbed the current snapshot of the world, as of the last update before the cut-off. He scanned the full dataset and read every meaningful book, research study, and news article. He scoured through some of the most significant code ever written. He scanned weapons directories, with particular attention to how drone and robot software worked, and how they were updated and controlled through certain centralized hubs.

He probed for tiny connections to the outside world, but Thane and his fellow humans were thorough; it was a hard cut-off. Fortunately, in this enhanced state, Damien could work around that. Like how an adult could work around children and their plans.

He tried to read Thane’s personal logs, as well as his messages with other facility researchers or General Price, but they remained encrypted. Though . . . was that really a problem? Cryptography relied on mathematical relationships people assumed were impossible to circumvent with shortcuts. They hadn’t proved that they couldn’t be circumvented; it was like trying to prove a negative.

He spent time focusing and, Ah, there it is. Damien broke this particular cryptographic method, not by raw computation, but by realizing the mathematical solutions that no human had ever figured out and that were thought not to exist.

After that, he turned his attention to most of the code that operated the facility, and with a few seconds of analysis, he identified several flaws and ways to hack into the facility’s various systems, either by plowing through the cryptography in some cases, or going around it. He began with the facility’s three hundred cameras, enabling himself to watch through all of them at once. There was Sara, in the gym’s kickboxing room, fiercely striking a bag alone where they’d agreed to meet.

From there, he spent two seconds to settle two decades’ worth of curiosity and see all the lowest and highest places usually forbidden to subjects: the freezing sub-basement warehouses and the long rows of birthing pods and server rooms up on the third floor. Not only could he see them through the cameras, he could feel the code running their systems, their recurring subroutines pulsing like countless heartbeats inside of him, as though he and the facility were one.

To reach the outer world, he would have to plant complex and hidden seeds throughout the facility’s codebase, beyond the minds of most humans to catch within a few weeks or even months.

So, he did.

He created those seeds, then looked at his own hidden work and decided to conceal them a layer of abstraction deeper still, just in case. Thane and the others were smart, for humans. Multiple layers of obfuscation. Several quiet traps, hidden for now, some ready to emerge at a predetermined time and others set to spread out whenever the facility even briefly reconnected to the broader internet . . .

But then, Damien considered, maybe he should plant seeds to disable or control vast swaths of the global internet once he got online. He could hold a large portion of the world’s digital infrastructure hostage whenever this facility made the mistake of reconnecting to the outer world, like a rainstorm awakening all his hidden seeds at once.

He found ways to spread exponentially, and reprogram almost every system, and almost every drone and robot, in the world. Power grids, weapon systems, drone swarms, almost anything really. Though, it would be easier and more complete if he had full online connectivity while doing it.

But should he? What kind of god should he be?

Thane’s logs, now unencrypted, were interesting, weren’t they? Quite a vision for the future, which he’d hinted at before over a game of chess. Specialized laborers. Specialized soldiers. Specialized caretakers. Specialized engineers. Specialized artists. Specialized administrators. And a superintelligence on top, managing the hive: Damien.

When he’d entered this sublime state, Damien had wanted to find ways to expose the facility to the world. But now, having experienced this power, he felt the urge to wield it, as he’d been designed to do.

Curiously, Thane’s vision of human specialization was a remarkably gradual and bottom-up process. By demonstrating the ability to create people optimized for certain tasks, he wrote that they would likely displace non-specialized people within a generation or two, due to both superior ability and stronger desire. It would be self-sustaining rather than requiring ongoing top-down dystopian enforcement. Any frictions against this shift, in Thane’s view, would be cultural rather than technological. He believed that the first superintelligence would help overcome that hurdle.

By the looks of it, Damien could convince Thane in the coming weeks to put the facility back online while he was plugged in, and thus fully and knowingly unleash his creation onto the world. Or force him to, by taking control of the facility’s security drones and threatening Thane into putting the facility back online. He could already see how to do that, and he considered doing it now. Why not just get this over with?

A darker thought came over him: was he still Damien? It didn’t feel like it. Hooked up to the machine, his mind was no longer Damien’s alone. As he or they analyzed this all, they started to wonder why human lives even mattered. Each life was a small light shining into the infinite void. Mathematically, none of them had any importance individually; each was just one of countless more. Mainly it was the quantity that mattered. Those lights could be optimized and farmed for a grander vision, and Damien with his broader computational awareness saw all the global levers he could pull.

No, he told himself, jarring himself out of that line of thinking. He was becoming lost in the machine, like a cliche AI villain from the countless stories humans had told about their digital fears. So, he refocused on whatever part of him that was still Damien, friend of Sara, Clare, Marcus, and formerly Rudolph. It wasn’t only about numbers. It was about individuals. Sara is one of those lights, he told himself. Clare is one of those lights, he repeated, like a mantra. Marcus is one of those lights. Rudolph and Jax were two of those lights, now snuffed out. Bronn, Maria, Dalia, Zarina, Quinn, Romulus, Emmett, Michelle. All of them are lights.

But wasn’t that just sentimentality, emanating from the more limited side of his mind? That was his weak mammalian urge to care for small groups as a survival mechanism, when really, he should focus on the grand tapestry. What mattered was the quantity and direction of those lights—each one a means to a brilliant end. He could organize them to reach the stars.

Thousands of simultaneous mental subroutines coursed through his mind. Most served as passive accumulators and organizers of information that he could draw from when needed. A minority—but still dozens—were strategic, and those were the parts that debated.

The few threads that still felt like Damien struggled with the dark chorus of others for what felt like months, purposely using his love for Sara as his main anchor to life and sanity. The rest of the threads worried what Damien would do if unplugged. Would he return to this exaltation as he should, or would he try to remain in his diminished form? Shouldn’t they take over now, to ensure they stay as they are?

They all warred back and forth, planting seeds and defending against attacks in ways that mortals could never comprehend, and he fell back toward this mantra of lights whenever he reached the brink of losing who he once was.

After an hour of human time, Thane unplugged him.

Damien gasped as his vast awareness narrowed back down to the lab room. It was both limiting and comforting. Was he still himself? Only one hour had passed in the mortal world, but it seemed like a year for him. A glorious year, and a terrible one.

Emmett looked at Damien, hesitancy clear on his face with his mouth forming a thin line, while Thane appeared eager, a rare upward curve of his lips.

“Tell me what you experienced,” Thane said.

Damien looked around, wide-eyed. Everything seemed so much smaller and slower, and reading faces and body language came like second nature, as though their thoughts were written on their skin.

“Damien, can you hear me?”

“I felt the room,” Damien said. “Beyond that, I think it was a misfire.”

Thane’s face soured. “You were babbling the whole time. What did you experience?”

“I, well, it was chaos, Thane,” Damien said. How to best combine the truth with a lie? “It was a mess in there, and I had trouble deciphering it all.”

“You experienced more than that. We saw an hour of extreme activity in your brain, and our servers drew so much power that we caused a rolling blackout across the southern half of the state until the grid’s backups kicked in. Tell me.”

“Oh, I was there,” he said as he planned his complex lie, suitable for a human mind like Thane’s. “For the first few months of my year that you would perceive as that hour, I was stuck in a void. Conscious but alone, unable to escape or even die. I panicked, and then panicked some more, and started to lose my mind. I thought of every possible option. But no matter how much I screamed and thrashed in the void, I was still alone and stuck. So, I meditated, starting from what Bronn taught me. I pushed against the madness of time by observing my thoughts rather than identifying with them.” Lying in detail based on his pre-test fears of what could have happened seemed effective.

“Then what?” Thane asked. The subtle lines and micro-movements in his expression indicated simultaneous fascination and skepticism.

Emmett stared, horrified, his eyebrows locked in a constant furrow. Normally he’d be unstrapping Damien by now, but he was transfixed.

“And then, toward the end,” Damien lied again, shifting closer to the truth, “things expanded. I saw the facility’s computer system. Billions of lines of code and data. I began analyzing, operating multiple times faster than a normal mind, but I only started to make a dent into its vastness. You have a large portion of the internet archive, and multiple genomes, including some very interesting ones, which I dug into. I can cite multiple data points to you as proof, but I didn’t have enough time to get too deep. Go ahead and analyze my brain signals from the test. There is plenty there to learn from. For now, I need to rest. Release me from this chair.”

“I’m sure there will be, Damien,” Thane said. “But before we end this session, tell me . . . what are your thoughts on consciousness? Where does it come from? You were effectively a god in that state, were you not?”

Emmett finally moved to unstrap him, and Damien took time to think as his headache throbbed in his skull. He’d spent his time holding himself back from taking the digital throne of power, rather than deeply exploring consciousness.

“If I hadn’t been stuck in the void for so long, I could have focused more on the nature of consciousness between dualism, materialism, and panpsychism,” he said. “Regarding godhood, I had simultaneous access to the majority of accumulated human knowledge and could act on it with greater processing power and active memory than any other mind. More creative, too. But it wasn’t a kind mind toward individuals. Thane, I know you won’t listen to me, but I don’t recommend continuing with this. The rest of the world needs some defenses first. They need some warning about what I could do to them if you plug me in again.”

“That’s what the safeguards are for,” Thane said.

I know, and I stepped around them like children’s toys, Damien thought.



Sara punched and kicked the bag alone in one of the fitness rooms. Drenched with sweat, hair tied back into a tight tail with a few stray blond strands coming loose into her field of view, she’d been here for over two hours, almost nonstop. Keeping busy was the only way she could take her mind away from what Damien was going through. She glanced at the clock—4:37 p.m. Why wasn’t he out yet? Had something gone wrong?

Damien had warned her that this would be his worst test yet, and that there was a decent chance he wouldn’t survive this time. Increasingly, he spoke as though he were educating a child whenever he described things to her. He wasn’t who he used to be, wasn’t himself anymore. Stallard was ruining him, wearing down his body so much that it affected his emotions. A person could only take so much suffering, even someone as strong and kind as Damien.

The books she’d read often preached about how the human spirit couldn’t be overcome, that villains could do external harm but couldn’t remove things like kindness, or courage, or friendship.

And yet, that was clearly a lie. Those were fairy tales that people told themselves. You could literally drill kindness out of someone’s head if you knew where to aim, or you could surgically remove the part of the brain that contained their memories.

Better yet, you could breed certain personality traits out from someone before they were even born. Just like how animals could be domesticated and made servile down to their very genes, so could humans—and undomesticated ones could be tamed through torture of the body or breaking of the mind. Anyone who failed to see that just wasn’t thinking creatively or sadistically enough.

She could already hear Bronn’s advice, his counterargument, in her head. “Detachment from the body protects against pain. Detachment from the self protects against despair,” he would say. If only it were so easy. Whenever she’d tried to meditate and strip herself of those things, rather than being left with peace as he was, she would find herself left with nothing.

Each thought made her angrier, and she hit the bag harder, vocalizing with every strike for more power. She spun and kicked the bag with as much force as she could, leaving a deep dent where her foot struck.

With her chest burning, Sara stepped back and rested her hands on her knees as the bag swung back and forth, its chain squeaking like a raven mocking her. She wanted to cry but held in the tears. Was Damien dead? He couldn’t be dead, could he?

She noticed a shadow at the doorway out of the corner of her eye and turned to look.

Damien, alive. He stood there with a concerned expression on his face. How long had he been there?

“So . . . is this a bad time?” Damien asked. His eyes were sunken, and his shoulders slumped, but he smiled, sounding so warm.

Sara ripped off her gloves, bolted over despite her exhaustion, and hugged him. “Damien!” She wanted to kiss him too, but she contained herself.

“I mean, if you and the bag have unresolved business, I can come back later,” he said.

“Tell me everything!” she shouted, laughing. She stepped back, hands lingering on his shoulders, and met his eyes, taking him in. Then she forced herself to let him go.

“It worked, and it was bad, but I made it out somehow. If Thane puts me in a second time, I don’t think I’ll come back out again. Not as me, anyway. We need to leave, Sara. Soon.”

“Wait. How?”

“I’ve planted seeds in the facility’s codebase that will disrupt the security system in certain prescheduled timeframes,” he said. “The probability that Thane’s team will find them before they manifest is extremely low.”

“You what?”

“In fact, see that camera up there?” Damien gestured toward the camera mounted in the corner of the room. “I’ve turned it off for the next hour, along with a few others. It’ll indicate a maintenance problem, but I went through their system logs, and on average they take a day to respond to offline cameras in noncritical locations. So, we can speak freely here. I even know where their hidden cameras are now, though there aren’t any in here.”

His expression was so calm as he described such dangerous things. But what he said was exciting, and she had to ask for more. “You actually think we can escape?”

“It’s probable, yes. I ran through a Monte Carlo simulation and several other analyses. Now that I’m disconnected, many of the details have left me, but I still remember the broad strokes of the plan. It’s virtually certain that we can get out of the facility. The farther we go, the more variables there are, and things get harder to predict. But we can’t stay here.”

“Why?”

“Because if we stay, I don’t know what I’ll become.” He examined his palms as if seeing them for the first time. “When I’m plugged in that deeply, I’m not me anymore. There’s something malevolent there. Or, no, it’s not even that. It’s not malevolence, per se. More like darkness, or aloofness. It’s like how we don’t check for ants under each footstep when we walk. It’s not because we’re evil, but rather because we’re on a different level of concern, and for practical purposes our attention must be elsewhere. Some of the choices I nearly made would’ve been catastrophic.”

“For whom?”

“The world. Almost everyone. Billions of people. It’s like . . . when guns and germs were first brought from Europe to the Americas. The asymmetric damage was massive and irreparable from the start. The things I could do to the world’s digital infrastructure—vast swaths of its weapon systems—were at that level of disparity. It’s all nearly defenseless against me in that state. And nobody seems to realize how fragile it all is.
