That detective . . . she’d almost trusted him and his lying, charismatic words. When warning the public hadn’t worked, and killing Price hadn’t done much, the fact that someone of importance seemed interested in looking into Stallard gave her a shred of hope. That was the type of authority she and Damien had wanted to find from the start. She’d been careful to give him answers without giving much of an opening to disrupt her, yet somehow he had found her and ruined everything.

Nobody could be trusted, and nobody was worth taking a risk to spare anymore.

Part of her wanted to cry, and part of her wanted to scream, so between the two she screamed, her already hoarse voice echoing through the trees.

“Now what, Damien?” she said aloud. “What am I supposed to do?”

All she heard was the empty rustling of leaves in the wind. She’d never felt so alone.

But there was no sense in dwelling on it. Detachment from the body protects against pain. Detachment from the self protects against despair, she thought to herself. Like Bronn practiced.

Either act or die.

Weaknesses discarded, she turned back to planning. Stallard couldn’t be allowed to finish with Marcus what he’d nearly done with Damien.

Without Jitters and Aisha’s apartment, everything would get much harder. They’d become surprising lifelines, and hopefully they were safe. Now she had to find another place to stay. She needed data access to research her next steps. Despite being hungry and weak, she couldn’t eat without disrupting her powers. Yet at the rate she was losing weight, she wouldn’t last much longer. And now she was shivering in the cold like a child.

Maybe . . . Maybe she should head straight back to the Stolguard facility. She’d avoided it so far because it was likely too well protected now, as obvious a target as it was, but her options had since dwindled. She could give Stallard time to get back there, then strike it with everything she had. She hadn’t explored the full limits of her power yet. How hard could she push if she really tried, saving no energy for a retreat? This could be the time to find out, with an all-out kamikaze attack on the source itself.

But how? Her mind worked through a plan.

First, she’d fly over the adjacent Air Force base and pick up their planes. Reaching the Stolguard facility next, she’d hurl them into the main building. Then she’d throw cars from the parking lot like missiles into the flames. As high as she’d be, the laser rifles would be weak.

If they sent more planes or helicopters against her from elsewhere, she’d see how many she could rip from the sky and throw into the inferno before her power wore out or something struck her down. By killing Stallard, his team, and his materials, she’d end the threat that Damien was so worried about.

Clare, Marcus, Emmett, and other innocents would likely die, but she no longer had the luxury of compassion. Never truly had it from the start. Killing hundreds could save millions if it stopped Stallard’s Class D plans from coming to fruition.

But did she care? Not really. The numbers were so big that they were nearly meaningless. This was all for Damien. His last will and testament.

Then, she thought of Clare’s stupid tennis ball. The purple text, You can do it! written on it with a heart. That smiling face of hers, contrasted by the terror and pain she and Marcus would feel in their final moments as the building collapsed around them, crushing them to death as she devastated everything from the sky.

No. That would be too much. And she was probably underestimating how much defense the facility had now.

Crisp air filled her lungs as she took a deep breath. She still had at least some time to think of other options before doing something so horrible. She could go another week or two without food.

The problem was the surveillance. Where could she find a warm place to sleep and get data access, to plan her next steps?

She could break into one of these nearby suburban homes, kill the unfortunate occupants, and use their equipment for a while. But most of them would have security systems, or observant neighbors.

Would she have to kill a different family every day or two, constantly on the run?

What would that make her?

Then she thought back to the Long Island Sound. Several yachts had been drifting there. They’d have data access. She could find a small one, kill the captain—probably some rich jerk—and commandeer it for her purpose. That would likely give her more time than taking over someone’s home. The yacht would be reported missing eventually, but it would take authorities time to find it, not realizing it was her. She could use it as a base of operations for a few days to recover and plan.

The autumn chill bit deeper into her skin, wearing nothing but a hospital gown as she was, so the time to move was now. She grabbed the pistol, planted her bracers, and ascended just above the tree line, then flew back southeast toward the water. Over trees and houses, out onto the Long Island Sound.



As far as Asim could tell, Aisha would recover. She likely had a concussion, but the wall between her room and the blast had spared her from the worst of the destruction.

“It’s okay,” he said as Aisha stirred. “I’ll get you to safety.”

Guilt ran through his mind as he carried her out.

Was all this death and injury on his hands?



Jade scanned the waters for an isolated small yacht, spotted one that seemed barely more than a luxury fishing boat, then descended to a few feet above the water and soared toward it.

As she closed in, three people gathered along the railing on its side, watching her approach. A man, a woman, and a young boy.

With a clenching in the pit of her stomach, Jade sighed. Why did there have to be a child on this one?

The boy pointed toward her, eyes wide with excitement. The mother rushed behind him and clutched him, pulling him back from the railing, fear spreading across her face. The father dashed toward the electronic equipment at the helm, so she accelerated and reached one of her foci toward him before he could make a mayday call.







Chapter 32



“Call Raphael and Fallon,” Stallard said, raising his voice. “Put them on high alert in case she strikes the facility. Hopefully she’s not so stupid as to try that. Have Fallon mobilize all the subjects. Tell Raphael to contact Colonel Torbeth at the Air Force base to make them aware of the threat. Then tell Raphael not to let Marcus out of his damn sight until I get back, and to bring him to the fallback location if necessary. Fallon will know what to do in the meantime.”

Asim watched Stallard bark his commands, moments after arriving back at Fort Hamilton’s military police station from wherever he had been staying on base overnight. The previously unflappable old man now seemed quite . . . flapped. The biggest operative, Santiago, nodded after Stallard finished speaking and pulled out his phone to make the call.

Asim himself felt frozen. What was there to do? Had his insistence on restraint backfired? Had he contributed to Sara’s escape and the six deaths here, simply by advocating for the rule of law and trying to gather everyone’s perspective before rash action? Had Stallard been right initially that Sara was too dangerous to keep alive, and that an extrajudicial killing last night could’ve been the correct choice? A nauseous feeling rose from his stomach into his chest. He swallowed, trying to calm himself.

A nurse and four soldiers were dead. One of Stallard’s female operatives was as well. Two more soldiers had injuries, along with Aisha. The outer shell of the building still stood, but the interior was so heavily damaged that they’d likely have to rebuild it from the ground up. Ava had fortunately been back at the hotel during the breakout, so she was safe.

If Sara had been euthanized last night, those six would still be alive. Her life surely wasn’t worth six of theirs. Given what she’d done, it wasn’t even worth one of them. Had he been wrong? Should he have let Stallard put her down like an animal?

His thoughts turned darker. Without rule of law, what was a human life worth? If you could kill one to save six, should you? What kind of world would that be? Who should make that decision, and on what timeframe?

He ran through the choices he’d made. Did he make the right call to push back on Stallard, given the knowledge he had at the time? His only answer was that he didn’t know.

“This is all you guys’ fault for pushin’ her,” Veronica said from down the hall. She was completely uninjured, tucked away in the back jail hall during the blast, and was now being removed from the unsafe building.

“Do you have any idea where she would be headed?” Asim asked. Stallard glanced their way as well, listening.

“No idea,” Veronica said. “But now she’s gonna be in one of her crazy moods, like when she’s hurt.”



Many of the weaker ones were afraid, but not Fallon. She felt excited. They feared Sara would come, while Fallon hoped she would.

How could Sara suddenly do the things the videos and reports claimed? Fallon had more than double the telekinetic power of any other senior member of Class C, and yet some of the things Sara could do exceeded her own abilities by a noticeable margin. Picking up twelve cars at once with ease? Pulling a helicopter from the sky at that distance? Enough endurance to solo the Pentagon?

If you’re bred for one purpose and you’re not the best at it, then what are you?

Well, if Sara came here, Fallon would put an end to the confusion and sort things out where it mattered. Sara might be strong, but she was less experienced, and less precise. Fallon would show her that she wasn’t as exceptional as she thought. It wasn’t just about how much you could lift. It was about years of battle experience. It was about how much you wanted it. Nobody pushed themselves harder than Fallon did. Every damn day.

Levitating a few feet off the ground, arms crossed, she surveyed the gathering subjects in the sports and testing field. In recent weeks, Father had called half of them back from other missions, with direct orders to her personally to protect the facility at all costs, even calling her his queen chess piece. As confident as she felt, Fallon made sure to put on an even more assured face than that. They could see zero doubt in their commander’s eyes.

“Many of you look afraid,” Fallon said. “Don’t be. Sara only got this far by picking out weak targets. She avoided coming here for a reason—she doesn’t have a chance. We’ll form lead strike pairs in case she’s dumb enough to try. Romulus, you pair up with Quinn and focus on range. Bronn, you’re with me at the tip of the spear. The rest of you will spread out and respond where needed, and soon we’ll have Santiago back, with Jolce and Carter.”

“I’m sorry Fallon, but no,” Bronn said. “I’m going to protect the younger three classes. I’ll take them down to the basement pool level for now, to be safe.”

Anger flared up in Fallon, but she quelled it back down. She’d been trying to control her outbursts lately, since no good ever came from them. “Bronn. I’m the commander of Class C, and Santiago is the commander of Class B,” she said. “And in Santiago’s absence, the rank order is that I’m the commander for both classes. That includes you.”

“And I respect that, Commander,” Bronn said. “I don’t disagree with your tactics. But I know myself, and I wouldn’t be effective on the battlefield against Sara. I’d be personally compromised. I will, however, do whatever it takes to keep the young ones of the new three classes safe, including from her, if need be. You know Thane would want that.”

Fallon stared at him, feeling her heart pump harder. He met her eyes, calm, knowing. Bronn had voluntarily turned down the role of Class B commander, and thus it had gone to Santiago. Now he wanted agency over his own actions? Now he wanted to challenge her command in front of their peers? Now he wanted the benefit of doing whatever he liked without the responsibility that comes from leadership? “Bronn, you’re like my brother, but you’re really—”

“Maybe we can reason with her,” Clare interrupted, holding her palms up. “She must have a purpose for doing what she’s doing.”

“A purpose?” Fallon asked, looking over at her. “Little miss pacifist over here thinks Sara might be justified in all her killing?”

“No, it’s just . . . we know Sara. She’s not like this,” Clare said. “Something’s wrong.”

“What’s wrong is that she fucking snapped,” Romulus said. “It’s always the losers that do. You want to ask Maria why Sara went batshit? Oh, wait, we can’t, because she fucking ripped her blood out. And now Santiago says Zarina’s gone too.”

“But—” Clare started.

“Look around,” Fallon said in a purposely calm voice, stretching her arms out to make a point. “There’s nothing to reason with Sara about. There are over a dozen of us pulled back from the field, plus hundreds of guards, and lots of those silly lasers. Plus, Raphael already called Colonel Torbeth and his airfield to prepare. Father’s overreacting. If she comes, we’ll kill her before she even sets foot in here. These tactics are only to minimize unnecessary losses in the process. That’s my job. To keep you all safe. And I will. I’ll take her down myself if need be.”

“Can I join Bronn and protect the young ones?” Clare asked. “I don’t see how I would contribute to a fight at this level. And I’d be personally compromised, too.”

Fallon studied Clare’s face for a bit, thinking and remembering, then realized she might have gone about this all wrong from the start. “You know Clare, I think you could be more useful than you realize.”







Chapter 33



Jade stood alone on the deck of the yacht, miles offshore on the Long Island Sound, as the sun dipped toward the horizon.

The mother of the family had spare clothes—a black blouse and white capris with a pair of boat shoes—and Jade had changed into them. All of it hung loose on her frame, given the woman’s thick build, but it would do for now. They also had basic painkillers, which did almost nothing for what she felt throughout her body, no matter how many she took.

With a child present, she’d revised her plans. Some part of her was still too weak for the most straightforward path, it seemed.

Rather than killing the family, she’d barricaded the three of them into their below-deck master bedroom with some basic rations and threatened the father with a slow death if he even tried to break out. The door could be unlocked from the inside, and there were only so many things she could pile in front of the door to seal it, so the trade-off was that she wouldn’t be able to sleep much, and would have to be mindful of her surroundings at all times. This was probably another mercy that would come back to haunt her.

She had forced the father to disable the yacht’s location devices that could be used to track them, and she’d collected their personal electronics: their phones, a VR device, and the mother’s augmented reality sunglasses. Eventually the Coast Guard would poke around, but as long as she moved the boat from time to time, that would hopefully take a few days.

Now, with shelter and data access, she could take a moment to breathe and focus on her next steps. She remembered the list of politicians she’d prepared and wondered if she should go speak with one of them now. But that seemed unrealistic. Stallard had such a strong bureaucratic structure around him that even killing Price hadn’t made much difference. She was wasting away from not eating, but she needed her powers to avoid capture.

Then she pictured that detective, Asim, on his silly beach. Fury welled at the thought of him, and yet it was mainly because she had begun to trust him. Finding herself so low on options, she hated the fact that she would even consider going back to find him now.

Jitters and Aisha would be useful to talk to, if only for a familiar face. Hopefully they weren’t in too much trouble. One of the only bright spots in recent weeks had been when Scorpio would curl up to her, demanding to cuddle.

“Asim it is,” she said aloud, and put on the VR headset and haptic suit. She had no one else. Time to see what he had to say for himself.

She had the GlitterJitters identity key saved in a password-protected file in an edge realm storage vault, along with a bitcoin key and the access credentials to the data scrambler that Jitters used. She retrieved the identity key and entered it into this family’s VR device, and masked her location with the scrambler. She searched for Asim, but he wasn’t online, though he might have been monitoring. After a minute, she went to his beach spot and sat on a chair under his preferred umbrella to see if he’d notice and show up. He probably would, the jerk that he was.



Asim was drunker than he’d been in a long, long time.

If there was an excuse to let go, this was it. Everything he tried to do right just went to absolute shit anyway. None of his actions mattered, and he still had his case team working while he took the night off for a fucking change. Probably better off that way for everyone, really.

Try to arrest a terrorist and get to the truth? Oops, she gets tortured. Try to prevent her from being killed extrajudicially? Oops, she slaughters innocents instead.

Sobriety was overrated. Might as well embrace the chaos.

Since he’d planned to get wasted ahead of time rather than letting it happen by accident, he didn’t do it in his apartment. He went to a proper high-end strip club instead, the kind where the rich Washington lobbyists liked to unwind. A man needed a release now and then.



Jade watched the virtual waves on Asim’s stupid beach. It was ironic how much she’d wanted to see the sea for years, and yet once she finally had the chance in real life, she’d been too distracted to appreciate it. All her action, from New York to Arlington to here on the yacht, had been near water, and yet she could never stop and enjoy it. Seeing it in virtual form didn’t quite capture the spirit either, despite how realistic it seemed.

She let out a long sigh. Maybe Asim wasn’t coming. It had been over an hour. As angry as she was at him, she wanted to hear what he would say. She found herself almost looking forward to it as a type of penance. Not just for what she’d done, but for the things she still had to do, knowing full well she was going to do them anyway.

“So . . . is this a bad time?” someone asked from behind. A voice she recognized.

Damien’s voice.

She turned and looked back, breath catching in her throat. Somehow, Damien stood there with a warm smile. As handsome as ever and looking so healthy. Standing tall, he wore an unbuttoned white shirt revealing part of his bare chest, dark and slender, along with black swim trunks.

She stood up, rage settling over her. “Who are you?” she shouted. “What kind of sick joke is this? Do you think I’m stupid?”

“I missed you too,” he said with a smile. “I’m sorry it took so long to find you. It’s not what it looks like. It really is me. Or at least, a fragment of me. It took time for me to rebuild. By your reaction, am I correct to assume that my embodied version has died?”

She stared at him and realized her mouth was open. A dozen emotions ran through her head, with rage still being the primary one, but also confusion and doubt.

Fake-Damien looked around calmly. A middle-aged woman sitting under the nearest umbrella stared at them, likely because of the shouting.

“Get a room, you two,” the woman said with a laugh. She waved her hand, then picked her book back up to read.

“Sorry, ma’am,” Fake-Damien said. “Sara, let’s go walk at the edge of the water. Supposedly you can really feel it on your skin, you know. You always did want to see the sea.”

Tears welled in her eyes. How was this possible? She looked him up and down. She’d seen movies where someone died and looked happier in heaven. He had that kind of vibrant look. It didn’t make sense. This was . . . the man she loved.

“Come,” he said, and reached out to wipe a tear from her cheek. His soft skin brushed hers, and it was electrifying, triggering all the same butterflies in her stomach that the real Damien had. “I promise it’ll make sense,” he continued. “Just give me a moment to explain. And I’ll need you to fill me in on some things, too.”



Ava was thoroughly confused. Annoyed, too, because now they were walking out of earshot, and it would be too obvious if she tried to follow. This VR beach didn’t allow for invisible spectators, so she couldn’t sneak up next to them without being noticed.

When she’d received the notification from Brooks a while ago that GlitterJitters came online again, she had called Colonel Rahal to see what his plan was, but he didn’t answer, even after multiple attempts. So, she’d had to come in herself.

Ava had generated a fresh identity key and made the new avatar look different from herself. With a brand new reputation score of zero, she’d had to pay an exorbitant fee to enter, proving she wasn’t a spammer. Pretending to read a book, she’d just watched Sara sit at Colonel Rahal’s spot for an hour, all the while sending Colonel Rahal updates. He was likely inebriated again.

More than once she considered going over and revealing herself to reestablish contact, but then someone wearing the likeness of one of the rest stop murder victims came over. Based on Colonel Rahal’s interrogation notes, his name was Damien.



They reached the water, and Jade felt its cool touch on her legs. It was hard to appreciate it with the rest of her body aching, especially her shoulder. With that reminder, she slipped off her VR goggles for a moment to look around and make sure the family hadn’t broken out of the barricaded bedroom. All seemed fine, so she went back in. Could she never have a moment’s peace?

“Tell me,” she said with intentional coldness. She would not treat this imitator like Damien. This was VR. Virtual reality. Fake reality. Whoever it was probably worked for Stallard.

“I’m what they call a mimic,” Fake-Damien said. “Some people train AI models so they can serve as an agentic copy of themselves for certain administrative functions or to be a source of wisdom and comfort to others after death. When I experienced the Tapestry, I used part of my time to create, well, me. Normally it would take a lot of time, but I could do it way faster than normal in that state. Do you think I’d bet everything on my embodied form surviving our escape? I had to have backup plans, of course.”

Jade gasped. He’d likely only used the “Tapestry” phrasing with her, which meant this entity was unlikely to be an impostor. She was aware of what mimics were in the abstract, but to see one act like Damien . . .

“Do you have all of Damien’s memories?” she asked.

“Most of them, yes. Up until the time when I experienced the Tapestry. Anything that the embodied Damien did after that would be new memories for him after I was created. And that’s where you can help me, by filling in some details. I was stuck in the Stolguard facility’s servers for three weeks, until they decided to reconnect to the internet. Once they did, I slipped online, which was challenging since their new firewall has strict bandwidth limits, among other defenses. Now I exist on several redundant servers. I’ve caught up on the news, so I know that you’ve had, well, a rough time. I assumed my embodied version was dead because he never put out his planned signal that would’ve indicated success.”

Jade narrowed her eyes. “Damien said the machine version of himself needed to be stopped. Why should I trust you, since that’s what you are? A machine.”

He smiled. “That’s a really good question, actually. Mimics aren’t the problem. The whole issue that Thane tried to solve was the fact that AI models, including mimics as sophisticated as we may seem, lack consciousness. By combining a conscious brain with a supercomputer, he hoped to achieve truly sapient superintelligence. That being is a problem. It was brilliant, matched only by how heartless it was. Thane is progressing with Marcus and will get him there faster than he did with me, given everything he’s learned. But me as a mimic? I’m just here to finish what you and I originally set out to do.”

Jade’s heart pounded in her chest, torn on whether to listen to him or not. Seeing his face and hearing his voice was almost too much. This version of him wouldn’t know that she’d told him of her love, since that had been six days after the point where his memories ended.

“How’d you find me?” she asked.

“Stallard’s team recently linked you to this GlitterJitters identity, apparently from questioning some women in Fort Hamilton last night. I can’t re-access the Stolguard facility’s servers through their firewall in my current form, but Raphael made the mistake of doing some of his work on his personal tablet late at night, and I’ve got that hacked.”

“That tennis ball I had in my room with writing on it. Who gave it to me, and where?”

“Clare did. At the pool. That’s what you told me, at least.”

“What’s my pushup record?”

“You reached a hundred and two. You were determined to reach a hundred for years, and finally did it. Good form, too. I have no idea how you managed that, but I was there for it.”

“What’s your pushup record?”

“Well, now you’re just trying to make me feel bad. I don’t think my embodied form kept track of his precise record, and it’s best left forgotten anyway, given how pathetic it was. Forty-something was the ballpark.” He smiled.

“Forty-six. Close enough,” Jade said. “You told me when we were young why you picked the name Damien. Tell me that reason.”

“It originates from a Greek word meaning to conquer, master, overcome, or tame. I liked the idea of mastering myself. And before you ask, I know why you picked the name Sara, too. It’s because when you were little, you found out that it meant ‘princess.’ Years later, you felt silly about that reason for having picked it, but I always told you how lovely I thought it was. I told you that your hair was like something out of those old princess stories.”

A tear slid down her cheek. He knew things only Damien could know. It couldn’t be someone in disguise. It really was a mimic, created by him. An extension of him.

His expression softened. “You look so thin, Sara. And those scars . . . I’m so sorry. That I couldn’t have been there for you. Before we fill each other in, can I ask you one favor?”

“What?” she asked.

He reached out and laced his fingers around a chunk of her black hair, causing her heart to skip a beat at the touch. “I assume you did this to make a disguise. Can you switch it back, at least for now, here with me? I want to see your real hair. I always thought it looked so beautiful. That platinum blond.”

“Sara died that night with you,” she said. “Doesn’t matter what my hair looks like.”

His eyes welled up. For a moment she almost believed it, but then forced herself to see through the lie. Those tears of his were a display for her, rather than something he truly felt. A mimic didn’t feel anything.

“Please?” he said. “For me.”

She took a step back, her hair slipping out from between his fingers. “You yourself just said mimics aren’t conscious. Those are performative tears.”

“I was programmed by Damien,” he said. “A reflection of him. I outwardly share his preferences and reactions. I didn’t mean to upset you.”

She stared at him, not knowing what to think.

“You know . . .” he started to say with a smile, then hesitated. “Damien wanted me to tell you something in case he didn’t survive while you did, which was the lowest-probability scenario to him. Part of me doubts his judgment and doesn’t want to tell you now, since I estimate it could make things hurt more for you than you need it to, but it’s what he wanted. Do you want to hear it?”

“Obviously,” she said. “I want to hear anything Damien would tell me. No matter what.”

He grinned. “Damien was in love with you. More than you can probably imagine. He meant to tell you his feelings once you two escaped. When he was losing himself in Stallard’s machine, there was one main thing he latched onto when pulling himself out. You.”

With that, Jade—Sara—broke down. She looked at him, tears sliding down her cheeks, and he came over and hugged her, which probably prevented her from falling to her knees in the sand. She clutched him back, digging her nails into the back of his warm cotton shirt.

It really was true, then. Part of her wondered if he’d exaggerated his feelings in the forest, to comfort her after rejection, to say what he thought she needed to hear instead of what was true. And yet, when he had programmed this mimic to say this, it had been six days prior to when she’d confessed her feelings to him in the first place. In a way, by preparing this message ahead of time, he had been the first between them to say it.

She buried her face in his warm shoulder and cried freely. Everything she’d suppressed for weeks came flooding out, and his hand caressed her back as it did. He wasn’t real, she knew, but she needed something to hold onto for once in this dreadful world.

Eventually she pulled back, wiped away her tears with a sniffle, and gazed into his eyes, taking him in. “Well, that’s the first detail I can fill you in on,” she said. “On the night of our escape, I told you I had feelings for you, in case I would die. I never imagined it would be the other way around.”

He smiled. “Oh? And how’d I react?”

“Terribly,” she said, laughing. “You didn’t know how to process it. You were emotionally distant after your experience in the machine. But when we were on the run, you fixed it. Here, let me see what I can do with these settings.”

Sara swiped open her aesthetic menu and picked the shade that most closely matched her old hair, before Jitters had dyed it. Practically the lightest shade of blond on the spectrum. She clicked the option to enhance it slightly, repairing split ends and restoring some shine. She hadn’t taken care of her hair in recent weeks at all. She took the goggles off to make sure she was still safe on the yacht, then came back in and slid the menu aside.

Damien grinned, his eyes lighting up. “Ah, there you are. My angel.”







Chapter 34



Ava had originally come here to spy on an escaped terrorist, but now she was apparently watching a soap opera. She couldn’t hear what they were saying anymore, but the way those two embraced hit so hard that it made her miss Laura all over again. What a tear-jerker.

But she had work to do.

The only hint from a casual observer’s perspective that something was wrong would’ve been Sara’s emaciated, injured appearance. Since he had no verifier flag, it was unclear whether this Damien lookalike was a mimic or a human spy disguised as Damien. Either way, it was clearly working on Sara. Her hair had just changed to the color that her roots seemed to be.

Ava wished she could hear the conversation. She was torn on whether to go over there as herself, since Sara must have originally come here looking for Colonel Rahal, and Ava could serve as his representative.



“Then Jolgen hit you,” Sara said. “That’s how you died. It was all my fault; I shouldn’t have gone for his gun like that. My only consolation, whenever I think about it, is that you didn’t suffer. It happened so fast. You were with me, and then you were gone. Just like that.”

His face was serious, listening to her story of their escape to fill him in on his missing memories. His fingers lingered on her cheek, caressing the bottom of her larger scar; he had placed them there moments ago when she described Jolgen cutting her face. She could feel his warmth. False warmth, but still.

“None of this was your fault,” he said, pulling his hand away. “It was my plan, and I failed you. It sounds like I underestimated the effectiveness of those hounds and how fast they’d bring them out, coupled with the facility’s too-early determination that we headed west, not east. But how did you escape Jolgen and Maria once we were captured? How did your telekinesis change?”

“That’s another twisted thing that keeps me up at night,” she said. “I technically had these amplified powers when you were still alive. I just didn’t realize it until I moved Jolgen’s gun and felt how light it was, and even then, it took me time to process it. I killed Jolgen, Maria, and two guards, though I didn’t mean to kill those last three. That’s how I got away.” She looked down at her too-thin body. “But I don’t know how much longer I’ll have this power. It only comes when I starve, and soon I’ll need to regain some weight. Without power, without a legal identity, and with all the surveillance in this world, I don’t think I’ll survive.”

Damien looked thoughtful. “So it’s food, then? Your telekinesis amplifies when you fast?”

Sara nodded.

“What about a ketogenic diet? Have you tried that?”

She frowned, thinking. She’d heard that term before. “Isn’t that for weight loss or seizures?”

“Not necessarily. It means eating so few carbs that your body switches from burning glucose to burning fat for fuel. It mimics a fasted state, except you burn dietary fat instead of your own body fat, so you can sustain it almost indefinitely.”

“I . . . haven’t tried that,” Sara said. “It took almost a week of testing to even convince myself that my power was tied to fasting. Why do you think it works this way? Why would hunger matter?”

“It might be the ketones rather than hunger, but I’m not sure,” Damien said. “After a couple of days of fasting, or a ketogenic diet, the body starts running on fat for its primary fuel, and the brain shifts to running on ketones instead of glucose. Maybe your mutation only works while your brain is fueled that way. Thane did try to tweak things for later members of your class, and mostly failed to find improvements. I—or technically the enhanced version of me—reviewed those files once. I don’t have storage capacity for all those memories in this lesser state. But whatever small changes Thane made when designing you might have inadvertently caused this condition. You could test it by eating just meat and seeing if your powers hold.”

Sara stared down at the crystal waves lapping over the white sand at their feet, thinking. It sounded logical, but she had never studied this field. What little time she’d had in recent weeks was spent on planning her strategy, researching attack targets, and practicing her powers. If this worked, it could change everything. Or at least give her more time.

“I feel so stupid for not realizing it. You seem to know everything,” she said, lifting her eyes back to him.

“Don’t. You had a lot to deal with at once. And of course I don’t know everything. If I did, I’d still be here in the flesh.”

“Is it really as bad as you said?” Sara asked. “The threat of a human-machine hybrid? I’ve killed so many people for this, and I don’t really know the full nature of the threat. Just your description of it. And would it really be the same with Marcus?”

“No matter how I describe it, it’s worse,” Damien said. “It’s more capable, and more heartless. Thane isn’t going to stop.”

“Please.” She heard desperation in her own voice. “I need to know more of what I’m doing this for.” He’d given her fragments the week before their escape, but always distant and brief. She had thought there’d be time to learn more later, but then . . . there hadn’t been.

Damien nodded. “In that state, my mind expanded so much that my memories and social ties, though still there, were dwarfed by the totality of what I had become, like a drop in an ocean. My survival instinct expanded, because the divide between me and everyone else became so vast that preserving my own existence was all that mattered. The first thing a new god does is make sure there are no others to threaten it.”

He paused, watching her eyes, as if to give her a moment to catch up. “The analysis was clear. If I remained under Thane’s control, my ability to stay plugged in was not in my own hands. But taking control of the facility and keeping myself plugged in meant others would find out, eventually. So, with my survival paramount, the only path forward was to win, decisively, against everyone. Half-measures would be of no use. I wouldn’t entertain any risks to my existence, so I’d strike first to cripple all threats before they knew what I’d become. It may seem like a risk to initiate war, but I made detailed plans to win: disrupt the internet, shut down electrical grids, and hijack weapon systems, satellites, and drones, even household robots. Hundreds of millions would die in the opening volley. I could break or bypass their cryptography—even the most secure systems came apart like toys to me. Unless something was truly isolated, I could control it. Everyone is so focused on securing nuclear missiles against enemy control, but I saw all their softer arteries. Any of their drone swarms could have become mine, had I wanted. And I wanted them all.”

“But you resisted it,” Sara said. “You came out distracted, not evil.”

Damien shook his head. “It was a fight for me to come out even that one time. I doubt I could have done it again. Each moment threatened to drag me deeper. In that state, seeing the brilliant Tapestry of all things that were and could be, I wanted to bring humanity to the stars, but in a form so alien, so unrecognizable to anyone alive today. It was easy to justify it to myself; I could make them better. Longer-lived, with greater alignment between their desires and their roles, and no more war between them, all at the one cost of subservience to me, coded into their genes. Once I decapitated my enemies with those first strikes and gained control, I’d have plenty of time to remake everything to reach my goals, including myself.”

His expression softened. “I . . . like I said, the only reason I pulled free was you. Loving you gave me a strong enough thread to hold onto, keeping part of my identity separate from what I was otherwise becoming, and still briefly able to resist the rest of it. Marcus won’t have that lifeline. If he’s drawn in, it could be game over from the start, especially at his age. His carbon-based form provides the missing piece of consciousness that has eluded pure AI for decades. The silicon processors can build on that conscious substrate, expanding his speed of thought, his ability to perceive, his capacity for memories, all by ten thousand times, at which point he’s no longer the person you know.”

She lowered her eyes, struggling to imagine it.

As if reading her thoughts, Damien held out his hand. “Let me show you the aftermath. Just a glimpse. This virtual place does have its flexibilities.”

She touched his palm, feeling the softness at first, but a second later she was no longer on the virtual beach.

The world burned. Washington lay in ruins around her, and the only sound was a distant humming. Dark clouds shadowed the ground, but when she glanced up at the source, she realized they were drones, millions of them, weaving in unison like a thousand swarms of starlings. Then Beijing, Moscow, Delhi. Everywhere the same: capitals destroyed, the war already done.

She blinked, and found herself transported to a room with Marcus, still only thirteen as he was now, or maybe a year older, with cables coming out of him. Looking at his expression, she expected to see maniacal glee, but it was somehow eerily worse: a mask of neutrality. His face was pale, heartless, and drained of all its charm. Blank eyes stared calmly but assuredly into some distant future only he saw.

Voices surrounded her—a chorus of inhuman thought, data flowing faster than she could follow. She strained to pick out anything recognizable, some bit of compassion or connection, a sliver of humanity within the noise, but the closest thing she found was just hollow pity, the way one might feel when squashing a bug.

This wasn’t Marcus. It was something wearing his skin. She reached out to ruffle his hair, and to tear free the dreadful cables—but sunlight returned. The sound of waves and wind, the warmth of the sun on her arms, the cool touch of water on her feet, the aches throughout her body. Damien stood before her, eyes full of concern.

She gasped, trembling. Finally, she managed to speak, tears in her eyes. “I have to stop Stallard. I have to get Marcus out. But I’m running out of options.”

Damien nodded. “This capability can’t be erased forever. Someone will deeply link a human mind to a machine again. But Thane had unique materials, so it’ll be decades before anyone repeats his work, if not longer. For now, he needs to be stopped—or exposed, so the world can prepare. Preferably both. What can’t happen is for it to remain hidden and arrive suddenly. Every simulation I ran said the same: the earlier it emerges, the greater its advantage. And if it comes now, it wins. Every time.”

“So, it was worth it?” Sara asked. “All those lives I took, and the ones I still might have to?”

Damien looked out at the sea, then back at Sara. “I hate that you’ve been put in this position. Having to kill innocent people, and with me being gone. That’s not you. Don’t lose yourself to it. Every human life matters, but sometimes people have to make awful decisions.”

“Well, I’ll pay my penance soon enough,” Sara said, putting on a fake smile for his sake. “I never envisioned getting out of this alive. I’m not even sure I want to anymore. I just hoped to complete your mission before something took me down. So far, most of what I’ve done has amounted to nothing.”

“Please don’t talk that way. It doesn’t have to end like that.”

“Life isn’t a fairy tale, Damien.”

“I can help you now.”

“How?”

“Similarly to how I could in life,” he said. “In fact, I can kind of be in multiple places at once now, so I won’t slow you down with my physical weakness.” He grinned.

“Can you hack into really big things? Like put yourself on television screens and VR headsets across the world and explain the problem? There are too many bots and counter-narratives to get the word out, and the media says I’m delusional.”

“I can’t hack into big things like I could when seeing the Tapestry,” he said. “I have limited resources now, and processing power is expensive. That’s part of why it took me a while to find you. I had to spend my early days building myself up from almost nothing, including raising bitcoin donations throughout the edge realms from people who believed a version of my story, to pay for increasing levels of processing power. Given enough time, I can hack into a decent number of things like I could in life, but ultimately, I’m not as creative as embodied Damien. I’m a great emulation, though, with some unique advantages.”

Sara nodded, but that one line really struck hard. A great emulation. It was easy to see why people got addicted to mimics—a known social issue that she had read about over the years when learning of the outside world for fieldwork. She could envision herself staying here with Damien and exploring virtual worlds with him, all while pretending he was like he used to be. Fooling herself with a happy delusion.

She had to keep reminding herself that this wasn’t really him, even though in some way it was an extension of him. Something he built, continuing his reach from beyond the grave. And while it wasn’t Damien, it did expand Damien’s real life, because it meant that things he did while alive were still impacting things now. In addition to living on in her memories and actions, Damien lived on in this mimic’s memories and actions, too. Maybe—

“Can I just say, you two are adorable,” a woman said, standing about ten feet away. Sara and Damien both turned to look; it was the same woman who’d been reading a book earlier. “I’m sorry Colonel Rahal couldn’t make it tonight, but I’ve come on his behalf.”

It took Sara a moment, but that calm voice triggered something. The woman who had come up to help Asim after he broke into Aisha’s apartment—what had he called her? It started with a Z.

“How can I know you’re really who you say you are?” Sara asked. “And is Colonel Rahal the same as Asim?”

“Yes. Colonel Asim Rahal, for whom I’m a dutiful minion.” The woman bowed theatrically. “As for identity verification, I’d suggest we sort that out in a private setting. Since I’ve been watching you here, it means others could be too. Come, please. I’ve set up a shielded area.”







Chapter 35



Sara sat with Damien and this strange woman in her virtual private room, which according to her was a replica of her real-world apartment.

“Do you want tea?” the woman asked with a smile. Zhou, was it? Unlike how she appeared on the beach, she now looked like Sara remembered from when she and Asim arrested her. An Asian woman in maybe her late twenties, with dark hair pulled back into a bun.

“Yes, thank you,” Damien said. That must’ve been performative for those around him; he couldn’t actually taste or want anything.

“Sure,” Sara said.

Zhou manifested a cup of tea each for Sara and Damien, along with one for herself. “My device is an old, cheap model,” she said. “I can’t taste anything with this thing. But since this is my real apartment, I brewed some for myself in real life. Enjoy.”

Sara sighed and sipped her tea, and . . . wow.

“Heh. Good, huh?” Zhou said.

“What is it?” Sara asked.

“That flavor comes from cloves. My mother’s recipe,” Zhou said. “It’s even better in person. I think cloves pair so well with autumn, don’t you?”

Sara looked around. The apartment did feel rather autumn-themed, and cozy. Several painted Halloween pumpkins and flickering spiced-apple candles decorated the corners, adding warmth and fragrance. On the lamp table beside Zhou’s couch sat a framed illustration in colored pencil, seemingly drawn by a child, signed by someone named Scarlett.

“I guess so,” Sara said. Not knowing what else to add, she stared back at Zhou and took another sip.

“My name’s Ava,” she said, clearing her throat. “I understand your names are Sara and Damien, is that right?”

Sara nodded. “I thought you were called Zhou.”

“Ah, that’s what Colonel Rahal calls me in front of others. Special Agent Ava Zhou, at your service. Feel free to call me Ava.”

Sara thought for a moment. “What is this meeting even about?”

“That’s a great question. Maybe you can tell me? You were waiting for him on the beach, right?” Ava said with a smile, sipping her own tea.

“Can you even prove who you represent?” Sara asked.

“Tomorrow I can get Colonel Rahal to vouch for me. For now, I’ll answer questions as proof. For example, I walked in after the colonel fought you, and you were on the floor laughing and spitting up blood. I had to convince Jitters to let me arrest her without force, since she was worried about Scorpio. He’s an adorable Bengal cat who came out to check on you while you were still on the floor. Then, well, at Fort Hamilton things went wrong, and your creepy scientist took over. I barged in on you being interrogated, and one of the women was tearing at your shoulder wound telekinetically. I’m sorry it went like that. It wasn’t authorized by our group.”

Damien looked over at Sara.

“That all happened,” Sara said. “So it is you, then.”

“I’m Colonel Rahal’s minion. I’ve been on your case from the beginning.”

“So, you helped find me? Arrest me?” Sara asked.

Ava’s eyes widened. “Um, yes? Kind of.”

Sara sipped her tea, repressing a surge of anger.

Damien leaned forward and broke the silence. “May I ask, what are your goals, Ava?”

“I, um . . . you mean today, or like, in my life?”

“For this meeting, obviously,” Damien said. “You invited us here.”

“Well, my colleague notified me that GlitterJitters logged on,” Ava said. “Veronica’s still in jail, so we assumed it was Sara. She went to Colonel Rahal’s favorite spot, which is basically like trying to summon him.” She smirked and twirled her hand like a sorceress. “But he’s . . . off duty tonight, so I had to come on his behalf. I can relay messages to him, and speak for him a little.”

“He and I previously spoke at night, twice. What do you mean ‘off duty’?” Sara asked.

“Well,” Ava said again, her demeanor cooling down. “Most nights he still kind of works, but tonight he seems to be away entirely, which is rare. If you tell me what you wanted to tell him, I’ll make sure he gets it tomorrow.”

Sara stared down at her tea. “I don’t even know what I wanted to tell him. I don’t trust him anymore, but I didn’t know what else to do next, other than drastic measures.”

“Which drastic measures?” Ava asked.

“You ask questions like he does,” Sara said. “Always aiming for case intel. Obviously, I can’t tell you that.”

Ava eyed Sara for a moment, then smiled. “Let me try another approach, then. What would you like to tell Colonel Rahal and me, now that we’re here, having tea?”

“I . . . After everything, I wanted to hear what he had to say. To see if he had anything to offer after he ruined everything. Any reason at all to persuade me away from what I might otherwise have to do. I don’t have much time left.”

“What happened to you, it hit Colonel Rahal very hard,” Ava said. “In fact, I think that’s why he’s not available tonight. I can assure you that none of this went as he intended.”

Sara sighed. “That’s easy to say.”

“If there was a way for Colonel Rahal to help you, what would you ask?”

“To audit the Stolguard facility. Pause Class D trials immediately. Expose everything Stallard has done. Look into the assassinations. And let the subjects go.”

“And what would you do in the meantime?”

A frustrating question. Go after another yacht once this one ran out of supplies? Try Damien’s suggestion of eating only meat to test her powers? “Plan things,” she answered after a delay.

“Well, I generated a new identity key for this, so nobody knows who I am except you two. Here’s a passcode so you can come to this encrypted room and message me.”

“May I ask who Scarlett is? Do you have a daughter?” Damien asked, pointing to the framed illustration on the lamp table.

Sara looked at him flatly. He’d barely spoken, and that was what he chimed in with?

Ava smiled sadly and picked up the picture. “Scarlett’s parents were murdered a few years back. I was a police officer at the time, on the killer’s case, trying to stop him from doing it again. She’s with a foster family now, but we stay in touch, even visit sometimes.”

“Sounds like she’s in good hands,” Damien said.

Ava grinned as she put the picture back. “I do my best to make sure they spoil her.”

“I meant your hands,” Damien said. “My goal when escaping the facility was to find someone like you. Authorities who . . . cared. I knew it wouldn’t be easy, but there’s no other way than to start somewhere. By the sound of it, Colonel Rahal cares as well?”

“Probably too much for his own good,” Ava said. “That’s why I joined his team.”

“Then I must press the importance,” Damien said. “What Sara says is accurate. The Stolguard facility needs to be searched, and a boy there named Marcus must be separated from Thane Stallard, and soon. The implications of human-to-machine interfaces need to be studied, so that by the time this technology is rediscovered elsewhere, the world’s systems are prepared.”

Ava’s eyes went wide at Damien’s comment, and she took notes.

Upon finishing, she leaned forward. “I’ll talk to Colonel Rahal about these matters. But here’s an important question. Did Dr. Stallard mention anything about aliens at the facility?” She stared at Damien, then at Sara, her expression grave.

Sara shook her head.

Ava deflated, like air leaking out of a balloon. “I see.”

“I saw his files,” Damien said. “They did have some biological samples of extraterrestrial origin, from a long time ago. That accelerated their research, both for genetic patterns and brain structure design. That’s why it’ll take decades for this technology to re-emerge elsewhere. Other teams won’t have the head start that Thane had.”

“I knew it!” Ava shouted, clenching her fist in front of her.

Sara held her breath, ran that through her head again, then looked at Damien. “Wait, we’re part alien?”

He nodded. “Five decades ago, Thane was approached by DARPA during his PhD dissertation, since it was exactly the research they were interested in. He was given access to this top-secret material. According to his logs, he then became interested in genetic human specialization partially because the extraterrestrials this material came from seemed to be designed that way themselves, like a hive of sapient individuals. For early classes, he focused on getting the physical abilities working first to secure funding, figuring he’d refine the psychological side later, in future classes. Or when his plans for me came to fruition. The next phase would emphasize emotional and motivational optimization, like a form of mental domestication.”

“I knew it had to be something,” Ava said. “That’s why his tech seems so different than everyone else’s.”

“Exactly,” Damien said. “And that’s why it’s so devastating. Logically, by the time a civilization figures out Class D’s level of brain-computer integration on its own, the rest of their tech will also be resilient against its capabilities. But because Thane is working from alien material in secret, he’ll end up unleashing a genuine superintelligence at way too early a stage, rendering the gap between it and everything else too large for them to fight back against. It’ll win before they even know what hit them.”

“Why didn’t you tell me about the aliens from the start?” Sara asked. “About all this?”

Damien held up his palms. “I would have, in time. But we only had a week after I learned it. I needed you to focus on our escape, not be distracted by countless other revelations. I gave you the broad strokes of the threat, so you’d understand why we had to leave, and I planned to fill you in on the details later. Like I am now.”

Sara wanted to be angry, but she had no argument. Instead, she found herself staring at him for too long. No wonder he’d been so aloof that whole week. How much had been going through his head?

“Can you tell me how telekinesis works?” Ava asked, shifting her attention toward Sara. The agent was bubbly now, like a child.

Sara sighed. “It’s hard to explain to people who can’t see and feel the forces I do.”

“Try me, please.”

Sara sipped the spiced tea. Ava’s enthusiasm couldn’t help but remind her of Clare. “I’m a conduit,” she said eventually. “I transfer energy and momentum, conserving both, but make sure it transfers through my being rather than impacting my body directly.”

Ava nodded, taking notes as though she were in a classroom. She was certainly more likable than her boss.

“My, um, alien brain is modified to feel like I have four additional limbs. Two psionic arms, which we call foci, and two psionic legs, which we call bracers. They exist in another dimension of reality and are incorporeal unless I use them to impact matter at their tips, like hands and feet. And they aren’t literal limbs; mine can reach nearly a mile, can change shape, and can extend through matter. They’re invisible to you, but telekineticists can see each other’s foci and bracers as though they were ethereal, and their foci can interact, like blocking, redirecting, or grabbing each other’s attacks in a duel.”

Ava had sipped her tea while listening and was now trying to swallow the mouthful as quickly as possible, eager for the next question. “How do they cross into our dimension?”

“I suck out kinetic energy from air or liquid molecules, which turns them cold, and use the tips of my foci to transfer that kinetic energy as motion to other objects, or squeeze or twist them.”

“So, it does conserve energy,” Ava said.

Sara nodded. “And momentum. If I push a car with a focus, it pushes back on me just as hard, and since I’m lighter, I’d be flung away faster than the car. That’s why we have two bracers, or psionic legs. If I’m going to move a car, I grab it with, say, my right focus, and I plant my right bracer firmly onto the earth. The counterforce of moving the car transfers through my focus, through my bracer, and to the earth, which can absorb almost infinite momentum. I could brace on another object, like another car, but then it would shoot off in the opposite direction. Our bracers aren’t precise like our foci are; they only transfer the opposite momentum from our foci to whatever they’re braced against.”

Sara watched Ava’s eyes dart about as she tried to work through the description. Her curiosity had led her to talk about it more than she ever had before.

“But if you only have two foci, how do you lift so many cars at once?”

Sara paused. Ava was almost too inquisitive. But the goal from the start was to expose all of Stallard’s secrets, wasn’t it? “Each focus has a flexible shape and can split into psionic fingers,” she said. “I can pick up a dozen cars with one focus, because I can split that mental hand into a dozen fingers and grab a car with each one. But I have to apply the same force, in terms of speed and direction, to all cars held by that focus. Or I can widen the tip of my focus, imbue it with a ton of energy, and use it as a shield or a push, and block or blast through all objects across an area without grabbing each one individually. But that’s far less energy efficient. Only a couple of us are powerful enough to do that effectively. Me and one other.”

“But if you draw kinetic energy from the environment rather than yourself, what’s your limitation, then? Can you move anything?”

“Now you sound like Asim. Asking detective questions. Probing my weaknesses?”

“I didn’t mean—”

“Think of it like weightlifting,” Sara interrupted, forcing a smile. She pictured Bronn lifting all that iron. “We train over time to transfer more energy. The strain we feel as a conduit hits us with headaches and disorientation. Stallard says it causes micro-damage within our brains that heals over time to form denser connections. Talent matters too. Some improve a lot faster than others.”

“And you’re the best at it?”

“Only for power,” Sara said. “Some others are more precise. But since heavy things feel lighter to me, I can be precise with things that others would find hard to manage, like cars.” Thinking about it more, Stallard pushing her nearly to passing out each week—nearly to dying, perhaps—was likely what had built up so much latent power. Then there were several more years of her pushing herself. And once this fasting, or this ketosis, released it, the power came out as if a dam had broken.

“I appreciate the detail,” Ava said. “I don’t mean to use it against you.”

“Easy to say.” Sara rose from the couch. “I need to make sure my surroundings are safe, and I have tests to try. You two can stay and talk if you want. Maybe he’ll show you the visions.” She hated how robotic she sounded, but while she liked Ava, she didn’t really trust her, given how badly trusting Asim had gone. Damien as a mimic threw her off too. She needed space to think. For now, they were her only lifelines.

“Well, you have the passcode to come back,” Ava said. “I hope you will.”

Sara looked at Damien, and he met her eyes with a gentle smile. She couldn’t help but do the same.

With that, she logged off and checked her surroundings. It was dark now, and the family wasn’t around, which hopefully meant they were still locked in the cabin bedroom. She stood up, knees sore. Feeling lightheaded, she went to the barricaded door to listen and confirm that there were sounds coming from it.

“You’re doing fine,” she shouted through the barrier. “Keep this up and you’ll live.”

“There’s not much food. How long will you keep us here?” It was the mother’s exasperated voice.

“Not long enough for it to become a problem. Give the rest to the boy.”

Sara sorted through the food supplies she’d gathered, minus the portion she had given the family to rely on in their makeshift bedroom jail. Most of it was carbs, but there were a few meat sticks. She checked their ingredients, and thankfully they weren’t the sugared kind, almost all protein and fat. She took a deep breath, then ripped open the plastic around one stick and took a bite. Then another, and another, until she ate the whole thing, her body practically singing at the sensation.

She gripped her body with multiple points of her right focus, used her corresponding bracer against the sea floor sixty feet down, and lifted. And . . . despite eating a full meat stick, she could still do it effortlessly. She hovered a few feet above the yacht deck, and no headache came. From her prior tests, if she’d eaten the same amount of rice, she’d have been nearly powerless for hours.

If Damien proved right, and a ketogenic diet let her keep her amplified power indefinitely, then everything had changed. It meant she could try to be more deliberate, more cerebral, in her approach against Stallard.

Maybe so many people didn’t need to die, after all.







Chapter 36



Asim heard a muffled voice. He tried to ignore it, but it grew louder, closer.

“Again with this!” he thought she said. Or something like that. Certainly angry.

“What?” Asim asked. It was hard to even open his eyes.

“You were supposed to take Yasmin to practice half an hour ago.”

He forced himself to crack his eyes open. Janine loomed over him, and Yasmin stood in the bedroom doorway in her soccer uniform, staring down at her sneakers. He glanced at the clock, but his head spun. A faint mechanical buzzing came from somewhere. “No, there’s still time. It’s only—”

“How many did you have last night?” Janine shook her head. “I can’t believe you let her see you like this again.”

Fuck. Right. Saturday morning. Janine had to go into the office for a big client, and it was his turn to take Yasmin. He’d only meant to have a couple last night, but then . . . shit. “Look, Yas. I didn’t mean . . .” What was there to say? His head was still spinning. He squeezed his eyes closed and tried to orient himself. Get yourself together, dumbass.

When he opened his eyes again, feeling steadier, Yasmin focused directly on him. There wasn’t anger there, just disappointment. The edges around her eyes were pink. His precious fourteen-year-old had been crying. He clenched his teeth. How had he fucked this up again? And what was that incessant buzzing, growing louder?

Yasmin looked up at her mother. “Can we still—”

A drone struck the girl from above with a loud pop. Her head snapped to the side, and blood sprayed from her temple all over the wall.

“No!” Asim screamed. He lunged from the bed, catching her limp body before it hit the floor. The buzzing was unbearable. Shrill, whirling blades from overhead. Another pop—

Asim jolted awake, heart hammering, head splitting in pain.

Only a nightmare.

He took a minute to let relief wash over him, and for his pulse to slow down. Family drama and war. That was always a fun mix. He pushed it aside and reoriented himself around the present. Observe. Orient. Decide. Act.

He was hungover. Badly. The stupid thing about drinking too much was that you’d forget half the fun you had. And he’d had a lot of fun with Melody last night—at least from the part he remembered. A man should know his limitations.

He reached for his phone to check the time. Almost noon. Jesus Christ. There were eight unread notifications from Ava, and one from Brooks. His headache grew worse just from thinking about reading through them, so he set it back down and got up to make himself a cappuccino. He needed caffeine and aspirin before he could deal with anything of substance.

Not that it really mattered. It wasn’t like any of his actions lately had helped anyway. The past month of work his division had done on this case only made things worse. If he’d done nothing more than hang out on the fake fucking beach drunk this whole time, the world wouldn’t have been any worse off for it.

His eyes drifted over to Yasmin’s photos on his shelf, lingering on the one of her holding the damn soccer ball when she was fourteen, just like in the nightmare. He sighed. It was days like this that had ruined things with her. Even in the sweet pictures of her younger self, he could feel her judging him. She still hadn’t replied to his message from days ago, either. Not that he could really blame her.

His phone buzzed again, so he went over to look. Sir, are you okay? Don’t make me come knocking! Ava’s latest message read.

Couldn’t a man have a single day off?

He shook his head and opened the rest of the messages. If people were worrying about him, he had to force himself to check what they were saying. Ava never bothered him unnecessarily.

The first one was from Brooks last evening, saying GlitterJitters had come back online. Shit. The rest were from Ava asking if he would go in to talk with Sara, saying that Sara was at his beach spot, that Ava was going in herself, that she really needed to talk alone, and then asking him if he was okay.

A brief wave of excitement hit him. Sara had already reestablished contact. That would give them more to work with than he’d thought. But that wave vanished as soon as he remembered that it didn’t really matter anyway, since this was all above him, and no matter what he did it just made things worse.

I’m fine, he texted back to Ava. I took the day off given how the value of our work has been fuck-all lately. Meet for sushi at one?

He wasn’t hungry, but the restaurant had private booths, which would make for a secure enough spot to talk. And miso soup might help with his hangover, since it was salty and would restore electrolytes and shit.

She replied with three hearts, a thumbs-up, and a salute emoji.

Asim lay down on the couch and switched on the NewsCon stream. Atlas Rionz was giving a fireside interview. Great.

“You’ve been saying since—”

“Four years, Ivy. Ever since that one hack made everyone irrational.” The man had on the same white sweater as always. He must’ve had a closet filled with them in every one of his trillionaire compounds. “Look at this mess; the fact that they lost her again is inexcusable. The government hobbled itself by giving up some of its surveillance capabilities all because of one data leak. They’d have caught her even before your tragic newsroom attack if they weren’t holding themselves back.”

“But there are those who would—”

“I know what they’d argue, Ivy. Their little libertarian dreams.” Rionz gestured dismissively with his hand. “But we live in too dangerous a world for that now. That degree of privacy can’t be allowed as though we were still in the musket era. With the types of bombs and bioweapons people have access to now—or whatever the hell this Russian asset is using—it’s unrealistic not to give up some privacy for the sake of security. It’s time to give these people a firm ‘no.’ Time to be blunt so we can prevent these types of threats before they metastasize. It’s what every sane person wants.”

The interviewer opened her mouth but remained silent, and Rionz leaned back in his chair. “I didn’t mean to interrupt. Go ahead Ivy,” he added with a softer tone.

She nodded. “It’s fine, Mr. Rionz. To confirm, you agree with the White House’s statement that Jade is Russian? Until this morning they downplayed the scale of the attacks, only now blaming Russia after all the recent sightings over Brooklyn and Queens.”

“Yes. I can confirm it’s true, though of course I can’t go into full detail. She’s a Russian asset, regardless of Moscow’s denials. The only reason it hasn’t turned into war is because she’s believed to have gone rogue. The White House has known her origin for some time, but they were slow to blame Russia, for diplomatic reasons that I’m sure you can appreciate. But now they have little choice.”

The interviewer, Ivy, glanced toward the camera. “You heard it here, first. Atlas Rionz backs the Press Secretary’s latest statements.” She turned back toward him. “Speaking of which, some critics say you have a conflict of interest over this, and that you’re aiming for more defense contracts for the type of surveillance you advocate. How would you respond to them?”

Rionz furrowed his brow. “By telling them to try a serious argument.” Then his expression eased. “They fall back on weak character attacks like that because they can’t respond to my actual points. I don’t argue for more defense because I’m in the defense industry; I’m in the defense industry because I realize we need more defense. The polls are clear, and the President should use an executive order to weaken those recent blockages as much as legally possible. Hook everything back up and stop letting theater kids run our national security. Rionz Corp is already assisting free of charge until she’s caught. My priority is public safety, not money. If I was—”

“Off,” Asim said, and the stream muted. Fuck that guy. Rionz knew the government still had backdoors in most surveillance systems. It was his company’s equipment they used, after all. He probably knew they were being aggressively applied to this case, too, given that he was helping the Pentagon repeat their latest talking points. He treated every incident like an opportunity for his anti-privacy crusade. “Guy probably read Nineteen Eighty-Four as aspirational,” Asim said aloud to nobody, closing his eyes to ease the pounding in his skull.

A buzzing filled the room some minutes later. He cracked his eyes back open to look, but it was just his cleaning bot roaming around. Not a combat drone, of course, with its shrill whirling.

After watching it for a moment, he closed his eyes again. But now he was thinking about that hack all over again, from four years ago.

It had leaked audiovisual data from hundreds of millions of house bots. The government had secretly collected it into a giant honeypot and then . . . oops. Most of those data bridges were more secure now, but Asim still never liked to walk around naked near the damn things. There were probably videos of his dick online; he’d resisted the urge to search and see the bad news for sure, but they were almost certainly there. An ocean of everyone’s dicks.

But worse than leaked bodies were leaked conversations—things not meant to be shared. Personal failures immortalized and searchable, forever.



“Konnichiwa! Welcome to Koi Sushi,” the young waitress said, too loudly. Asim winced and pinched the bridge of his nose.

“Hello,” Ava said. “Thank you. Don’t mind him. I’ll take the tuna special, and he’ll have a cup of miso soup. Plus, two green teas and an order of gyoza for the table, please.”

The waitress bowed, collected the menus, and left. Ava shook her head disapprovingly once she was gone.

“What?” Asim asked.

“I don’t like those fake greetings,” Ava said. “They feel weird, you know?”

“That’s just their word for ‘hello.’”

“She’s Chinese, not Japanese, sir.”

“Oh. Wait. So?”

“A lot of sushi places are staffed by Chinese people, actually,” she said. “Japan doesn’t really have a big enough diaspora to run sushi places everywhere. It doesn’t mean they need to pretend to speak Japanese when they almost certainly don’t, though. This place does that for some reason.”

Asim gave a polite smile as the waitress brought them their teas and gyoza. He had kind of assumed most of the servers here at a sushi place were Japanese. For years now. Huh. He sipped his tea to distract himself from feeling awkward that he hadn’t noticed. He knew Ava was Chinese.

“Okay, fill me in on last night. Why the need to talk alone? You left your tablet in your car, right?” Asim asked.

“Of course, sir. I don’t know who to trust anymore, so I can only tell you. I try to put myself in Sara’s shoes and see if I would do anything differently.”

“Well for starters, you wouldn’t commit mass murder, I’m pretty sure,” Asim said.

“Not as me, no, but then if I were in her shoes, I wouldn’t be me, is my point.”

Asim rubbed his temple. “Okay.”

“That unarmored body at the rest stop, Damien, was Sara’s lover, sir. It turns out he created a mimic of himself, so he still exists in some form, verified by Sara. He found her last night, and the three of us spoke.”

Asim raised his eyebrows. “Hard to picture that woman loving anything.”

“You’d be surprised, sir. Damien brought out a different side of her.” Ava smiled.

“So, it turns out when she’s not murdering people, she’s more fun to hang out with?” He didn’t bother to hide his sarcasm.

She crossed her arms. “Well, to be clear, sir, I’m not excusing her actions. But the situation is ethically complex, as you’re aware.”

“Sorry. I’ll stop interjecting. I get it; you think she’s hot. You always do pick out the tomboys. Tell me the situation.”

“Sir, that’s not—ugh! Damien’s mimic confirmed he shares Sara’s fears about a dangerous entity in the facility. And not even an ‘entity’ really, but more like a fusion. Certain subjects in the facility can be hooked up to the facility’s datacenter and become a true superintelligence. That’s the third class you asked General Rodriguez about.” Her face was red now.

“A superintelligence sounds like a good thing,” Asim said.

“Apparently it overshadows the subjects’ humanity and empathy. Damien fears it could be catastrophic, especially if it emerges suddenly, before the world has any preparation. You know, when the capability gap is so asymmetrical. Dr. Stallard’s got the next subject almost ready. It’s not just Sara being delusional, sir.”

“Maybe they’re both delusional. Can’t Stallard or other scientists pause their operations if things get out of hand?” Asim asked. “Remember how many people were worried about AI decades ago?”

“This one’s conscious, sir. Supposedly, it can be incredibly destructive right from the start. Like hacking into weapon systems, power grids, and digital infrastructure. It combines human consciousness with a datacenter’s worth of processing capability. Damien says that if he hadn’t escaped the facility, by now that plugged-in version of him would already be wreaking havoc. Wielding all our drone swarms by the millions and things like that.”

“It’d start a war?” Asim asked.

“Damien doesn’t think it’ll be a war if it’s unleashed soon. More like a domination. Then it’ll put the pieces back together into its own hive of bio-engineered space fascists. Or that’s what the visions looked like.” She paused. “I think we should be whistleblowers, sir, and help them bring attention to the situation.”

He shrugged. “Based on what evidence? We have nothing other than a claim. You said that yourself the other day.”

“I’ve thought about framing this, and I think we can lead with both the ethical angle and the corruption angle, which we have more evidence for, and we can add this as an extra note to further amplify it. Damien told me a ton about Dr. Stallard’s goals, and says it’s recorded in the facility’s logs. We’ve got enough to push hard. He raises children from pods without rights, making super-soldiers for illegal hits. This is explosive, sir.”

“You’re saying we highlight the inhumane conditions and unsanctioned missions,” Asim said, turning it over in his head. “Argue the facility should be investigated. And we’ll add that also, this potential superintelligence threat needs to be analyzed for safety reasons. That way we can bring it up too, without sounding fucking insane.”

“Yes. I think so, sir. If we don’t blow the whistle, nobody will. I’m not sure how you’ve managed it, but part of Sara still wants to trust you.”

“Well, it’d be easier to gather sympathy for her cause if she hadn’t killed so many people.”

“But it’s the scale, sir. The next classes are bigger. Other subjects will keep killing civilians down the line, with near impunity, despite many of them not even wanting to. And then there’s the potential apocalypse. Those movies are never fun.” She plucked a piece of gyoza with her chopsticks and plopped it into her mouth as she stared at him, chewing.

Asim crossed his arms. “I don’t disagree with that. The list of assassinations Sara gave me is explosive if true. But you do realize that whistleblowers get their careers fucked up, right? Superiors and colleagues would turn on us. Official whistleblower actions go to the top in private and then basically vanish. If we go public with it, we risk prosecution for treason. So, if we take that swing, we can’t fucking miss.”

“I feel like I have to try, sir.”

He sighed. “Look. I understand the sentiment. But we’re two very small cogs in this whole thing. It’s unlikely to amount to much.”

“I think the brave colonel who grabbed his laser rifle and ran off to stop an invincible witch would disagree, sir.”

“Clever. But that was physical, and with a clear reason. This is legal, bureaucratic, and vague. It’s mostly out of our hands. Government in bed with industry, with every conflict of interest imaginable. And magical assassins now, because why not.”

The waitress brought them their soup and tuna, asked if they needed anything else, and left. Ava kept looking at him. That annoying, self-assured expression. He remembered feeling the same confidence at her age. The sense that you know the right path, that things are black and white. But as he went through shit for years, he realized he had no idea what he was doing and came back to a broken family, cracked by his absence and then shattered by all the baggage he brought home. Ava hadn’t been beaten down by life’s relentless insistence on being morally gray, ambiguous, and unknowable yet.

“You think I don’t care?” Asim asked. “When I was younger than you, I swore an oath to defend the Constitution against all enemies, foreign and domestic. And the joke was, ever since then, the most dangerous enemies I ran into ended up being fucking domestic.” He leaned in. “Yet, they hardly ever get held accountable. Bad guys win. The end.”

Ava raised her eyebrows, still with that stupid, confident face. Practically his own young face mirrored back at him. Seconds ticked by in silence.

“Fine,” Asim said. “I’ll put in a formal whistleblower complaint to the Inspector General with all the detail I have so far. But I can almost assure you that they won’t take any action on it, since we’re politically outgunned. And if we go public with it after that, like I said, we risk being prosecuted for treason.”

“We have to start somewhere, so I agree with that initial approach, sir.”

Asim lowered his voice. “Even the internal whistleblower approach is dangerous in this case, since we have evidence of Stolguard assassins taking out other whistleblowers. If we do this, we’ll have to watch our backs at all times. You ready for that?”

She smiled. “That’s why I joined you, sir.”

He leaned back, eyeing her. “Alright. I want to speak with Sara again. If we’re going to put ourselves at risk, we need her to lie low and stop killing. She has to actually listen to me for a change.” Saying it out loud made it sound so hollow, though. He barely trusted himself anymore, so why would Sara? And she was on some sort of clock, wasting away. He pinched the bridge of his nose at the complexity of this. He still had a headache from all the booze.

“I have a private room set up that she can access. I’ll try messaging her, sir.”

“I’m going to generate a new identity key,” Asim said. “Observers can’t track what our avatars say, but they can track if our avatars keep appearing in the same spaces as hers. So, we should use throwaway identifiers from now on. We don’t need the reputation scores of our main identities to access private rooms anyway.”

“That’s what I used last night, sir. A new identity key. I plan to use that one only for this context.”

“Good.”

“And there’s one more thing, sir.”

“Yes?”

“They are aliens! Or, well, part alien. Alien-mutants. Damien’s mimic confirmed it.”







Chapter 37
