That would ideally allow a more gradual leakage of the threat, and there’d be more defenses ready if it ever came to fruition again.

Authorities never found evidence of Asim and Ava’s collaboration with Sara or with Damien’s mimic. They had covered their tracks well. His prospects for promotion had likely taken a hit, but that was fine with him. He was at peace with what he’d done, and with what he was still doing.

“You haven’t been here in a while,” said a voice from behind. Sara.

He turned to look. She stood there, in her true form with blond hair, using a new identity key. She was no longer gaunt, and her facial scars were noticeably thinner. Almost gone. She had other faint scars on her body, including on her right shoulder and left side, but her fingernails had regrown.

“What’s this?” he asked. “A mimic? At least you don’t look like shit anymore.” He did his best to hide his unease.

“Try again,” she said, then came and sat beside him in a black bikini and a loose, colorful beach cloak that flapped in the wind.

Asim opened his information screen. A visual verifier flag claimed that her device was showing the user’s real scanned body, with no alterations. A mimic couldn’t generally fake that. It was the mode people used when trying to prove that they were who they claimed to be. Only a minority of people used it, either because most people liked their little artificial touch-ups or because they wanted to be anonymous. Technically, it was possible to fake the verifier flag by hacking into a VR device’s hardware, but by design that was very difficult to do.

“Wait, so . . .” he started to say, but the sentence felt too stupid to finish.

“Yeah.” She smiled, and this time it reached her eyes.

“How?”

She shrugged. “Who can say? Anyway, I’d much rather be speaking with Ava right now. But she hasn’t used her identity key in months, so I wasn’t sure how to reach her. Can you tell her I said goodbye, and thanks for all the tea?”

Asim asked her a few verifier questions, which she passed, then sipped his water as he tried to wrap his head around this. “Sure. That was a throwaway identity key she used with you, for privacy. If you plan to stick with the one you’re using now, I’ll let Ava know so she can message you on it.”

“Thank you,” she said. “I’m at least glad you didn’t end up in prison. Damien was worried that you might. I wondered if I’d have to come back and bust you out or something.”

“Fortunately not. I just got chewed out a lot. And I’ve had a ton of extra paperwork to deal with, thanks to you two.” He cracked a smile.

“Anything interesting?” she asked.

“The domestic assassinations really struck a nerve. Several whistleblowers and corporate tycoons were taken out by senior Class C members during the past decade, and there’s documentation. Even a few politicians and judges. The sheer scale of it has caused quite a stir. Some other agencies keep trying to bury it, but Damien threatening to release the data whenever things slow down gives me more leverage to push forward internally. The Class D computer hookups and Singularity shit are all beyond my understanding, but the other stuff there was, well, powerful. Thank you.”

“Damien says that the facility’s being reformed,” she said. “Is that what you see from your side?”

“It’s true. Doing my best on that. Others as well. Bronn and Clare gave great closed-door Congressional testimonies about various ethical breaches. Jessica, too. Meanwhile, Fallon testified in defense of the facility. What a piece of work she is. Protective of your memory, though. Whenever one of her questioners referred to you as C17, she’d insist they call you Sara instead.” He sipped his water, watching her hold back a smile.

“And Class D?” she asked.

“I think the best-case scenario is that the tech will get more gradually distributed as it’s studied and rediscovered, rather than hitting the unprepared world all at once, like it would have under Stallard if you and Damien hadn’t caused all this mess. Certain cryptographic vulnerabilities are being patched and responsibly shared. Damien’s comparison to European diseases and guns hitting the New World feels apt.” He shrugged. “But then again, I don’t really know shit.”

Sara nodded, then turned toward the sea. “Why haven’t you been coming here?”

He thought for a moment. “I guess I don’t need to,” he said eventually, and took another sip of water. “My work is fulfilling for a change, and I got my daughter to talk to me again, at least to some extent. I’m trying to break out of repetition, you know? To live a little.”

“What is life, to you?” she asked, turning back to look at him.

“Hell if I know. Not being dead, I guess. Nor wanting to be.”

“That’s my favorite answer yet, I think. It’s simple.” Her eyes lit up as she seemed to contemplate it. “Anyway, I’ve got to go. Can’t risk being seen in this mode. I’ll keep this identity key, though. Ciao!”

“Bye,” he said, and she vanished.

Well, then.



Sara took off her headset and stood, gazing out over the sea, catching the last rays of sunset over the water. A real sea, with no distractions. Down south, out of the country, in a simpler place with fewer cameras.

Only Clare knew where she was, and she had promised to visit once it became possible. After this meeting, Asim and Ava would also have a way to contact her if needed.

Damien’s mimic was still out there somewhere, but he had convinced her that it was best that he distance himself to make it easier for her to move on. At least they’d been able to say their goodbyes.

As she thought about how much she missed the real Damien, a breeze ruffled her hair and she took a deep breath. She wished she could tell him that he succeeded in the end. That the things he set in motion mattered.

She would have to look over her shoulder for the rest of her life, and she was more alone than she preferred, but overall, things had turned out better than she could’ve hoped. Certainly better than she deserved. She still sat up at night with dark thoughts sometimes, either about the lives she’d taken or about what she might have done differently, but not as often as before.

It was time to rebuild. Time to figure out what life meant, at least to her. She couldn’t take back what she had done, for better or worse, but she could move forward.

As Clare liked to say, one step at a time.

About the Author





Lyn Alden is an investor and author who focuses on the intersection of finance and technology.

She holds a bachelor’s degree in electrical engineering and a master’s in engineering management, and spent over a decade designing and building aircraft simulators at the FAA’s Cockpit Simulation Facility.

Today, Lyn runs an investment research business at LynAlden.com and serves as a general partner at the venture firm Ego Death Capital. Her 2023 bestselling nonfiction book, Broken Money, explores how ever-changing technology shapes the nature of money and power over time.
